The Ruling on Deficient Intercourse and Seminal Emission in Fasting
Listing rulings for seminal emission from deficient intercourse and insertion nullifiers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between complete and deficient intercourse in Hanafi fiqh and state that deficient intercourse requires qada' but not kaffara in fasting.

Core Idea
Deficient intercourse requires makeup but no expiation; kaffara is only for full intercourse in Ramadan.

The passage lists various actions that cause seminal emission or insertion during fasting. The key distinction is between complete and deficient intercourse: only the former triggers the heavy penalty of kaffara, while the latter only requires qada' (makeup). This lesson unpacks that distinction and its rationale.

Guided Path
1
Categorize the acts of seminal emission
Identify which acts are classified as deficient intercourse (jimā‘ qāṣir): intercourse with a corpse or animal, thighing, lining, kiss, touch, and masturbation. Contrast with full intercourse (complete penetration and emission).
The passage explicitly lists these under 'or he emits from...'
2
State the ruling for deficient acts
The fast is invalidated. The consequence is that qada' (makeup) is wajib, but kaffara (expiation) is not. The proof is analogical: kaffara is only for full violation of Ramadan's sanctity.
This is the mufta bihi position from Zahir al-Riwayah.
3
Analyze the dalil (proof)
The 'illa (effective cause) for kaffara is the complete and deliberate violation of the sanctity of Ramadan through full intercourse. Deficient acts lack this 'illa, hence only qada' is required. This is a rational (aqli) analogue.
The passage says 'because it is deficient intercourse' and 'because expiation is for violating the sanctity of Ramadan'.
4
Apply the ruling to inserted substances
Insertion of water, finger, or cotton into the vagina or anus nullifies the fast only if the substance reaches the interior (amount of an enema). If not, the fast remains valid.
This is based on the principle of complete entry.
5
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference)
If one performs a deficient act, they only need to make up one day. If full intercourse occurs, they must make up and also perform kaffara (e.g., fasting two months or feeding sixty poor). Other schools may require kaffara for deficient acts, so the difference is significant.
The Hanafi position is relied upon for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو أنزلَ بوَطْءِ مَيْتَةٍ أو بَهِيمَةٍ، أو بتَفْخِيذ، أو بتبطين أوْ قُبْلَةٍ أَوْ لَمْسٍ أَوْ أَفْسَدَ صومَ غير أداءِ رمضان، أو وطِئَتْ وهي نائمةٌ، أَوْ أَقَطَرَتْ في فَرْجِهَا، على الأصح أو أدخل إصبعه، مبلولة بماء أو دُهْنِ، في دُبُرِهِ، أو أدخلَتْهُ في فَرْجِها الداخل، في المختار، أو أدخلَ قُطْنَة في دبره وغيبها، أو في فرجها الداخل ......... الغروب أو لم يتبين له شيء؛ لأن غلبة الظن كاليقين والأصل بقاء النهار. (أو أنزلَ بوَطْءِ مَيْتَةٍ أَوْ بَهِيمَةٍ)، لأنه جماع قاصر فلا يوجب الكفارة ويوجب القضاء، كما في « التجنيس» وغيره، (أو) أنزل ( بتَفْخِيذ أو أنزل بتبطين)، أو استمناء بالكف (أو) أنزل ( مِن قُبْلَةٍ أَوْ لَمْس) لا كفارة عليه؛ لقصور الجناية وعليه القضاء لوجود معنى الجماع، ولو قبلت زوجها فأمنت فسد الصوم، وإن أمــذى أو أمذت لا يفسد كما في ( الظهيرية » و « التجنيس» . ( أوْ أَفْسَدَ صومَ غير أداء رمضان) بجمـاع أو غيره؛ لأن الكفارة وردت في هتك حرمة شهر رمضان، إذ لا يجوز إخلاؤه عن الصوم بلا ضرورة بخلاف غيره ( أوْ وُطِئَتْ وهي نائمةٌ) لعدم الفعل منها وفسد صومها، فعليها القضاء دون الكفارة، وكذا لو وطأ التي جُنَّت بالنهار وقد نوت الصوم قبل الجنون ليلاً؛ لعدم الجناية منها، والجنــون الطارئ لا ينافي الصحة حتى إذا لم يوجد معه ما يفسد، ثم أفاقت لا تقضي اليــوم الـذي جنـت فـيـه. ( أوْ أَقَطَرَتْ في فَرْجِهَا، على الأصح لشبهه بالحقنة، كما في « التجنيس» و«الفتح» (أو أدخل إصبعه، مبلولةٌ بماءٍ أوْ دُهْنِ، في دُبُرِهِ) أو استنجى فوصل الماء إلى داخـــل دبــره أو فـرجـهـا الـداخـل؛ للمبالغة فيه والحد الذي يتعلق بالوصول إليه الفساد قدر المحقنة، قال في « الخلاصة : وقـل مـا يكون ذلك، لو خرج . ) فغسله إن نشفه قبل أن يقوم ويرجع لمحله لا يفسد صومه؛ لأن الماء اتصل بظاهر ثم زال قبل أن يصل إلى الباطن بعود المقعدة، وإلا فسد، كذا في «الفتح»، (أو أدخلَتْهُ) أي: إصبعها مبلولة بماء، أو دهن (في فَرْجِها الداخل، في المختار) ولمـا ذكـرنـا (أو أدخل قُطْنَة) [ أو خـرقـة ] أو خشبة أو حجراً ( في دبره وغيبها، أو أدخلته (في فرجها الداخـــل)، [ وغيبتها ] (٣) أي: القطنة ونحوها؛ لأنه تم الدخول كما في «التجنيس » ، بخلاف ما إذا كــان طـرف الخشبة أو الخرقة بيده وطرف الحشوة في الفرج الخارج، و[ الماء ] لم يصل إلى [ كثير ] داخل فإنه لا يفسد كما في « التبيين»، و «الفتح»، لأن عدم تمام الدخول كعدم دخول شيء بالمرة،
Or he emits semen from intercourse with a corpse or an animal, or from thighing, or from lining the thigh, or from a kiss or touch, or he spoils a fast other than performing Ramadan, or she was sleeping and was penetrated, or she inserted water into her vagina, on the soundest view, or he inserted his finger moistened with water or oil into his anus, or she inserted it into her inner vagina, in the chosen view, or he inserted a cotton ball into his anus and concealed it, or into her inner vagina ... sunset or nothing becomes clear to him; because overwhelming probability is like certainty and the original state is the continuation of the day. (Or he emits semen from intercourse with a corpse or an animal) because it is deficient intercourse so it does not necessitate expiation but necessitates makeup, as in 'al-Tajnis' and others. (Or) he emits (from thighing or from lining the thigh), or masturbation with the hand (or) he emits (from a kiss or touch): no expiation upon him due to the deficiency of the offense, and upon him is makeup due to the existence of the meaning of intercourse. And if his wife kissed him and he had a seminal emission, the fast is invalidated, but if he or she had pre-ejaculate, it does not invalidate as in 'al-Zhahiriyya' and 'al-Tajnis'. (Or he spoils a fast other than performing Ramadan) by intercourse or otherwise; because expiation is prescribed for violating the sanctity of the month of Ramadan, as it is not permissible to leave it without necessity unlike others. (Or she was sleeping and was penetrated) due to the absence of action from her, and her fast is invalidated, so upon her is makeup without expiation. Similarly, if a woman who became insane during the day was penetrated, and she had intended the fast before insanity at night, due to the absence of offense from her, and intervening insanity does not negate validity, so if no invalidator occurs with it, then she regains consciousness, she does not make up that day in which she became insane. (Or she inserted water into her vagina, on the soundest view, due to its resemblance to an enema, as in 'al-Tajnis' and 'al-Fath'. Or he inserted his finger moistened with water or oil into his anus) or he performed istinja and water reached inside his anus or her inner vagina; due to exaggeration in it, and the limit that relates to reaching which causes corruption is the amount of an enema. He said in 'al-Khulasa': and rarely does that happen. If he excreted and then washed it and dried it before standing up and it returns to its place, his fast is not invalidated; because water contacted the exterior then disappeared before reaching the interior by the return of the rectum, otherwise it is invalidated. Thus in 'al-Fath'. (Or she inserted it) i.e. her finger moistened with water or oil (into her inner vagina, in the chosen view). And what we mentioned (or he inserted a cotton ball) [or a rag] or a stick or a stone (into his anus and concealed it, or she inserted it (into her inner vagina) [and concealed it] i.e. the cotton ball and the like; because entry is complete as in 'al-Tajnis', unlike if the end of the stick or rag is in his hand and the end of the filling is in the outer vagina, and [water] did not reach [much] of the inside, then it does not invalidate as in 'al-Tabyin' and 'al-Fath', because incomplete entry is like no entry at all.
Source pages p. 658

Commentary
The passage is a ruling with explanation listing various nullifiers of fasting that involve emission or insertion. The text begins by stating that seminal emission from intercourse with a corpse or an animal, or from thighing, lining, kiss, or touch, does not require expiation but does require makeup, because these acts are deficient in the sense of full intercourse. The commentary explains that expiation is specific to violating the sanctity of Ramadan, so spoiling a voluntary fast or a makeup fast does not entail expiation. If a sleeping woman is penetrated, she must make up the fast but no expiation, as she had no intention. Insertion of water or a moist finger into the vagina or anus is also a nullifier if water reaches the interior. The limit is the amount of an enema. If one cleanses and dries before the rectum returns, the fast is not broken. The commentary cites multiple authoritative sources like al-Tajnis, al-Fath, al-Tabyin, and al-Khulasa.

Key Terms
جماع قاصر (jimā‘ qāṣir): Deficient intercourse, such as with a corpse or animal, or through thighing, kissing, etc., which does not require expiation.
كفارة (kaffāra): Expiation required for intentionally breaking the fast of Ramadan with full intercourse, typically freeing a slave, fasting two months, or feeding sixty poor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on one who has seminal emission from masturbation during a voluntary (nafl) fast?
A) The fast remains valid
B) The fast is invalid, requiring both qada' and kaffara
C) The fast is invalid, requiring qada' but no kaffara
D) The fast is invalid, requiring kaffara but no qada'

Key Takeaway
Deficient intercourse (jimā‘ qāṣir) nullifies the fast and requires qada' but not kaffara; kaffara is exclusively for full intercourse in Ramadan.

Sawm — Fasting
intermediate
8 min
Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Vomiting Threshold in Fasting
Ruling on intentional vomiting and intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf about amount.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Zahir al-Riwayah and Abu Yusuf positions on intentional vomiting less than a mouthful and identify which position is mufta bihi in the later Hanafi tradition.

Core Idea
For intentional vomiting, the relied-upon Hanafi view requires a mouthful to break the fast; less than that is legally negligible.

This passage from Imdad al-Fattah addresses a classic intra-Hanafi dispute: does intentionally vomiting a small amount (less than a mouthful) invalidate the fast? The Zahir al-Riwayah says yes, but Abu Yusuf restricts it to a mouthful, and the commentator declares his view 'sahih' (correct). Understanding this disagreement sharpens your ability to weigh different narrations within the school and apply the principle of tarjih.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is intentionally inducing vomiting (istiqā') during a Ramadan fast. Determine the initial hukm: intentional vomiting is prohibited and, if done, requires qada'.
The passage also covers involuntary vomiting and returning vomit, but focus on the intentional case.
2
State the Zahir al-Riwayah position
According to the apparent narration (Zahir al-Riwayah), any amount of intentional vomiting breaks the fast, even less than a mouthful. The proof is the general hadith: 'Whoever vomits intentionally, let him make up.'
This is the default position transmitted in the core six books of Imam Muhammad.
3
State Abu Yusuf's position and his dalil
Abu Yusuf stipulates a mouthful (mil' al-fam). His evidence: less than a mouthful is legally like nothing (ka al-'adam), as it does not even break wudu. Therefore, it cannot break the fast.
This is an example of qiyas with wudu: what is negligible in purity is negligible in fasting.
4
Analyze the tarjih in the text
The commentator says 'wa huwa al-sahih' (and it is the correct view), meaning the later school (mutakhkhirūn) prefers Abu Yusuf's view. Note that tarjih is not arbitrary; it is based on the strength of the analogy and practical ease.
The mufta bihi in many later fatwa collections follows this tarjih.
5
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical consequence: If a fasting person intentionally vomits less than a mouthful, according to Abu Yusuf the fast remains valid and no qada' is needed; according to Zahir al-Riwayah, qada' is required. The fatwa follows Abu Yusuf.
This illustrates how an intra-madhhab ikhtilaf affects real practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو أدخلَ حَلْقَةً دُخاناً بصُنْعِهِ، أو استقاءَ، ولو دونَ مِلء الفم، في ظاهر الروايةِ. وَشَرَطَ أَبو يُوسُفَ، رَحِمَهُ الله: ملء الفم، وهو الصحيح أو أعاد ما ذَرَعَهُ مِنَ القَيْء، وكانَ مِلْءَ الفم، وهو ذاكر لصوْمِهِ، أو أكل ما بين أَسنانِهِ، وَكَانَ قَدْرَ الحِمْصَةِ، أو نوى الصوم نهاراً، بعدما أكل ناسياً، قبلَ إِيجَادِ نِيَّتِهِ مِنَ النَّهَارِ، أَوْ أُغْمِيَ عليه، ولو جميع الشهر ، إلا أنه لا يقضي اليومَ الَّذي حَدَثَ فيه الإغماء، أَوْ حَدَثَ في ليلتهِ، أَوْ جُنّ، غيرَ مُمْتَد جميع الشهر، ولا يلزمه قضاؤه بإفاقته، ليلاً. (1) وكذا لو ابتلع خيطاً وطرفه بيده ثم أخرجه لا ينتقض صومه، ولو ابتلعه كله انتقض وعليه القضاء، كذا في « التجنيس وقدمنا ) نظيره ( أو أدخلَ حَلْقَةُ دُخاناً بصُنْعِهِ) متعمداً إلى جوفه أو دماغه؛ لوجود المفطر وهذا في دخان غير العنبر والـعــود، وفيهما لا يبعـد لزوم الكفارة أيضاً للنفع والتداوي، وكذا الدخان الحادث شربه وابتدع بهذا الزمان كما قدمناه). (أو استقاء) أي: تعمد إخراجه (ولو دونَ مِلء الفم، في ظاهرِ الرّواية لإطلاق قوله : ( وَمَنِ اسْتَقَاءَ عَمْداً فَلْيَقْض ) (۳) وشَرَطَ أبو يُوسُفَ، رَحِمَهُ اللهُ) أن يكون (مِل الفم، وهو الصحيح؛ لأن ما دونه كالعدم حكماً، حتى لا ينقض الوضوء، (أو أعادَ ) [ بصنعه ] (ما ذَرَعَهُ) أي غلبه وخرج بغير اختياره (من القيء، وكانَ مِلْءَ الفم) وإن كان أقل ففيه روايتان عن أبي يوسف، في رواية: لا يفطـر لـعـدم الخروج، وفي رواية: يفطر لكثرة الصنع هذا (وهو ذاكر لصوْمِهِ)، أما لو كان ناسياً فلا يفطر لما تقدم، أو أكل ما) بقي من سحوره (بين أسنانه، وكانَ قَدْرَ الحِمصَةِ) لإمكان الاحتراز عنـه بـلا مشقة. (أو نــوى الصوم نهاراً، بعدما أكل) نهاراً ( ناسياً، قبل إيجاد نيته ) الصوم ( منَ النَّهارِ)، كما في اغنية ذوي الأحكام، وهو حاشيتي على ( الدرر والغرر ». (أو أُغْمِيَ عليه لأنه نوع مرض يضعف القـــوى ولا يزيل الحجا)، فلا ينافي الوجوب ولا الأداء، فيقضي (ولو) أغمي عـلـيـه (جميع الشهر ) لأنـه بمنزلة النوم، وامتداده نادر، ولا حرج في ترتب الحكم على ما هو من النوادر، بخلاف الجنون، (إلاّ أنّه لا يقضي اليومَ الَّذي حَدَثَ فيه الإغماء، أوْ حَدَثَ في ليلته)؛ لوجود الصوم بنيته، إذ الظاهر أنه ينوي الصوم من الليل حملاً للمسلم على الصلاح، حتى لو تيقن أنه لم ينو يقضيه أيضاً، كمـا لـو كـان مسافراً أو مريضاً أو متهتكاً يعتاد الأكل في رمضان، (أو جُنّ) جنوناً غيرَ مُمْتَدِّ جميعَ الشَّهر)، بأن أفاق في وقت النية نهاراً، إذ لا حرج فيما دونه، (وَ) في لزوم قضاء الشهر المستوعب وهو مدفوع، (لا يلزمه قضاؤه ) أي : لا يلزم المجنون قضاء الشهر المستوعب حقيقة أو حكماً وهو (بإفاقته، ليلاً)
Or he intentionally inhales smoke, or vomits, even if less than a mouthful, according to the apparent narration. Abu Yusuf, may Allah have mercy on him, stipulated a mouthful, and it is the correct view. Or he returns what he regurgitates (what overcame him) from vomit, and it is a mouthful, while he is mindful of his fast. Or he eats what is between his teeth, and it is the size of a chickpea. Or he intended to fast during the day after having eaten forgetfully, before initiating his intention from the day. Or he becomes unconscious, even for the whole month, except that he does not make up the day in which the unconsciousness occurred, or it occurred during its night. Or he becomes insane, not covering the whole month, and he is not required to make it up upon regaining consciousness at night. (1) Similarly, if he swallows a thread with its end in his hand and then pulls it out, his fast is not broken; if he swallows it entirely, it is broken and he must make up, as in al-Tajnis. And we have presented its analogy. (Or he intentionally inhales smoke into his stomach or brain; because an invalidator exists. This is regarding smoke other than amber and aloeswood, in which it is not unlikely that expiation is also necessary for benefit and treatment. Similarly, the smoke that is drunk and innovated in this time, as we have mentioned.) (Or vomits) i.e., intentionally expelling it (even if less than a mouthful, according to the apparent narration due to the generality of his statement: 'Whoever vomits intentionally, let him make up.') And Abu Yusuf, may Allah have mercy on him, stipulated that it be a mouthful, and it is the correct view; because less than that is like nothing in law, such that it does not break wudu. (Or he returns) [by his action] (what he regurgitates) i.e., what overcame him and came out involuntarily (of vomit, and it is a mouthful). If it is less, there are two narrations from Abu Yusuf: in one narration, it does not break the fast due to the lack of exit; in another, it breaks the fast due to the frequency of the action this (while he is mindful of his fast). As for if he was forgetful, he does not break as previously. Or he eats what remains of his suhur (between his teeth and it is the size of a chickpea) because it is possible to avoid it without difficulty. (Or he intended to fast during the day after having eaten) during the day (forgetfully, before initiating his intention) of fasting (from the day), as in Ghunyat Dhawi al-Ahkam, and it is my hashya on al-Durar wa al-Ghurar. (Or he becomes unconscious) because it is a kind of illness that weakens the powers and does not remove intellect, so it does not negate obligation nor performance, so he makes up (even if) he is unconscious (the entire month) because it is like sleep, and its extension is rare, and there is no harm in attaching a ruling to what is among rarities, unlike insanity. (Except that he does not make up the day in which the unconsciousness occurred, or it occurred during its night) due to the existence of the fast with its intention, as the apparent is that he intends fasting from the night, carrying the Muslim upon righteousness, until if he is certain that he did not intend, he also makes it up, just as if he was a traveler or sick or a dissolute person accustomed to eating in Ramadan. (Or he becomes insane) insanity not extending the entire month, (i.e., he regained consciousness at the time of intention during the day, as there is no hardship in what is less than that). (And) regarding the necessity of making up the month that covers (which is refuted), (he is not required to make it up) i.e., the insane person is not required to make up the month that covers de jure or de facto (upon regaining consciousness, at night).
Source pages p. 659

Commentary
This passage presents a series of rulings on nullifiers of fasting, including intentional vomiting, returning vomit, eating residual food, intention during the day, unconsciousness, and insanity. The key intra-Hanafi dispute is on vomiting: according to the apparent narration (Zahir al-Riwayah), any intentional vomiting breaks the fast, even less than a mouthful, based on the general command. Abu Yusuf restricts it to a mouthful, which is considered the correct (sahih) view in this text. The reasoning is that less than a mouthful is legally negligible (like not breaking wudu). There are also two narrations from Abu Yusuf on returning vomit that was a mouthful: one view says it breaks the fast, another says no. Unconsciousness is treated like sleep: it does not remove obligation, and if it covers the whole month, one must make up all days except the day it started (if intended from night). Insanity covering the whole month relieves the obligation entirely, but if it is less than a month, one must make up the days missed. The text also covers swallowing a thread; if the end is held, the fast is not broken; if fully swallowed, it breaks.

Key Terms
استقاء (istiqā’): Intentionally inducing vomiting.
ملء الفم (mil’ al-fam): A mouthful, the quantity required to break the fast according to Abu Yusuf.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the ruling on a fasting person who intentionally vomits less than a mouthful?
A) The fast is invalid and requires kaffara
B) The fast is invalid and requires qada' according to the apparent narration
C) The fast remains valid according to both views
D) The fast is invalid and requires only qada' according to Abu Yusuf

Key Takeaway
The relied-upon Hanafi position on intentional vomiting is that only a mouthful or more breaks the fast, due to qiyas with wudu and the principle of legal negligibility.

Sawm — Fasting
beginner
8 min
Rulings on Returning Vomit, Eating Residual Food, and Reintention
Additional nullifiers related to returning vomit, eating food stuck in teeth, and making intention after eating forgetfully.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three actions that invalidate a fast according to the Hanafi school and identify the conditions for each (mouthful vomit, chickpea-sized residual food, and intention after forgetful eating).

Core Idea
Three specific actions break the fast: returning mouthful vomit, eating residual food of chickpea size, and intending to fast after forgetful eating during the day.

This passage from Nur al-Idah presents three additional scenarios that invalidate a fast in the Hanafi school. While the general rule is that eating and drinking break the fast, these cases address borderline situations: returning vomit, consuming small residual food, and making intention after the day has started. Understanding the precise conditions ensures correct practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first ruling: returning mouthful vomit
Find in the text the phrase 'أو أعاد ما ذرعه من القيء وكان ملء الفم'. This ruling states that if a fasting person involuntarily vomits a mouthful and then deliberately swallows it back while mindful of the fast, the fast is broken. Note the conditions: the vomit must be a mouthful (ملء الفم) and the person must be remembering (ذاكر لصومه).
Involuntary vomiting does not break the fast; only the voluntary return matters.
2
Identify the second ruling: eating residual food
Find 'أو أكل ما بين أسنانه وكان قدر الحمصة'. This ruling states that if leftover food from suhur remains between teeth and one eats it during the day, the fast breaks only if the amount is the size of a chickpea or more. Less than that is excusable due to difficulty in avoidance.
The threshold is chickpea size; smaller particles are ignored.
3
Identify the third ruling: intention after forgetful eating
Find 'أو نوى الصوم نهاراً بعدما أكل ناسياً قبل إيجاد نيته من النهار'. This ruling states that if someone forgetfully eats during the day and then makes an intention to fast later in the same day, that intention does not count and the fast remains invalid. The forgetful eating already broke the day's potential fast.
The fast is not valid because the day was interrupted before the intention.
4
Summarize the common consequence
All three actions necessitate qada' (make-up) of the fast. No kaffara (expiation) is required unless otherwise specified.
These are all nullifiers that require makeup, not expiation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو أعاد ما ذَرَعَهُ مِنَ القَيْء، وكانَ مِلْءَ الفم، وهو ذاكر لصوْمِهِ، أو أكل ما بين أَسنانِهِ، وَكَانَ قَدْرَ الحِمْصَةِ، أو نوى الصوم نهاراً، بعدما أكل ناسياً، قبلَ إِيجَادِ نِيَّتِهِ مِنَ النَّهَارِ
Or he returns what he regurgitated (what overcame him) from vomit, and it is a mouthful, while he is mindful of his fast. Or he eats what is between his teeth, and it is the size of a chickpea. Or he intended to fast during the day after having eaten forgetfully, before initiating his intention from the day.
Source pages p. 659

Commentary
These are three distinct rulings on fast invalidation. First, if someone involuntarily vomits a mouthful (qay' ma dhara'ahu), and then deliberately swallows it back while remembering his fast, the fast is broken and requires makeup. This is because the return constitutes consumption. Second, if leftover food from suhur (pre-dawn meal) remains between teeth, and one eats it during the day, the fast breaks only if the amount is approximately the size of a chickpea; less than that is excusable due to difficulty in avoidance. Third, if someone forgetfully eats during the day and then, before initiating his fast from that day (i.e., before making a proper intention for that day), decides to intend the fast, that intention does not count because the day has already been interrupted by the eating after the time of imsak. The fast is invalid and must be made up.

Key Terms
ذرعه القيء (dhara‘ahu al-qay’): Vomitus that comes out involuntarily, overcoming the person.
قدر الحمصة (qadr al-ḥimmaṣa): The size of a chickpea, a legal measure for residual food that breaks the fast.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on a fasting person who forgetfully eats a piece of food smaller than a chickpea from between his teeth?
A) The fast is invalid and requires qada'
B) The fast remains valid because it is a small amount
C) The fast becomes makruh but valid
D) The fast is invalid and requires kaffara

Key Takeaway
A fast is invalidated by intentionally returning a mouthful of vomit, eating a chickpea-sized bit of residual food, or making an intention to fast after forgetful eating during the day.

Hanafi Rulings on Unconsciousness and Insanity in Fasting
Rulings on unconsciousness and insanity affecting the fast.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to STATE the Hanafi rulings on making up fasts for unconsciousness and insanity, and APPLY the distinction to determine liability for qada.

Core Idea
Unconsciousness does not remove liability; insanity covering the whole month does.

Fasting during Ramadan is an obligation upon every sane adult Muslim. But what happens if a person loses consciousness or becomes insane for part or all of the month? This passage from Imdad al-Fattah explains the Hanafi position: unconsciousness is treated like sleep—obligation remains—while insanity that spans the entire month removes liability entirely. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly determining makeup (qada) requirements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditions
The passage distinguishes between unconsciousness (ighma') and insanity (junun). Note the key difference: unconsciousness 'does not remove intellect' while insanity does.
This distinction is the foundation for different rulings.
2
Determine ruling for unconsciousness
For unconsciousness, makeup is required for all days except the first day if the person intended to fast from the night. The text presumes intention for a Muslim unless certainty otherwise.
Exception: day of onset if intention existed.
3
Determine ruling for insanity
If insanity covers the entire month (de jure or de facto), no makeup is required. If it covers less, only the days of insanity are exempt, provided the person regains consciousness during the night or before the time of intention.
Full-month insanity removes liability entirely.
4
Recognize the reasoning (ta'lil)
Unconsciousness is likened to illness and sleep—it weakens powers but does not remove intellect, so obligation remains. Insanity removes intellect (zawal al-'aql), thus lifts the obligation.
The core legal cause (illa) is presence or absence of intellect.
5
Apply to a scenario
A person is unconscious for 10 days, having intended the first day from the night. He must make up 9 days. A person insane for the entire Ramadan makes up nothing.
Test understanding with hypotheticals.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أَوْ أُغْمِيَ عليه، ولو جميع الشهر ، إلا أنه لا يقضي اليومَ الَّذي حَدَثَ فيه الإغماء، أَوْ حَدَثَ في ليلتهِ، أَوْ جُنّ، غيرَ مُمْتَد جميع الشهر، ولا يلزمه قضاؤه بإفاقته، ليلاً. (أو أُغْمِيَ عليه لأنه نوع مرض يضعف القـــوى ولا يزيل الحجا)، فلا ينافي الوجوب ولا الأداء، فيقضي (ولو) أغمي عـلـيـه (جميع الشهر ) لأنـه بمنزلة النوم، وامتداده نادر، ولا حرج في ترتب الحكم على ما هو من النوادر، بخلاف الجنون، (إلاّ أنّه لا يقضي اليومَ الَّذي حَدَثَ فيه الإغماء، أوْ حَدَثَ في ليلته)؛ لوجود الصوم بنيته، إذ الظاهر أنه ينوي الصوم من الليل حملاً للمسلم على الصلاح، حتى لو تيقن أنه لم ينو يقضيه أيضاً، كمـا لـو كـان مسافراً أو مريضاً أو متهتكاً يعتاد الأكل في رمضان، (أو جُنّ) جنوناً غيرَ مُمْتَدِّ جميعَ الشَّهر)، بأن أفاق في وقت النية نهاراً، إذ لا حرج فيما دونه، (وَ) في لزوم قضاء الشهر المستوعب وهو مدفوع، (لا يلزمه قضاؤه ) أي : لا يلزم المجنون قضاء الشهر المستوعب حقيقة أو حكماً وهو (بإفاقته، ليلاً)
Or he becomes unconscious, even for the whole month, except that he does not make up the day in which the unconsciousness occurred, or it occurred during its night. Or he becomes insane, not extending the entire month, and he is not required to make it up upon regaining consciousness at night. (Or he becomes unconscious) because it is a kind of illness that weakens the powers and does not remove intellect, so it does not negate obligation nor performance, so he makes up (even if) he is unconscious (the entire month) because it is like sleep, and its extension is rare, and there is no harm in attaching a ruling to what is among rarities, unlike insanity. (Except that he does not make up the day in which the unconsciousness occurred, or it occurred during its night) due to the existence of the fast with its intention, as the apparent is that he intends fasting from the night, carrying the Muslim upon righteousness, until if he is certain that he did not intend, he also makes it up, just as if he was a traveler or sick or a dissolute person accustomed to eating in Ramadan. (Or he becomes insane) insanity not extending the entire month, (i.e., he regained consciousness at the time of intention during the day, as there is no hardship in what is less than that). (And) regarding the necessity of making up the month that covers (which is refuted), (he is not required to make it up) i.e., the insane person is not required to make up the month that covers de jure or de facto (upon regaining consciousness, at night).
Source pages p. 659

Commentary
This passage explains the effect of unconsciousness (ighma') and insanity (junun) on the fast. Unconsciousness is treated as an illness that weakens but does not remove intellect, so it is analogous to sleep: the obligation of fasting remains, and all days of unconsciousness must be made up, except the day it began if the intention was made the night before. If the unconsciousness starts during the night, the intention is presumed valid, so that day is counted as fasted. If one is sure no intention was made, then that day must also be made up. For insanity, if it extends the entire month, no makeup is required because the person lacked liability. If it is less than the whole month, only the days of insanity are not required, provided the person regains consciousness during the night or before the time of intention. The text emphasizes that unconsciousness is rare in extending a full month, but the ruling is not burdensome. The difference from insanity is that insanity removes intellect entirely (zawal al-'aql), while unconsciousness does not.

Key Terms
إغماء (ighmā’): Unconsciousness, a state where senses are lost but intellect remains intact.
جنون ممتد (junūn mumtadd): Insanity that extends over the entire month of Ramadan.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A person becomes unconscious during the night of Ramadan and remains unconscious for the entire month. What must he do regarding his fasts?
A) He does not need to make up any days because he was unconscious
B) He must make up all days except the first day if he had intended
C) He must make up all days without exception
D) He must make up only the days after the first day

Key Takeaway
Unconsciousness does not remove the obligation of fasting; only insanity covering the entire month lifts liability for makeup.

Swallowing Smoke Deliberately Nullifies Fast
Ruling on deliberate smoke ingestion as a fast nullifier

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on deliberate smoke ingestion during fasting and identify the conditions that trigger nullification.

Core Idea
Intentional ingestion of any smoke to the stomach or brain breaks the fast.

Many assume that only eating or drinking nullifies the fast, but the Hanafi school holds that introducing any substance into the body through a passage – including smoke – has the same effect. This lesson examines a key passage from Nur al-Idah that explicitly covers smoke, with an important extension to modern smoking and medicinal aromatics.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
Read the Arabic: 'أو أدخل حَلْقَةً دُخاناً بصُنْعِهِ'. The ruling is that if a person deliberately inserts smoke into their throat, the fast is nullified.
The act requires intention (بصُنْعِهِ) - accidental inhalation does not nullify.
2
Understand the condition of intentionality
The text specifies 'متعمداً إلى جوفه أو دماغه' – deliberately into the stomach or brain. This indicates that the nullification depends on the smoke reaching an internal cavity with intent.
Hanafi: the nullifier (mufṭir) is any substance entering the body through an open passage intentionally.
3
Distinguish ordinary smoke from aromatic smoke
The passage distinguishes 'دخان غير العنبر والعود' (smoke other than amber and aloeswood). For ordinary smoke, qada is required; for amber/aloeswood, kaffarah may also be required because they are used for benefit or medicine.
This shows that the ruling can change based on the substance's purpose and nutritional/medicinal value.
4
Apply to modern smoking
The commentator explicitly includes 'الدخان الحادث شربه' – the modern smoking innovated in this time (e.g., cigarettes, shisha). This is treated like ordinary smoke: deliberate inhalation nullifies the fast and requires both qada and kaffarah.
This is an example of the Hanafi method of extending rulings to new cases based on the underlying cause (ʿilla).
5
Derive the underlying principle
The reasoning (ʿilla) is 'لوجود المفطر' – because the nullifier exists. Any substance that enters the stomach or brain via an open passage and is deliberately taken in is a nullifier, regardless of whether it is food, drink, or smoke.
This principle aligns with the broader Hanafi definition of mufṭir: anything that reaches the interior (jauf) intentionally.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو أدخلَ حَلْقَةً دُخاناً بصُنْعِهِ، متعمداً إلى جوفه أو دماغه؛ لوجود المفطر وهذا في دخان غير العنبر والعود، وفيهما لا يبعد لزوم الكفارة أيضاً للنفع والتداوي، وكذا الدخان الحادث شربه وابتدع بهذا الزمان كما قدمناه.
Or he inserted into his throat smoke by his own doing, intentionally into his stomach or brain, because the nullifier exists. This applies to smoke other than amber and aloeswood; for those two, it is not unlikely that kaffarah is also required due to benefit and medicinal use. Likewise, the modern smoking that has emerged and been innovated in this time, as we have previously mentioned.
Source pages p. 659

Commentary
This passage is a ruling explaining that deliberately swallowing smoke (dakhan) breaks the fast because it reaches the stomach or brain, which constitutes ingestion of a substance. The commentator distinguishes between ordinary smoke and that from amber (anbar) or aloeswood (ud), which are aromatic and often used for medicinal purposes; for these, kaffarah may be required because they are consumed for benefit. He explicitly includes modern smoking (e.g., cigarettes), which is an innovation of later times, as a nullifier requiring both qada and kaffarah. The reasoning is that the smoke is a substance that reaches the internal cavity, similar to food or drink.

Key Terms
دخان (dukhān): Smoke that is deliberately ingested into the throat or brain by one's own action.
مفطر (mufṭir): Something that breaks the fast, requiring qada and possibly kaffarah.
جوف (jawf): Interior cavity of the body, including the stomach and brain in this context.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Does swallowing incense smoke (e.g., from aloeswood) with intention break the fast according to this passage?
A) No, because it is not food or drink
B) Yes, and kaffarah may be required
C) Only qada is required, no kaffarah
D) It is makruh but does not nullify

Key Takeaway
Deliberately ingesting any smoke into the stomach or brain nullifies the fast, requiring qada and possibly kaffarah.

Induced Vomiting: The Mouthful Condition
Intra-Hanafi ruling on induced vomiting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on induced vomiting, identify the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf (Abu Yusuf vs. the rest), and explain why the mouthful condition is preferred.

Core Idea
Induced vomiting nullifies the fast only if it is a mouthful — the preferred Hanafi view sets a threshold.

Fasting is an act of worship that requires safeguarding one's fast from internal and external invalidators. One common issue is intentionally induced vomiting. Does any amount break the fast, or is there a minimum? This passage from Imdad al-Fattah explains the Hanafi position and the intra-madhhab difference: while the apparent transmission (Zahir al-Riwayah) holds that any intentional vomiting nullifies, Imam Abu Yusuf conditioned it to be a mouthful, and this has become the relied-upon view.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling from the passage
Identify what the passage says about intentional vomiting: it is a fast nullifier. Note the qualification of intentionality (amdan) vs. involuntary.
Involuntary vomiting does not break the fast.
2
Identify the two competing positions
The passage presents two views: (1) Zahir al-Riwayah (Imam Muhammad and Abu Hanifa): any amount, even less than a mouthful. (2) Abu Yusuf: only if it is a mouthful (mil' al-fam).
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
3
Analyze the evidence for each view
The hadith 'Man istaqa'a 'amdan falyaqdhi' is cited as a general proof. For Abu Yusuf, the analogy (qiyas) to wudu: less than a mouthful of vomit does not nullify wudu, so it should not nullify the fast either.
Abu Yusuf's condition is based on the principle that small quantities are legally negligible.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
The commentator states 'wa huwa al-sahih' (this is the correct view), adopting Abu Yusuf's position. Thus, the fatwa is given on the mouthful condition.
The authority weight is from the later scholars (fatawa mutakhkhirin).
5
Apply the ruling to a scenario
Suppose a fasting person intentionally inserts a finger to induce vomiting and expels a small amount. According to the preferred view, the fast remains valid because it was less than a mouthful.
If it is a mouthful or more, the fast is invalid and qada is required.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو استقاء أي: تعمد إخراجه ولو دونَ مِلء الفم، في ظاهرِ الرّواية لإطلاق قوله : ( وَمَنِ اسْتَقَاءَ عَمْداً فَلْيَقْض ) وشَرَطَ أَبو يُوسُفَ، رَحِمَهُ اللهُ أن يكون مِل الفم، وهو الصحيح؛ لأن ما دونه كالعدم حكماً، حتى لا ينقض الوضوء.
Or he induced vomiting, meaning he intentionally expelled it, even if less than a mouthful, in the apparent narration (zahir al-riwayah) due to the general meaning of his statement: 'Whoever intentionally vomits, let him make up [the fast].' And Abu Yusuf, may Allah have mercy on him, stipulated that it be a mouthful, and this is the correct view, because less than that is legally considered non-existent, such that it does not nullify wudu.
Source pages p. 659

Commentary
This passage addresses induced vomiting (istiqa') as a fast nullifier. The Hanafi position in Zahir al-Riwayah, based on a hadith command, is that any intentional vomiting, regardless of quantity, breaks the fast and requires qada. Abu Yusuf, however, conditioned that it must be a mouthful (mil' al-fam) to nullify, analogizing to wudu where less than a mouthful does not break it. The commentator states that Abu Yusuf's view is considered the 'sahih' (correct) one, likely because it is more cautious and consistent with legal principles: small amounts are treated as non-existent. This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf where the mufta bihi is the mouthful condition.

Key Terms
استقاء (istiqā'): Intentionally inducing vomiting by putting a finger in the throat or similar means.
ملء الفم (mil' al-fam): A mouthful, the quantity that fills the mouth; used as a threshold for nullification.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What condition did Abu Yusuf set for induced vomiting to nullify the fast, according to the preferred view in this passage?
A) The vomiting must occur twice
B) The vomit must be a mouthful
C) The vomiting must be involuntary
D) The vomit must include food

Key Takeaway
Induced vomiting nullifies the fast only if it was intentionally done and the expelled amount is a mouthful or more; less than a mouthful does not break the fast in the relied-upon Hanafi position.

Ruling on Returning Involuntary Vomit While Fasting
Intra-Hanafi dispute on returning involuntary vomit

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on returning involuntary vomit and distinguish the two narrations from Abu Yusuf regarding less than a mouthful.

Core Idea
Returning a mouthful of involuntary vomit breaks the fast; less than a mouthful is disputed.

What happens if someone vomits involuntarily and then swallows it back? The ruling depends on the amount and intent. This passage from Imdad al-Fattah clarifies the Hanafi position and presents two narrations from Abu Yusuf for smaller amounts, highlighting the relevance of mindfulness.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its circumstances
Determine that the vomit came out involuntarily (dhar'ahu) and was deliberately returned while the person is mindful of the fast.
Forgetfulness changes the ruling.
2
Determine the amount of vomit
Classify the vomit as a mouthful or less than a mouthful. This is the key variable.
A mouthful (mil' al-fam) is a significant quantity.
3
Apply the ruling for a mouthful
If it was a mouthful and deliberately returned while mindful, the fast is nullified (mufsid).
This is clear from the text.
4
Examine the two narrations for less than a mouthful
For less than a mouthful, there are two narrations from Abu Yusuf: one says it does not break, another says it does. Analyse the reasoning behind each.
First narration: no voluntary expulsion; second narration: excessive action.
5
Note the role of forgetfulness
If the person was forgetful (nasi), the fast is not broken regardless of amount, following the general principle.
Consistent with previous rulings on forgetfulness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو أعاد [ بصنعه ] ما ذَرَعَهُ أي غلبه وخرج بغير اختياره من القيء، وكانَ مِلْءَ الفم وإن كان أقل ففيه روايتان عن أبي يوسف، في رواية: لا يفطر لعدم الخروج، وفي رواية: يفطر لكثرة الصنع هذا وهو ذاكر لصومه، أما لو كان ناسياً فلا يفطر لما تقدم.
Or he returned by his own doing what he regurgitated, i.e., what overcame him and came out involuntarily of vomit, and it was a mouthful. If it was less, then there are two narrations from Abu Yusuf: in one narration, it does not break the fast because there was no [voluntary] expulsion; in another narration, it breaks the fast due to the excessive action. And this while he is mindful of his fast. As for if he was forgetful, it does not break the fast as previously mentioned.
Source pages p. 659

Commentary
This passage deals with someone who experiences involuntary vomiting (dhar'ahu, i.e., overpowered) and then deliberately swallows it back. If the vomit is a mouthful, it nullifies the fast because he intentionally re-ingested a significant amount. If less than a mouthful, there are two narrations from Abu Yusuf: one says it does not break the fast (since the original exit was involuntary, and the small amount is like non-existent), and another says it does break because of the deliberate act of returning it. The commentator notes that this applies when the person is mindful (dhakir) of the fast; if forgetful, it does not break the fast, consistent with the principle that forgetful eating/drinking does not invalidate.

Key Terms
ذرعه (dhar'ahu): Vomiting that overcomes a person involuntarily; it came out by itself.
مِلْءَ الفم (mil' al-fam): A mouthful; the amount that fills the mouth.
رواية (riwayah): A narration or transmitted opinion from a Hanafi authority.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It does not break the fast because it came out involuntarily.
B) It definitely breaks the fast.
C) There are two narrations.
D) It is makruh but not mufsid.

Key Takeaway
A mouthful of involuntary vomit returned deliberately breaks the fast; less than a mouthful is subject to two opinions within the Hanafi school.

The Chickpea Threshold: When Food Remnants Nullify the Fast
Ruling with rationale on a specific nullifier of the fast.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on swallowing food remnants stuck between teeth during fasting and apply the chickpea threshold to determine nullification.

Core Idea
Food remnants break the fast only if they equal a chickpea — hardship excuses smaller amounts.

A common question during Ramadan is whether food stuck between teeth invalidates the fast. The Hanafi school provides a clear, practical threshold: the size of a chickpea (himmasah). This ruling balances the obligation to avoid eating with the recognition that completely removing every trace of food is burdensome. Let us examine the precise ruling and its rationale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act
The act under discussion is deliberately swallowing food remnants that remained from the pre-dawn meal (suhur) and are stuck between the teeth.
The act is separate from intentional eating; remnants are leftovers.
2
Determine the Hukm Level
If the amount swallowed is the size of a chickpea (himmaṣah) or more, the fast is nullified (mufsid). If it is less, the fast remains valid.
The threshold is measured by volume, not weight.
3
Understand the Rationale (Illah)
The reason for the threshold is that a chickpea-sized amount can be avoided without undue hardship (mashaqqah). Smaller amounts are not easily detected or removed, so they are excused.
This reflects the Hanafi principle of raf' al-haraj (removal of hardship).
4
Distinguish Between Deliberate and Forgetful Acts
The nullification applies only when the person deliberately swallows the remnants. If done forgetfully, the fast is not broken, as per general rules.
Forgetfulness is excused in fasting.
5
Note the Cross-Madhhab Difference
Some other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) hold that any amount, however small, nullifies the fast if swallowed deliberately. The Hanafi position is based on ease.
The dispute is legitimate; each school has its proofs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو أكل ما بقي من سحوره بين أسنانه، وكانَ قَدْرَ الحِمصَةِ لإمكان الاحتراز عنه بلا مشقة.
Or he ate what remained of his suhur between his teeth, and it was the size of a chickpea, because it is possible to avoid it without hardship.
Source pages p. 659

Commentary
This ruling states that if food from suhur (pre-dawn meal) remains stuck between the teeth and the fasting person deliberately swallows it, it nullifies the fast only if the amount is the size of a chickpea (himmasah) or more. The rationale is that such an amount can be avoided without difficulty; smaller amounts are excused because they are difficult to detect and remove completely. This reflects the Hanafi principle of not imposing hardship (haraj) on the fasting person.

Key Terms
حمصة (himmaṣah): A chickpea; used as a measure for the minimum amount that nullifies the fast when swallowing food remnants.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the minimum amount of food from between teeth that, if swallowed, nullifies the fast according to the Hanafi school?
A) A sesame seed
B) A chickpea
C) A date
D) Any amount

Key Takeaway
Swallowing food remnants during fasting nullifies the fast only if the amount is a chickpea or more and done deliberately; smaller amounts are excused due to hardship.

intermediate
6 min
Intention After Forgetful Eating During the Day
Ruling on making intention to fast after forgetfully eating during the day

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on making the intention to fast after forgetful eating and distinguish it from other schools.

Core Idea
Forgetful eating does not break the fast; intention can still be made.

The Hanafi school holds that if someone forgetfully eats during the day, the fast is not nullified. Moreover, if they then make the intention to fast before the permissible deadline, the intention is valid and the fast counts. This lesson examines the ruling and its basis, highlighting the unique Hanafi perspective.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is making the intention (niyya) to fast after having eaten forgetfully during the day. The ruling is that the intention is valid (sahih) if made before the permissible time limit.
For obligatory fasts (e.g., Ramadan), intention must be before dawn; for voluntary fasts, before midday.
2
Understand the basis of the ruling
Forgetful eating does not invalidate the fast because it is excused. Therefore, the fast continues and the person is still in a state of fasting (though they must stop eating). Intention can be made later because the day's fast is still valid.
The key principle: forgetful acts are overlooked as long as the person was not negligent.
3
Note the time constraints
The intention must be made within the time frame allowed for that type of fast. For Ramadan, intention must be made before dawn; for voluntary fasts, before the sun passes the meridian (zawal). If the forgetful eating occurs after that time, the intention is not valid.
If forgetful eating occurs after the permissible intention window, the fast is invalid and qada is required.
4
Compare with other schools
Some schools (e.g., Shafi'i) require intention before dawn for all obligatory fasts, even if forgetful eating occurs. They hold that forgetful eating does not nullify the fast, but intention cannot be made after dawn for obligatory fasts. The Hanafi school allows it because the fast is still valid.
This shows a practical difference in fatwa.
5
Apply the ruling
If a person forgetfully eats during a Ramadan day, they stop immediately upon remembering, then make the intention (if not yet done) before dawn of that same day (if dawn hasn't passed). For a voluntary fast, they can make intention before midday.
If dawn has already passed when they remember, the intention is too late and the fast must be made up.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو نوى الصوم نهاراً، بعدما أكل ناسياً، قبل إيجاد نيته من النهار، كما في غنية ذوي الأحكام، وهو حاشيتي على الدرر والغرر.
Or he intended the fast during the day, after having eaten forgetfully, before [the time for] making his intention from the day, as in Ghunyat Dhawi al-Ahkam, and it is my hashya on al-Durar wa al-Ghurar.
Source pages p. 659

Commentary
This passage discusses a scenario where a person forgetfully eats during the day (e.g., thinking it is night) and then, after realizing, makes the intention to fast before the time when intention is normally required (i.e., before the middle of the day or similar). The commentator references his own gloss on al-Durar wa al-Ghurar and another work. The ruling implies that if one forgetfully eats, the fast is not broken (by the forgetful eating), and if he then makes a proper intention before the deadline for intention (for voluntary fasts, before midday; for obligatory, before dawn), the intention is valid and the fast counts. The text is abbreviated but conveys that forgetful eating does not prohibit subsequent valid intention.

Key Terms
نية (niyya): Intention to fast, which must be made before the time limit (for Ramadan, before dawn; for voluntary, before midday).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Forgetful eating does not invalidate the fast, and the intention to fast can be validly made later within the allowed time frame according to the Hanafi school.

Ruling on Unconsciousness in Ramadan
Ruling on unconsciousness: qada required, exception for onset day

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on qada for unconsciousness and identify the exception for the day it occurs.

Core Idea
Unconsciousness does not remove fasting obligation; only the onset day is exempt if intention is presumed.

Unconsciousness (ighmā') during Ramadan raises the question of whether missed fasts must be made up. The Hanafi school analogizes it to sleep, which does not invalidate obligation, but distinguishes it from insanity. This lesson unpacks the ruling, its proof through qiyas, and the important exception for the day unconsciousness began.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
Read the passage and determine the general ruling on unconsciousness. Note that the person must make up (qada) missed fasts.
The ruling applies even if unconsciousness covers the entire month.
2
Understand the Analogy
The passage compares unconsciousness to sleep. Both are conditions that weaken faculties but do not remove intellect (hija). Why does this justify qada?
Intellect is the basis of legal obligation (taklif).
3
Distinguish from Insanity
Insanity removes intellect, thus exempting from qada. Unconsciousness does not. The passage explicitly contrasts with insanity.
This distinction is crucial for understanding the reasoning.
4
Grasp the Exception
Note the exception: the day on which unconsciousness occurred (or the night before) is not made up, because the person is presumed to have intended the fast from the night.
This presumption (haml al-muslim ala al-salah) is a key Hanafi principle.
5
Apply the Limits
Consider the scenario where the person definitely did not intend (e.g., known traveler). The passage says he must make up even that day. Trace the reasoning.
Certainty overrides the default presumption.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو أُغْمِيَ عليه لأنه نوع مرض يضعف القوى ولا يزيل الحجا، فلا ينافي الوجوب ولا الأداء، فيقضي ولو أغمي عليه جميع الشهر لأنه بمنزلة النوم، وامتداده نادر، ولا حرج في ترتب الحكم على ما هو من النوادر، بخلاف الجنون، إلاّ أنّه لا يقضي اليومَ الذي حَدَثَ فيه الإغماء، أوْ حَدَثَ في ليلته؛ لوجود الصوم بنيته، إذ الظاهر أنه ينوي الصوم من الليل حملاً للمسلم على الصلاح، حتى لو تيقن أنه لم ينو يقضيه أيضاً، كما لو كان مسافراً أو مريضاً أو متهتكاً يعتاد الأكل في رمضان.
Or he fell unconscious because it is a type of illness that weakens the faculties but does not remove intellect (‘hija), so it does not contradict obligation nor performance, so he makes up (qada). Even if he was unconscious for the entire month, because it is analogous to sleep, and its continuation is rare, and there is no difficulty in attaching the ruling to what is rare, unlike insanity. Except that he does not make up the day in which the unconsciousness occurred, or if it occurred during its night, because the fast exists with its intention, as the apparent assumption is that he intended the fast from the night, carrying the Muslim [assumption] of righteousness. So even if he is certain that he did not intend, he also makes it up, as if he was a traveler or sick or a transgressor who habitually eats in Ramadan.
Source pages p. 659

Commentary
This passage addresses unconsciousness (ighma') during Ramadan. It rules that unconsciousness is akin to a sickness that weakens the body but does not remove intellect (hija), so it does not negate the obligation of fasting nor the validity of performance if the person had made the intention. Therefore, one must make up (qada) the days missed. This applies even if unconsciousness covers the entire month, because it is compared to sleep (which is common and does not remove obligation), and its extension is rare. There is no harm in applying the ruling to a rare case. However, an exception is made: one does not have to make up the specific day when unconsciousness occurred (i.e., if he became unconscious during the day) or if it occurred during the night preceding the day, because the default assumption is that a Muslim intended the fast from the night (carrying the Muslim on righteousness). If one is certain that he did not intend (e.g., a traveler or a rebellious person), then he must make up that day as well.

Key Terms
إغماء (ighmā'): Unconsciousness; a state where one loses awareness but retains mental capacity (unlike insanity).
حجا (ḥijā): Intellect or mental faculty; the presence of hija distinguishes ighma' from junun.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because it lasts longer
B) Because it does not remove intellect (hija)
C) Because it is a type of illness
D) Because it is always intentional

Key Takeaway
Unconsciousness during Ramadan does not remove the obligation to fast; the day it begins is excepted due to presumed intention.

Insanity During Ramadan: When Does Qada Become Obligatory?
Ruling on insanity not covering the entire month

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on qada for insanity during Ramadan and identify the critical condition (lucidity at valid intention time) that triggers the obligation to make up fasts.

Core Idea
Insanity removes obligation, but qada is required only if one regains consciousness at a time when intention is valid (before midday).

This passage refines the general principle that insanity exempts from fasting. It addresses the borderline case where insanity does not cover the entire month. The key question: does a moment of lucidity obligate making up the entire month? The Hanafi answer is nuanced, hinging on the timing of that lucidity relative to the intention period.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is insanity during Ramadan. Determine the ruling: if insanity covers the entire month (mustaw'ib), no qada. If partial, examine when lucidity occurs.
2
Locate the dalil
The text does not cite a direct primary source but uses talil (reasoning): insanity removes legal responsibility (raf' al-taklif). It also invokes fatwa authorities (Majma' al-Nawazil, al-Fath).
The reasoning is based on the hadith 'The pen is lifted from three…' etc., though not quoted here.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The key legal link: if one is lucid at a time when intention for fasting is valid (before midday), then the fast becomes obligatory for that day; otherwise, no obligation. The condition is the possibility of forming a valid intention.
Intention for Ramadan fast must be made before dawn, but for qada or voluntary fasts, it can be up to midday. The text uses 'during the day' implying before the time of intention expires.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Scholars of Bukhara differed: some held that awakening on the first night obligates the whole month. al-Fath held that any awakening obligates qada. The text prefers Majma' al-Nawazil's view.
This is intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf, not between madhhabs.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The mufta bihi position is from Majma' al-Nawazil: no qada if awakening at night or after intention time. This is based on the principle that the night is not a time for fasting.
Shams al-A'imma also chose this view. al-Fath's stricter view is rejected.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو جُنّ جنوناً غيرَ مُمْتَدِّ جميعَ الشهر، بأن أفاق في وقت النية نهاراً، إذ لا حرج فيما دونه، وفي لزوم قضاء الشهر المستوعب وهو مدفوع، لا يلزمه قضاؤه أي : لا يلزم المجنون قضاء الشهر المستوعب حقيقة أو حكماً وهو بإفاقته، ليلاً أو نهاراً، بعدَ فَوَاتِ وقتِ النِّيَّةِ، في الصحيح. فالشرط للزوم قضاء الشهر إفاقته فيه نهاراً في وقت يصح فيه إيجاد النية، قال في مجمع النوازل : إذا أفاق أول ليلة من رمضان، ثم أصبح مجنوناً أو استوعب الشهر، اختلف فيه أئمة بخارى، والفتوى على أنه لا يلزمه القضاء لأن الليلة لا يصام فيها، وكذا لو أفاق في ليلة من وسطه أو في آخر يوم من رمضان بعد الزوال، كذا في المجتبى والنهاية وغيرهما، ومختار شمس الأئمة، وفي الفتح: يلزمه بإفاقته فيه مطلقاً والله أعلم.
Or he became insane with an insanity that does not extend the entire month, such that he regained consciousness at the time of intention during the day, because there is no difficulty in what is less than that. And regarding the obligation of making up the month if it is entirely covered, that is rejected. It is not necessary for him to make it up, i.e., it is not necessary for the insane person to make up the month if it is totally covered actually or legally, and he regains consciousness at night or during the day after the time of intention has passed, in the correct view. So the condition for making up the month is that he regains consciousness during the day at a time when intention is valid. It is stated in Majma' al-Nawazil: If he regains consciousness on the first night of Ramadan, then becomes insane in the morning or covers the whole month, the scholars of Bukhara differed, and the fatwa is that he does not have to make up because the night is not fasted. Similarly, if he regains consciousness on a night in the middle or on the last day after midday, as in al-Mujtaba and al-Nihayah and others, and the choice of Shams al-A'imma. In al-Fath: he must make up if he regains consciousness in it absolutely, and Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 660

Commentary
This passage deals with insanity (junun) during Ramadan. If a person is insane for the entire month (mustaw'ib), he is not required to make up the fasts because insanity removes legal responsibility (taklif). However, if the insanity does not cover the entire month and he regains consciousness at a time when intention can be made (e.g., during the day before midday), then he must make up the missed days. The critical condition for requiring qada is that the person becomes lucid during a day at a time when a valid intention for fasting can be formed (i.e., before the middle of the day for voluntary fasts, or before dawn for obligatory). If he regains consciousness at night, the day is already past for intention, so he does not make up that day. The commentator then cites a difference among the scholars of Bukhara: if someone regains consciousness on the first night of Ramadan, then becomes insane for the rest of the month, some say he must make up the whole month (because he was lucid at the start), but the fatwa is that he does not, because the night itself is not a time for fasting. A similar ruling applies if he regains consciousness at night in the middle of the month or after midday on the last day. However, the book al-Fath holds a stricter view: if he regains consciousness at any point during the month, he must make up all missed days. The commentator ends with 'Allahu a'lam'.

Key Terms
جنون (junūn): Insanity; a state that removes legal responsibility (raf' al-taklif) because it removes intellect.
إفاقة (ifāqa): Regaining consciousness after insanity; the moment of lucidity is key for determining qada.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Yes, because he was lucid at the start
B) No, because the night is not a time for fasting
C) Yes, according to al-Fath
D) Only if he intended to fast that night

Key Takeaway
Qada for missed fasts due to insanity is only required if one regains consciousness during the day at a time when a valid intention can be made (before midday for qada).

Qada for a Person Who Becomes Insane During Ramadan
Explains the condition for qada when insanity occurs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi fatwa position on qada for a person who becomes insane after a valid intention time, and explain the reasoning behind the intra-school dispute.

Core Idea
Qada is not required if insanity covers all daytime after valid intention time.

This passage from Imdad al-Fattah addresses a specific scenario: a person regains sanity only at night during Ramadan but remains insane during the day. Does he have to make up the entire month? The Hanafi school has an internal dispute, but the later fatwa position provides a clear ruling. Let's analyze the condition for qada and the reasoning behind the two views.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition for qada
The text states that the condition for obligation of qada of the entire month is that the person regains sanity during the daytime at a time when it is valid to make intention (i.e., before dawn or before the time of intention expires).
Intention must be made before Fajr for each fast.
2
Understand the specific scenario
The case from Majma' al-Nawazil: a person regains sanity on the first night of Ramadan, then becomes insane in the morning or for the entire month. The scholars of Bukhara differed on this.
Night is not a time for fasting, so intention cannot lead to actual fasting.
3
Note the two positions
Some scholars said qada is required if any sanity occurs in the month; the fatwa (supporting al-Mujtaba, al-Nihaya, etc.) says it is not required because the night is not a time for fasting.
The opposing view from al-Fath: qada is obligatory absolutely upon any sanity.
4
Apply the fatwa
If a person only regains sanity at night (when fasting is not possible) and remains insane during the day, qada is not obligatory. Similarly, if he regains sanity on the last day after noon, no qada.
The fatwa relies on the principle that intention must be made in a time that permits fasting.
5
Distinguish the exception
According to al-Fath, any regain of sanity during Ramadan (even at night) makes qada obligatory. This is a minority view not adopted in fatwa.
The tarjih (preferred view) is the one from the later authorities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو نهاراً، بعدَ فَوَاتِ وقتِ النِّيَّةِ، في الصحيح. فقط (أو نهاراً، بعدَ فَوَاتِ وقتِ النِّيَّةِ، في الصَّحيح فالشرط للزوم قضاء الشهر، إفاقته فـيـه نـهـاراً في وقت يصح فيه إيجاد النية، قال في مجمع النوازل» : إذا أفاق أول ليلة من رمضان، ثم أصبح مجنوناً أو استوعب الشهر، اختلف فيه أئمة بخارى، والفتوى على أنه لا يلزمه القضاء لأن الليلة لا يصـام فيها، وكذا لو أفاق في ليلة من وسطه أو في آخر يوم من رمضان بعد الزوال، كذا في « المجتبى » و« النهاية» وغيرهما، و «مختار » شمس الأئمة، وفي «الفتح»: يلزمه بإفاقته فيه مطلقاً والله أعلم.
Or daytime, after the passing of the time of intention, according to the correct view. Only (or daytime, after the passing of the time of intention, in the correct view, the condition for the obligation of making up the month is his regaining sanity during the daytime at a time when it is valid to make intention. In Majma' al-Nawazil it is said: If he regains sanity on the first night of Ramadan, then becomes insane in the morning or for the entire month, the scholars of Bukhara differed, and the fatwa is that qada is not obligatory upon him because the night is not fasted. Similarly, if he regains sanity on a night in the middle or on the last day of Ramadan after noon, as in al-Mujtaba, al-Nihaya, and others, and Mukhtar of Shams al-A'imma. In al-Fath, it is made obligatory upon him by his regaining sanity in it absolutely. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 660

Commentary
This passage discusses the condition of sanity for the obligation of qada of Ramadan fasts. The author explains that after the time for intention passes, the condition for being required to make up the entire month is that the person regains sanity during the daytime at a time when it is valid to make intention. If one regains sanity on the first night of Ramadan before dawn and then becomes insane throughout the month, the scholars of Bukhara differed. The fatwa adopted is that qada is not required because the night itself is not a time for fasting. Similarly, if one regains sanity in the middle of the month on a night or on the last day after noon, qada is not required according to many sources. However, in al-Fath, it is stated that regaining sanity at any time makes qada obligatory absolutely. This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf, with the relied-upon position being the one that does not require qada.

Key Terms
قضاء الشهر (qada' al-shahr): Making up the entire month of Ramadan fasts due to missed days.
الإفاقه (al-ifaqah): Regaining sanity from insanity (juno/an).
مجمع النوازل (Majma' al-Nawazil): A Hanafi fatwa collection by Nāṣir al-Dīn al-Samarqandī.

The Hanafi fatwa holds that qada for a person who becomes insane is not required unless he regains sanity during the daytime at a time when intention is valid.

Obligation of Imsak for Those Whose Fast Is Broken
Defines the obligation of imsak after fast is broken.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the categories of people who must refrain (imsak) after their fast is broken and explain the Hanafi principle underlying the obligation.

Core Idea
If at any point in the day you become eligible for fasting, you must refrain; otherwise, you need not.

This lesson examines a key ruling in Hanafi fiqh: the obligation of imsak—abstaining from eating, drinking, and marital relations—for the remainder of the day after one's fast has become invalid. The ruling is based on a general principle and a prophetic hadith. Understanding which categories of people are required to refrain and which are exempt is essential for correctly applying this rule in practice.

Guided Path
1
Understand the General Principle
The text states a principle: if a person becomes, at some part of the day, in a condition that would have obligated fasting had it been present at dawn, then imsak is obligatory. If not, imsak is not required. Internalize this principle as it governs all cases.
This principle differentiates between those who were eligible for fasting at dawn and those who were not.
2
Identify Categories Where Imsak is Wajib
List the categories mentioned: one whose fast is broken (even by an excuse that ends), a menstruating woman or woman with postpartum bleeding who becomes pure after dawn, a traveler who becomes a resident, a sick person who recovers, an insane person who regains sanity, a child who reaches puberty, and a disbeliever who accepts Islam after dawn. All these must refrain.
For all except the child and disbeliever, qada is also obligatory.
3
Identify Exceptions Where Imsak is Not Required
The text clarifies that those who had a valid excuse from dawn—such as menstruation, postpartum bleeding, sickness, or travel—do not have to refrain. Imitating the fast in these cases would be either imitating a forbidden act or undermining the concession.
They must, however, make up the fast later, except for the child and disbeliever.
4
Examine the Hadith Proof
The author cites the hadith of the Prophet (peace be upon him) on the day of Ashura: 'Whoever has eaten should not eat for the rest of his day.' This command (amr) indicates obligation (wujub) according to Hanafi usul.
The hadith is sahih but ahad, so its thubut is zanni; the dalala is also zanni due to the possibility of it being a recommendation.
5
Note the Intra-Hanafi Difference
There is a difference within the Hanafi school: the correct view is that imsak is wajib; some scholars considered it mustahabb. The author states 'according to the correct view' (على الصحيح), indicating the mufta bihi position is wajib.
This difference arises from interpreting the command in the hadith as obligatory or recommendatory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل يجب الإمساك بقية اليوم على من فسد صومه يجب الإمساك بقية اليوم، على مَنْ فَسَدَ صومُهُ، وعلى حائض ونُفَسَاءَ، طَهُرَتا بعد طلوع الفجر، وعلى صبِي بَلَغَ، وكافرٍ أَسْلَمَ بَعْدَ الطُّلُوعِ، وعليهم القضاء، إلَّا الأَخِيرَينِ. فصل يجب الإمساك بقية اليوم على من فسد صومه اعلم أن الأصل عندنا أن من صار في بعض النهار على صفة لـو كـان عليها في أولـه يـلـزمـه الصوم، فعليه الإمساك بقية اليوم كما يمسك الصائم، ومن صار في بعض النهار على صفـة، لـو كـان عليها مع طلوع الفجر لم يلزمه الصوم، لا يجب عليه الإمساك، فعلى هذا ( يجب الإمساك بقية اليوم على الصحيح، وقيل: يستحب؛ تشبهاً لقضاء حق الوقت، كما في يوم ا الشك، بالقدر الممكن على مَنْ فَسَدَ صومُهُ) ولو بعذر ثم زال ؛ لقوله لا لا لها في يوم عاشوراء حين كان صومه واجباً: « ألا مَنْ أكَلَ فَلا يَأكلنَّ بَقِيَّةَ يَومِهِ » (۱) ولأنه عجز عن الصوم مع الأهلية، فيلزمه الإمساك، (و) يجب الإمساك (على حائض ونفساء طهرتا بعد طلوع فجر)، ومسافر أقام ومريـض بــرئ ومجنون أفاق، (و) يجب الإمساك (عـلـى صبي بَلَغَ. وكافر أَسْلَمَ بَعْدَ الطُّلُوعِ) لمـا ذكـرنـا، بخلاف الحائض والنفساء والمريض والمسافر، حيث لا يجب عليهم الإمساك تشبهاً عند وجود العذر اتفاقاً، أمـا الحائض والنفساء، فلأن الصوم عليهما حرام، والتشبه بالحرام حرام، وأما المريض والمسافر فلأن الرخصة في حقهما باعتبار الحرج، ولو ألزمناهما التشبه لعاد الشيء على موضوعه بالنقض، ولكن لا يأكلون جهراً بل سراً ( وعليهم القضاء، إلا الأخيرين الصبي إذا بلغ والكافر إذا أسـلـم لـعــدم الخطاب في حقهما عند طلوع الفجر بعدم أهليتهما له. (1) أخرجه البخاري في الصوم، باب صوم يوم عاشوراء (۲۰۰۷)، ومسلم في الصيام، باب: مــن أكــل في عاشوراء فليكف بقية يومه (١١٣٥).
Section: It is obligatory to refrain for the rest of the day upon the one whose fast is broken. Refraining for the rest of the day is obligatory upon one whose fast is broken, and upon a menstruating woman and a woman with postpartum bleeding who become pure after dawn, and upon a child who attains puberty and a disbeliever who accepts Islam after dawn, and upon them is qada, except the last two. Section: It is obligatory to refrain for the rest of the day upon the one whose fast is broken. Know that the principle with us is that whoever becomes in some part of the day in a condition that, had he been in it at its beginning, fasting would have been obligatory upon him, then refraining for the rest of the day is obligatory upon him just as the fasting person refrains. And whoever becomes in some part of the day in a condition that, had he been in it at dawn, fasting would not have been obligatory upon him, then refraining is not obligatory upon him. Accordingly, refraining for the rest of the day is obligatory according to the correct view, and it is said: recommended; as an imitation to fulfill the right of the time, as on the day of doubt, to the extent possible, upon one whose fast is broken, even if due to an excuse that then ceased. Because of the Prophet's saying concerning the day of Ashura when its fast was obligatory: 'Indeed, whoever has eaten should not eat for the rest of his day.' (1) And because he is incapable of fasting while being eligible, so refraining is obligatory upon him. And refraining is obligatory upon a menstruating woman and a woman with postpartum bleeding who become pure after dawn, and a traveler who becomes a resident, and a sick person who recovers, and an insane person who regains sanity. And refraining is obligatory upon a child who attains puberty and a disbeliever who accepts Islam after dawn, for what we have mentioned. Contrary to the menstruating woman, woman with postpartum bleeding, sick person, and traveler, where refraining is not obligatory upon them as an imitation when the excuse exists, by agreement. As for the menstruating woman and woman with postpartum bleeding, because fasting is forbidden for them, and imitation of the forbidden is forbidden. As for the sick and the traveler, because the concession is for them due to hardship, and if we obligated them to imitate, it would negate the purpose of the concession. However, they should not eat openly but secretly. And upon them is qada, except the last two: the child when he attains puberty and the disbeliever when he accepts Islam, due to the absence of address to them at dawn, as they were not eligible for it.
Source pages p. 661

Commentary
This section (fasl) covers the obligation of imsak (refraining from eating, drinking, and marital relations) for the rest of the day for those whose fast has been invalidated. The author establishes a principle: if a person is in a state at some point in the day that would have obligated fasting had it been present at dawn, then he must refrain; otherwise, he need not. Using this principle, the author lists several categories. First, those who must refrain: the one whose fast is broken (even by an excuse that then ends), the menstruating woman or woman with postpartum bleeding who become pure after dawn, a traveler who takes up residency, a sick person who recovers, an insane person who regains sanity, a child who reaches puberty, and a disbeliever who accepts Islam after dawn. For all except the child and the disbeliever, qada is also obligatory; the child and disbeliever do not have to make up because they were not addressed by the obligation at dawn. The proof is the hadith of the Prophet on Ashura when fasting was obligatory: 'Whoever has eaten should not eat for the rest of the day.' The author also clarifies that certain people do not have to refrain: the menstruating woman, woman with postpartum bleeding, the sick, and the traveler, because their original state at dawn was a valid excuse. Imitating the fast in these cases would be imitating something forbidden (for the first two) or would undermine the concession (for the latter), but they should eat in private to avoid suspicion.

Key Terms
الإمساك (al-imsak): Refraining from eating, drinking, and sexual intercourse for the remainder of the day out of respect for the time.
قضاء (qada): Making up a missed obligatory fast on a different day.
التشبه (al-tashabbuh): Imitating the fasting person in outward actions (abstaining) even though fasting is not valid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling for a menstruating woman who becomes pure after dawn?
A) She must refrain (imsak) for the rest of the day and make up the fast.
B) She must refrain but does not need to make up the fast.
C) She need not refrain but must make up the fast.
D) She need not refrain and does not need to make up.

Key Takeaway
Imsak is wajib for those who become eligible for fasting during the day; those with a valid excuse from dawn are exempt from imsak but must make up the fast.
