Disliked Acts for the Fasting Person: Tasting, Chewing, and Mastic Gum
Listing and explaining the first three disliked acts for a fasting person.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the disliked acts (tasting, chewing, and mastic gum) for a fasting person, state the general Hanafi ruling, and apply the exceptions when necessity permits.

Core Idea
Tasting and chewing are disliked for a fasting person unless genuine need makes them permissible.

This lesson examines three of the seven disliked acts (makruhat) for a fasting person as outlined in Nur al-Idah: tasting food, chewing without excuse, and chewing mastic gum. The commentary from Imdad al-Fattah explains the reasoning and important exceptions, such as a woman tasting for a hostile husband or a mother chewing for her baby. Understanding these nuances ensures correct application in daily life.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disliked acts
From the passage, list the three acts explicitly stated: tasting (dhawq), chewing without excuse (madgh), and chewing mastic gum (al-ilk).
The matn says 'seven things are disliked' but only three are discussed on this page.
2
Understand the default ruling and rationale
Explain why each act is disliked: tasting exposes the fast to nullification; chewing resembles eating; mastic gum leads to suspicion of breaking the fast and stimulates appetite.
The hadith 'Do not stand in places of suspicion' is cited for mastic gum.
3
Recognize exceptions based on necessity
Identify scenarios where the dislike is lifted: tasting to avoid being cheated in purchase, a woman tasting for a bad-tempered husband, a mother chewing for her baby when no helper is available.
The exception applies only when there is a genuine need; otherwise the default dislike remains.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi difference
Note that Shams al-A'imma al-Hulwani restricted dislike of tasting to obligatory fasts, but the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) applies to all fasts, including supererogatory ones.
The majority view from al-Hidayah, al-Kanz, and Sharh al-Mukhtar is absolute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل فيما يكره للصائم وما لا يكره وما يستحب له
كره للصائم سبعة أشياء: ذوق شيءٍ ومَضَعُهُ، بلا عذرٍ ومَضْعُ العِلْكِ.
«كُرِهَ للصَّائِمِ سبعة أشياء: ذوق شيءٍ) لما فيه من تعريض الـصـوم للفساد، كــذا أطلقه في « الهداية» و«الكنز» وشرح المختار » ، فشمل النفل لما أنه لا يباح فيه الفطـر بـلا عـذر علـى المذهب، ومن قيده بالفرض كشمس الأئمة ( الحلواني»، ونفى كراهة الذوق في النفل، إنما هو على رواية جواز الإفطار في النفل بلا عذر ، (وَ) كُره (مَضْغُهُ بلا عذر كالمرأة إذا وجدت من يمضغ الطعام لصبيها، كمفطرة لحيض وصغر، أما إذا لم تجد بداً منه فلا بأس بمضغها لصيانة الولد، وفي الذخيرة» : من المشايخ من قال في صوم الفرض إنما يكره ذوق شيء إذا كان له منه بد، أما إذا لم يكن له، بأن احتاج إلى شراء مأكول، وخاف أنه إن لم يذق يغبن فيه، أو لا يوافقه لا يـكــره، وفي المحيط » : لا بأس به كيلا يغبن، وفي الذخيرة و التجنيس خلافه، وفيه ذكــر في «فتـاوى النسفي : أن المرأة إذا كان لها زوج سيئ الخلق، يضايقها في ملوحة الطعام وقلة ملحه، يحل لها أن تذوق الطعام لتعرف طعمه؛ دفعاً لأذى الزوج عن نفسها، وإن كان حسن الخلق فلا يحل، كمـا هـو المذكور في الأصل، انتهى. وكذا الأمة كما في شرح المقدسي » و « المجمع » قلت: ويمكن أن يكون الأجير كذلك انتهى. (وَ) كره (مَضْعَ العِلْكِ) الذي لا يصل منه شيء إلى الجوف مع الريـــق، هو المصطكي وقيل : اللبان الذي يقال له الكندر، لأنه يتهم بالإفطار بمضغه سواء فيه المرأة والرجل؛ لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام : « مَنْ كَانَ يُؤْمِنُ بِاللهِ وَاليَومِ الآخِرِ فَلا يَقِفَنَّ مُواقِفَ النُّهَمِ ) وقال الإمام علي له: ( إياك وما يسبق إلى القلوب إنكاره، وإن كان عندك اعتذاره » (۳). ولأنه وإن لم يره أحد يكره أيضاً؛ لأن مضغه يدبغ المعدة ويشهي الطعام ولم يأن له، وإذا لم يأن وقت الاشتهاء فالاشتغال به اشتغال بما لا يفيد، وأما في غير حال الصوم فإنه يكره للرجال، إلا في الخلوة بـعــذر، كذا ذكره « البزدوي » و « المحبوبي»، وقيل: لا يستحب لهم ولا يكره، فهو مباح لهم بخلاف النساء، فإنه يستحب لهن مضغه لأنه سواكهن؛ لقيامه مقام السواك في حقهن لضعف بنيتهن، فقد لا تحتمل السواك فيخشى على اللثة والسن منه، كما في «الفتح». ومضغه يورث هزال الجنين كما في « الدراية ) .
العلك.
(۱) ذكره العجلوني في كشف الخفاء (٣٣٣/٢).
(۲) ذكره المناوي في فيض القدير (۱۱۸/۳)، عن عمر بن عبد العزيز
Section: Regarding what is disliked for the fasting person, what is not disliked, and what is recommended.
Seven things are disliked for the fasting person: tasting something, chewing it without excuse, and chewing mastic gum (al-'ilk).
(Seven things are disliked for the fasting person: tasting something) because it exposes the fast to nullification. This is stated absolutely in al-Hidayah, al-Kanz, and Sharh al-Mukhtar, so it includes supererogatory fasts because breaking it without excuse is not permitted in the madhhab. Whoever restricted it to obligatory fasts, like Shams al-A'imma al-Hulwani, and denied dislike in supererogatory fasts, that is only according to the narration permitting breaking a supererogatory fast without excuse. (And) chewing it without excuse is disliked, like a woman who finds someone to chew food for her child, like a mother weaning due to menstruation or young age. But if she has no alternative, there is no harm in her chewing to preserve the child. In al-Dhakhira: some scholars said in obligatory fasting that tasting something is only disliked if he has an alternative; but if he has no alternative, such as needing to buy food and fears being cheated if he does not taste, or it may not suit him, then it is not disliked. In al-Muhit: there is no harm to avoid being cheated. In al-Dhakhira and al-Tajnis, the opposite is mentioned. In it, it is mentioned in Fatawa al-Nasafi: if a woman has a bad-tempered husband who harasses her about the saltiness of food or lack of salt, it is permissible for her to taste the food to know its flavor, to repel her husband's harm from herself. If he is good-tempered, it is not permissible, as mentioned in the original. End. Similarly for a slave woman as in Sharh al-Maqdisi and al-Majma'. I say: It is possible to extend this to an employee. End. (And) chewing mastic gum (al-'ilk) is disliked, from which nothing reaches the stomach with saliva. It is mastic wax, and it is said: frankincense (kundur), because he is accused of breaking the fast by chewing it, whether man or woman. Because of his statement, peace be upon him: 'Whoever believes in Allah and the Last Day, let him not stand in places of suspicion.' And Imam 'Ali said: 'Beware of what people's hearts reject even if you have an excuse for it.' (2) And because even if no one sees him, it is still disliked because chewing it excites the stomach and provokes appetite when it is not time for eating. If it is not time for craving, then occupying oneself with it is an occupation with what is of no benefit. In other than fasting, it is disliked for men except in private with an excuse, as mentioned by al-Bazdawi and al-Mahbubi. It was said: it is neither recommended nor disliked for them, so it is permissible. For women, it is recommended for them to chew it because it is their siwak, as it takes the place of siwak for them due to their weak constitution; they may not tolerate siwak, and there is fear for the gums and teeth from it, as in al-Fath. Chewing it causes emaciation of the fetus as in al-Diraya. (End of mastic).
Source pages p. 662

Commentary
This passage from Imdad al-Fattah is a commentary (sharh) explaining the disliked acts (makruhat) for a fasting person. The matn states that seven things are disliked, but only three are discussed in detail on this page: (1) tasting food (dhawq), (2) chewing it without excuse, and (3) chewing mastic gum (al-'ilk). The commentary provides the reasoning: tasting exposes the fast to nullification. There is a difference of opinion on whether tasting is disliked in supererogatory fasts; the preferred view in the school is that it is disliked in both obligatory and supererogatory fasts because breaking a supererogatory fast without excuse is not allowed. Chewing food without excuse is disliked, but if there is a genuine need, such as a woman chewing for her baby or a person needing to taste food to avoid being cheated in purchase, then it is not disliked. Detailed exceptions are given for women with difficult husbands. Chewing mastic gum (a hard resin, also called kundur) is disliked because it leads to suspicion of breaking the fast, even if it does not reach the stomach. The hadith warns against standing in places of suspicion. Additionally, it stimulates appetite at a time when eating is not allowed. For non-fasting men, it is disliked except with excuse; for women, it is recommended as a substitute for siwak due to their weaker gums. Some consider it permissible for men. The passage also notes that chewing mastic can cause the fetus to become thin.

Key Terms
الذوق (al-dhawq): Tasting food or drink to test its flavor without swallowing.
المضغ (al-madgh): Chewing food or gum without swallowing, which is disliked if done without excuse.
العلك (al-'ilk): A type of mastic gum (also called kundur) that is chewed but does not dissolve completely.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on a woman chewing a small piece of bread for her baby while she is fasting in Ramadan?
A) It is absolutely disliked (makruh) under all circumstances.
B) It is disliked unless she has no other person to do it, in which case it is permissible.
C) It is permissible (mubah) and recommended.
D) It is haram because it breaks the fast.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, tasting, chewing without excuse, and chewing mastic gum are disliked for a fasting person, but these rulings are suspended in cases of genuine necessity.

Disliked and Non-Disliked Actions for the Fasting Person (Hanafi View)
Listing disliked actions with proofs and exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on seven disliked actions for the fasting person, identify exceptions based on necessity, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on intimate touching.

Core Idea
A fasting person avoids what risks the fast or creates suspicion, but necessity permits tasting or chewing; kissing is allowed if safe from arousal.

This passage from Imdad al-Fattah details which actions are makruh for the fasting person and which are not, with proofs from hadith. It clarifies that while tasting and chewing are generally disliked, exceptions exist for genuine need. It also addresses the controversial issue of intimate contact between spouses, providing the Hanafi position and its proofs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the seven disliked actions
Read the first part of the passage to list the seven actions the author states are disliked: tasting, chewing without excuse, chewing gum, kissing without safety, intimate touching without safety, collecting saliva and swallowing, and anything that weakens like cupping or phlebotomy.
Not all are equally severe; some are makruh tahrimi, others tanzih.
2
Understand the exceptions for tasting and chewing
Note that tasting and chewing are not disliked if there is a genuine need, such as to avoid being cheated in a purchase or to check seasoning for a bad-tempered husband. The passage cites various authorities allowing this.
The exceptions are based on istihsan and necessity (darura).
3
Analyze the ruling on chewing gum (al-‘ilk)
Chewing gum (mastic or frankincense) is disliked due to suspicion of breaking the fast and because it stimulates appetite. A hadith warns against standing in places of accusation. For men it is disliked even outside fasting; for women it is recommended as a substitute for siwak.
The ruling is based on tuhma (suspicion) and lack of benefit.
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute on kissing and touching
The apparent narration (zahir al-riwaya) from Abu Hanifa is that kissing and touching are not disliked if safe from arousal, based on the hadith of Aisha. However, Imam Muhammad and a narration from Abu Hanifa via Hasan dislike severe touching because it rarely avoids temptation.
The mufta bihi is the apparent narration, but caution is advised.
5
List the nine non-disliked actions
The passage explicitly lists nine actions that are not disliked: kissing/touching with safety, oiling mustache, kohl, cupping/phlebotomy that do not weaken, siwak at any time (actually sunnah). The proofs include hadith on siwak and the Prophet's own actions.
Siwak is emphasized as sunnah for the fasting person at the end of the day.

Source Text

فصل فيما يكره للصائم وما لا يكره وما يستحب له كره للصائم سبعة أشياء: ذوق شيء ومضغه بلا عذر ومضع العلك. كره للصائم سبعة أشياء: ذوق شيء لما فيه من تعريض الصوم للفساد، كذا أطلقه في «الهداية» و«الكنز» وشرح المختار، فشمل النفل لما أنه لا يباح فيه الفطر بلا عذر على المذهب، ومن قيده بالفرض كشمس الأئمة الحلواني، ونفى كراهة الذوق في النفل، إنما هو على رواية جواز الإفطار في النفل بلا عذر، وكره مضغه بلا عذر كالمرأة إذا وجدت من يمضغ الطعام لصبيها، كمفطرة لحيض وصغر، أما إذا لم تجد بداً منه فلا بأس بمضغها لصيانة الولد، وفي الذخيرة: من المشايخ من قال في صوم الفرض إنما يكره ذوق شيء إذا كان له منه بد، أما إذا لم يكن له، بأن احتاج إلى شراء مأكول، وخاف أنه إن لم يذق يغبن فيه، أو لا يوافقه لا يكره، وفي المحيط: لا بأس به كيلا يغبن، وفي الذخيرة والتجنيس خلافه، وفيه ذكر في فتاوى النسفي: أن المرأة إذا كان لها زوج سيئ الخلق، يضايقها في ملوحة الطعام وقلة ملحه، يحل لها أن تذوق الطعام لتعرف طعمه؛ دفعاً لأذى الزوج عن نفسها، وإن كان حسن الخلق فلا يحل، كما هو المذكور في الأصل، انتهى. وكذا الأمة كما في شرح المقدسي والمجمع. قلت: ويمكن أن يكون الأجير كذلك انتهى. وكره مضع العلك الذي لا يصل منه شيء إلى الجوف مع الريق، هو المصطكي وقيل: اللبان الذي يقال له الكندر، لأنه يتهم بالإفطار بمضغه سواء فيه المرأة والرجل؛ لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: «من كان يؤمن بالله واليوم الآخر فلا يقفن مواقف التهم» وقال الإمام علي له: «إياك وما يسبق إلى القلوب إنكاره، وإن كان عندك اعتذاره». ولأنه وإن لم يره أحد يكره أيضاً؛ لأن مضغه يدبغ المعدة ويشهي الطعام ولم يأن له، وإذا لم يأن وقت الاشتهاء فالاشتغال به اشتغال بما لا يفيد، وأما في غير حال الصوم فإنه يكره للرجال، إلا في الخلوة بعذر، كذا ذكره البزدوي والمحبوبي، وقيل: لا يستحب لهم ولا يكره، فهو مباح لهم بخلاف النساء، فإنه يستحب لهن مضغه لأنه سواكهن؛ لقيامه مقام السواك في حقهن لضعف بنيتهن، فقد لا تحتمل السواك فيخشى على اللثة والسن منه، كما في الفتح. ومضغه يورث هزال الجنين كما في الدراية.

والقبلة، والمباشرة، إن لم يأمن فيهما على نفسه الإنزال، أو الجماع في ظاهر الرواية، وجمع الريق في الفم، ثم ابتلاعه. وما ظن أنه يضعفه كالفصد والحجامة. وتسعة أشياء لا تكره للصائم: القبلة والمباشرة مع الأمن، ودهن الشارب والكحل والحجامة، والفصد، والسواك آخر النهار، بل هو سنة كأوله. وكره له القبلة، والمباشرة الفاحشة وغيرها، إن لم يأمن فيهما على نفسه الإنزال، أو الجماع في ظاهر الرواية لما فيه من تعريض الصوم على الفساد بعاقبة الفعل، ويكره التقبيل الفاحش، وهو: أن يمضغ شفتيها، كما في الظهيرية. وكره له جمع الريق في الفم قصداً ثم ابتلاعه كما في التاتارخانية. وكره له فعل ما ظن أنه يضعفه عن الصوم، كالفصد والحجامة والعمل الشاق؛ لما فيه من تعريضه للإفساد. وتسعة أشياء لا تكره للصائم وهي وإن علمت بالمفهوم مما سبق، فالتصريح به سائغ لذكر الدليل ولمقام التعليم القبلة والمباشرة مع الأمن من الإنزال والوقاع، لما روي عن عائشة رضي الله عنها أنه عليه الصلاة والسلام: «كان يقبل ويباشر وهو صائم» رواه البخاري ومسلم وهذا ظاهر الرواية، وعن محمد: أنه كره المباشرة الفاحشة، وهي رواية الحسن عن أبي حنيفة لأنها قل ما تخلو عن فتنة، وفي الجوهرة: قيل: إن المباشرة تكره وإن أمن على الصحيح، وهو: أن يمس فرجه فرجها، انتهى. وفي الظهيرية: وعن أبي حنيفة أنه قال: تكره المعانقة والمصافحة، وأنه خلاف المشهور انتهى. ودهن بفتح الدال على أنه مصدر، وبضمها على إقامة اسم العين مقام المصدر، الشارب لأنه ليس فيه شيء مما ينافي الصوم، والكحل «لأنه عليه الصلاة والسلام اكتحل وهو صائم»، والحجامة التي لا تضعفه عن الصوم، وينبغي له أن يؤخرها إلى وقت الغروب، والفصد كالحجامة، وذكر شيخ الإسلام أن شرط الكراهة ضعف يحتاج فيه إلى الفطر كما في التاتارخانية. ولا يكره له السواك آخر النهار، بل هو سنة كأوله؛ لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: «من خير خلال الصائم السواك» ولعموم قوله: «لولا أن أشق على أمتي لأمرتهم بالسواك عند كل صلاة» يدخل في عموم كل صلاة الظهر والعصر والمغرب، للصائم والمفطر.
Section on what is disliked for the fasting person and what is not disliked and what is recommended for him. Seven things are disliked for the fasting person: tasting something, chewing it without an excuse, and chewing gum. It is disliked for the fasting person to taste something because it exposes the fast to corruption. This is stated absolutely in Al-Hidayah, Al-Kanz, and Sharh al-Mukhtar, so it includes supererogatory fasts because it is not permissible to break them without an excuse according to the madhhab. Whoever restricted it to obligatory fasts, like Shams al-A'imma al-Hulwani, and denied dislike in supererogatory fasts, that is only based on the narration allowing breaking a supererogatory fast without an excuse. And it is disliked to chew it without an excuse, like a woman who finds someone to chew food for her child, such as one who breaks her fast due to menstruation or young age. However, if she has no alternative, then there is no harm in her chewing to protect the child. In Al-Dhakhira: Some shaykhs said that tasting is only disliked in an obligatory fast if there is an alternative. If there is no alternative, such as needing to buy food and fearing that if he does not taste it he will be cheated, or it will not suit him, then it is not disliked. In Al-Muhit: There is no harm in it to avoid being cheated. In Al-Dhakhira and Al-Tajnisa it is otherwise. In it, it is mentioned in Fatawa al-Nasafi: If a woman has a bad-tempered husband who harasses her about the saltiness or lack of salt in the food, it is permissible for her to taste the food to know its flavor, to repel harm from her husband. If he is good-tempered, it is not permissible, as mentioned in the original text. End quote. Similarly, a female slave, as in Sharh al-Maqdisi and al-Majma. I say: It is possible that a hired worker is likewise. End quote. And it is disliked to chew gum which does not allow anything to reach the stomach with saliva. It is mastic, and it is said: frankincense which is called luban (olibanum), because he is accused of breaking the fast by chewing it, equally for women and men; due to his saying, peace be upon him: 'Whoever believes in Allah and the Last Day, let him not stand in places of accusation.' And Imam Ali said: 'Beware of what precedent goes to hearts as its denial, even if you have an excuse for it.' And because even if no one sees him it is also disliked, because its chewing tans the stomach and appetizes food when it is not time for it. And when it is not time for appetite, engaging in it is engagement in what is useless. As for outside fasting, it is disliked for men, except in private with an excuse. Thus mentioned by al-Bazdawi and al-Mahbubi. It is said: It is not recommended for them nor disliked, so it is permissible for them, unlike women, for whom it is recommended to chew it because it is their siwak, as it serves the role of siwak for them due to their weak constitution. They may not tolerate siwak for fear of the gums and teeth, as in al-Fath. And its chewing causes emaciation of the fetus, as in al-Diraya.

And kissing, and touching, if he does not feel safe from emission or intercourse in the apparent narration, and collecting saliva in the mouth then swallowing it. And what he thinks weakens him, like phlebotomy and cupping. And nine things are not disliked for the fasting person: kissing and touching with safety, oiling the mustache, kohl, cupping, phlebotomy, and siwak at the end of the day, rather it is sunnah like at its beginning. And it is disliked for him kissing and intimate touching, whether severe or not, if he does not feel safe from emission or intercourse in the apparent narration, because it exposes the fast to corruption through the consequence of the act. And severe kissing is disliked, which is to suck her lips, as in al-Zahiriyya. And it is disliked for him to collect saliva in his mouth intentionally then swallow it, as in al-Tatarkhaniyya. And it is disliked for him to do what he thinks weakens him for the fast, like phlebotomy, cupping, and hard work, because it exposes it to corruption. And nine things are not disliked for the fasting person, and even if they are known from the previous by implication, stating them explicitly is permissible for the sake of mentioning evidence and for educational purposes: kissing and touching with safety from emission and intercourse, due to what is narrated from Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her) that the Prophet (peace be upon him) used to kiss and touch while fasting, narrated by Bukhari and Muslim, and this is the apparent narration. And from Muhammad: that he disliked severe intimate touching, and this is a narration from Hasan from Abu Hanifa because it rarely remains free from temptation. In al-Jawhara: It is said that intimate touching is disliked even with safety on the correct view, which is that he touches his private part to her private part. End quote. In al-Zahiriyya: From Abu Hanifa that he said: Embracing and handshaking are disliked, and that is contrary to the famous view. End quote. And oiling (with fatha on the dal as a masdar, and with damma as substituting the noun of object for the masdar) the mustache, because there is nothing in it that contradicts fasting. And kohl, because the Prophet (peace be upon him) applied kohl while fasting. And cupping that does not weaken him for the fast, and he should delay it until sunset. And phlebotomy is like cupping. And Shaykh al-Islam mentioned that the condition for dislike is weakness that requires breaking the fast, as in al-Tatarkhaniyya. And siwak at the end of the day is not disliked for him, rather it is sunnah like at its beginning, due to his saying: 'Among the best characteristics of a fasting person is siwak' and due to the generality of his saying: 'Were it not that I would burden my nation, I would have commanded them to use siwak at every prayer.' The generality includes every prayer: Zuhr, Asr, and Maghrib, for the fasting and the non-fasting.
Source pages p. 662

Commentary
This passage from Imdad al-Fattah is a commentary on a matn that categorizes actions for a fasting person into disliked and not disliked. The author begins by stating that seven things are disliked (makruh) for the fasting person: tasting food, chewing without excuse, and chewing gum (al-‘ilk). He then expands on each.

For tasting (dhawq), the reason is that it risks nullifying the fast. The ruling is general for both obligatory and supererogatory fasts according to the majority, because even in a supererogatory fast, breaking it without excuse is not permissible in the madhhab. However, Shams al-A'imma al-Hulwani restricted the dislike to obligatory fasts, based on a narration that allows breaking a supererogatory fast without excuse. The author then discusses exceptions: tasting is not disliked if there is a genuine need, such as when buying food and fearing being cheated, or when a woman has a harsh husband who complains about the food's seasoning. In such cases, it becomes permissible (halal) to taste. Similarly, a female slave or a hired worker may have similar allowances.

For chewing (madgh), the same principle applies: it is disliked without excuse. The example given is a woman chewing food for her child; if she has no alternative, it is permissible. The author cites various fiqh works (Al-Dhakhira, Al-Muhit, etc.) showing differences of opinion, but the relied-upon position allows it when necessary.

For chewing gum (madgh al-‘ilk), the gum is defined as mastic or frankincense (luban). The dislike is due to suspicion (tuhma) of breaking the fast, even if nothing reaches the stomach. A hadith is quoted: "Whoever believes in Allah and the Last Day, let him not stand in places of accusation." Also, the act is pointless as it stimulates appetite when it is not time to eat. For men, it is disliked even outside of fasting except in private with an excuse; for women, it is recommended because it serves as a substitute for siwak (toothstick) due to their delicate gums.

The author then lists additional disliked actions: kissing (al-qablah) and intimate touching (al-mubasharah) if one does not feel safe from arousal, as well as collecting saliva and swallowing it, and anything that weakens one like phlebotomy (fasd) or cupping (hijamah). These are disliked because they risk corrupting the fast.

Then nine things are listed as not disliked: kissing and touching with safety (i.e., without fear of arousal), oiling the mustache, applying kohl, cupping (if it does not weaken), phlebotomy, and using siwak at the end of the day (which is actually recommended). The author provides proofs: the hadith of Aisha that the Prophet kissed and touched while fasting (Bukhari, Muslim) establishes permissibility of kissing when safe. However, there is an intra-Hanafi difference: Imam Muhammad dislikes severe touching, and a narration from Abu Hanifa (via Hasan) also dislikes it because it rarely remains free from temptation. The author reconciles that the apparent narration (zahir al-riwaya) is that it is not disliked if safe. For siwak, hadith states it is from the best characteristics of the fasting person and that siwak at every prayer is recommended, which applies to Zuhr, Asr, and Maghrib for both fasting and non-fasting persons.

The commentary emphasizes that the rulings are based on the principle of avoiding what risks the fast or creates suspicion, with flexibility for genuine need. The Hanafi school uses istihsan (juristic preference) to allow exceptions, such as tasting to avoid being cheated.

Key Terms
علك (‘ilk): Chewing gum that does not dissolve or reach the stomach; specifically mastic or frankincense
ذوق (dhawq): Tasting food with the tongue without swallowing
مباشرة (mubasharah): Skin-to-skin contact between spouses, typically intimate touching
قبلة (qablah): Kissing, especially of the lips
فصد (fasd): Phlebotomy; opening a vein to draw blood for medical purposes
حجامة (hijamah): Cupping; a therapeutic technique involving suction on the skin
سواك (siwak): A tooth-cleaning stick, typically from the arak tree

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on a woman tasting the food she is cooking if her husband has a bad temper and complains about the seasoning?
A) It is forbidden (haram) for her to taste the food.
B) It is permissible (halal) for her to taste the food to save herself from his harm.
C) It is disliked (makruh) even if she has no alternative.
D) It is recommended (sunnah) for her to taste the food.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits tasting and chewing food for a fasting person when necessity arises, and considers kissing and intimate touching permissible if safe from arousal, based on the clear hadith of Aisha.

Kissing and Touching While Fasting: When Are They Disliked?
Listing and explaining the ruling of kissing/touching with condition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition under which kissing and touching are disliked for a fasting person and explain the Hanafi position including the intra-madhhab dispute.

Core Idea
Kissing while fasting is disliked only if it risks arousal; otherwise it is permitted.

In this lesson, we examine the first category of makruhat mentioned in the text: kissing and touching (al-qublah wa al-mubasharah). The Hanafi school distinguishes between safe and unsafe intimacy, a nuance reflected in the phrase 'if he is not safe from emission or intercourse.' We will dissect the ruling, its condition, and the internal Hanafi difference of opinion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the acts and the ruling
The acts are kissing and touching (al-qublah wa al-mubasharah). The ruling is karahah (dislike) under a specific condition.
The condition is key: 'if he is not safe from emission or intercourse.'
2
Understand the condition
The dislike applies only if the fasting person fears that the act will lead to seminal emission or intercourse. If he is safe (aman), the act is not disliked according to the Zahir al-Riwayah.
This is based on the Hadith of Aisha (ra) that the Prophet ﷺ would kiss and touch while fasting.
3
Distinguish between ordinary and immodest kissing
The text further defines 'immodest kissing' (al-taqbil al-fahish) as chewing the lips, which is more severely disliked regardless of safety.
This is from al-Zahiriya.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Imam Muhammad and a narration from Abu Hanifa consider even safe touching as disliked due to the likelihood of fitna. However, the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) from Zahir al-Riwayah is that it is not disliked if safe.
The thamarah: practical difference in ruling for a safe kiss.
5
Identify the underlying illa
The rationale (illa) is that these actions 'expose the fast to corruption' (تعريض الصوم على الفساد). This is a rational proof (dalil aqli) based on blocking means to nullification.
The illa is the risk, not certainty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والقُبْلَةُ، والمباشَرَةُ، إن لم يأمن فيهما على نفسِهِ الإنزال، أوِ الجَمَاعَ في ظاهر الرواية، وجمع الريق في الفم، ثم ابتلاعُهُ. وما ظَنَّ أنَّه يُضْعِفُهُ كالفَصْدِ والحِجَامَةِ. (و) كره له ( القُبْلَةُ، والمباشَرَةُ) الفاحشة وغيرها، (إن لم يأمن فيهما على نفسه الإنزال، أو الجماع في ظاهر الرّواية) لما فيه من تعريض الصوم على الفساد بعاقبة الفعل، ويكــره التقبيل الفاحش، وهـو : أن يمضغ شفتيها، كما في ( الظهيرية) (و) كره له ( جمعُ الرّيق في الفم) قصداً (ثم ابتلاعه) كما في التاتارخانية» (وَ) كره له فعل ( ما ظَنَّ أنَّه يُضْعِفُهُ عن الصوم، كالفَصْدِ والحِجَامَةِ) والعمل الشاق؛ لما فيه من تعريضه للإفساد.
And [disliked for him are] kissing and touching if he is not safe from emission in both, or intercourse according to the apparent narration, and collecting saliva in the mouth then swallowing it, and what he thinks will weaken him like phlebotomy and cupping. And it is disliked for him (kissing and touching) in an immodest or other manner (if he is not safe from emission or intercourse according to the apparent narration) because it exposes the fast to corruption by the consequence of the act. And immodest kissing is disliked, which is to chew her lips, as in al-Zahiriya. And it is disliked for him (collecting saliva in the mouth intentionally then swallowing it) as in al-Tatarkhaniyya. And it is disliked for him to do (what he thinks will weaken him for fasting, like phlebotomy and cupping) and hard work, because it exposes it to nullification.
Source pages p. 663

Commentary
This passage expands upon the ruling (hukm) of disliked actions (makruhat) for a fasting person. The author first quotes the matn listing the disliked actions: kissing and touching when one is not safe from seminal emission or intercourse, collecting saliva in the mouth and swallowing it, and actions that weaken the body like phlebotomy (fasad) and cupping (hijama). He then comments on each. The key ruling is that these actions are makruh tahrimi (close to haram) because they risk invalidating the fast, not because they directly nullify it. The commentator clarifies that 'immodest kissing' (taqbil fahish) is specifically defined as chewing the partner's lips. The mention of 'apparent narration' (zahir al-riwaya) indicates this is the relied-upon position from Imam Muhammad's six books. The underlying rationale (illa) is that these actions expose the fast to potential corruption. This is a case of talil, where the legal cause is the risk of nullification.

Key Terms
القُبْلَةُ (al-qublah): Kissing; in this context, referring to kissing one's spouse while fasting.
المباشَرَةُ (al-mubasharah): Touching skin-to-skin; here meaning physical intimacy short of intercourse.
الفاحشة (al-fahishah): Immodest or lewd; here specifically applied to kissing that involves chewing lips, indicating heightened sensuality.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Always disliked
B) Only if it leads to seminal emission or intercourse
C) Only if the person is not fasting
D) Only if it is immodest

Key Takeaway
Kissing and touching are only disliked for a fasting person if there is a real risk of arousal leading to nullification; otherwise they are permissible.

Additional Makruhat: Collecting Saliva and Weakening Actions
Listing additional makruhat with rational proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the additional makruhat for a fasting person (collecting saliva, weakening actions) and explain their illa.

Core Idea
Collecting saliva and deliberately swallowing it, or any action that weakens the body, is disliked because it risks invalidating the fast.

The second part of the passage addresses two more categories of disliked actions: intentionally collecting saliva and swallowing it, and acts that weaken the body like phlebotomy and cupping. These are disliked because they threaten the integrity of the fast, either by ingesting something (saliva) or by making completion difficult. We will examine the legal reasoning and the subjective element of 'what one thinks will weaken him.'

Guided Path
1
Identify the actions
The actions are: (1) collecting saliva in the mouth intentionally and then swallowing it (jamm al-riq wa bul'uhu), and (2) doing what one thinks will weaken him, such as phlebotomy (fasd) and cupping (hijama), or hard physical work.
These are additional to the previously mentioned kissing/touching.
2
Understand the ruling
Both are makruh. For saliva collection, the dislike is because it resembles eating/drinking intentionally (though it is not invalidating by itself). For weakening actions, the dislike is because they expose the fast to nullification (تعريضه للإفساد).
The text says 'because it exposes it to nullification.'
3
Note the subjective condition
The ruling for weakening actions depends on the person's own perception: 'what he thinks will weaken him' (ما ظن أنه يضعفه). This is a subjective standard based on the individual's condition.
If a person is strong and knows it won't weaken him, the act is not disliked.
4
Identify the illa
The illa (cause) is the risk of nullifying the fast. Both actions either introduce something into the body (saliva) or reduce strength to the point where one might break the fast.
This is a rational proof (dalil aqli): preventing harm.
5
Distinguish from invalidating actions
Note that these acts are disliked but do not invalidate the fast if performed. Only if they lead to actual nullification (e.g., swallowing water or breaking the fast due to weakness) does the fast become invalid.
The karahah is tahrimi (close to haram) because of the risk.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والقُبْلَةُ، والمباشَرَةُ، إن لم يأمن فيهما على نفسِهِ الإنزال، أوِ الجَمَاعَ في ظاهر الرواية، وجمع الريق في الفم، ثم ابتلاعُهُ. وما ظَنَّ أنَّه يُضْعِفُهُ كالفَصْدِ والحِجَامَةِ. (و) كره له ( القُبْلَةُ، والمباشَرَةُ) الفاحشة وغيرها، (إن لم يأمن فيهما على نفسه الإنزال، أو الجماع في ظاهر الرّواية) لما فيه من تعريض الصوم على الفساد بعاقبة الفعل، ويكــره التقبيل الفاحش، وهـو : أن يمضغ شفتيها، كما في ( الظهيرية) (و) كره له ( جمعُ الرّيق في الفم) قصداً (ثم ابتلاعه) كما في التاتارخانية» (وَ) كره له فعل ( ما ظَنَّ أنَّه يُضْعِفُهُ عن الصوم، كالفَصْدِ والحِجَامَةِ) والعمل الشاق؛ لما فيه من تعريضه للإفساد.
And [disliked for him are] kissing and touching if he is not safe from emission in both, or intercourse according to the apparent narration, and collecting saliva in the mouth then swallowing it, and what he thinks will weaken him like phlebotomy and cupping. And it is disliked for him (kissing and touching) in an immodest or other manner (if he is not safe from emission or intercourse according to the apparent narration) because it exposes the fast to corruption by the consequence of the act. And immodest kissing is disliked, which is to chew her lips, as in al-Zahiriya. And it is disliked for him (collecting saliva in the mouth intentionally then swallowing it) as in al-Tatarkhaniyya. And it is disliked for him to do (what he thinks will weaken him for fasting, like phlebotomy and cupping) and hard work, because it exposes it to nullification.
Source pages p. 663

Commentary
This passage expands upon the ruling (hukm) of disliked actions (makruhat) for a fasting person. The author first quotes the matn listing the disliked actions: kissing and touching when one is not safe from seminal emission or intercourse, collecting saliva in the mouth and swallowing it, and actions that weaken the body like phlebotomy (fasad) and cupping (hijama). He then comments on each. The key ruling is that these actions are makruh tahrimi (close to haram) because they risk invalidating the fast, not because they directly nullify it. The commentator clarifies that 'immodest kissing' (taqbil fahish) is specifically defined as chewing the partner's lips. The mention of 'apparent narration' (zahir al-riwaya) indicates this is the relied-upon position from Imam Muhammad's six books. The underlying rationale (illa) is that these actions expose the fast to potential corruption. This is a case of talil, where the legal cause is the risk of nullification.

Key Terms
جمع الريق (jamm al-riq): Collecting saliva in the mouth intentionally.
الفَصْدُ (al-fasd): Phlebotomy; a medical procedure of drawing blood.
الحِجَامَةُ (al-hijamah): Cupping; a therapeutic practice of creating suction on the skin.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the reason (illa) given in the text for disliking actions like cupping and phlebotomy for a fasting person?
A) They are physically painful
B) They expose the fast to nullification
C) They are prohibited in the Quran
D) They cause bleeding

Key Takeaway
Collecting and swallowing saliva, and actions that one believes will weaken him, are disliked because they risk nullifying the fast, but they do not invalidate it by themselves.

Nine Permissible Acts for the Fasting Person (Rulings and Proofs)
Listing nine actions not disliked with proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the nine actions that are not disliked for a fasting person and identify the scriptural proof (dalil) for at least three of them.

Core Idea
Fasting does not prohibit many normal actions; the Prophet's example establishes permissibility unless evidence shows otherwise.

In this passage, the author enumerates nine actions that are not disliked (makruh) for the fasting person, each supported by specific proofs from the Sunnah. Understanding these rulings helps a fasting person avoid unnecessary restrictions. This lesson will guide you through each action and its evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the nine actions
Read through the Arabic text and translation to list all nine actions the author mentions as not disliked. Write them down in order.
The actions are: 1) kissing, 2) touching, 3) oiling the mustache, 4) applying kohl, 5) cupping (that does not weaken), 6) phlebotomy, 7) siwak at the end of the day, 8) rinsing mouth and nose (without wudu), 9) bathing and wrapping in a wet cloth.
2
For each action, locate its proof
The author provides a hadith or reasoning for most actions. Identify which actions have explicit hadith evidence and which are supported by analogy or absence of harm.
Kissing/touching: hadith from Aisha (Bukhari, Muslim). Kohl: hadith that the Prophet applied kohl while fasting. Siwak: hadith 'from the best characteristics' and the generality of siwak at every prayer. Others are based on lack of conflict with fasting.
3
Note the ruling classification
The author states these actions are 'not disliked' (la yukrah). Some, like siwak, are explicitly described as sunnah. Understand that the default is permissibility (ibaha) unless there is evidence of dislike or prohibition.
4
Recognize the educational purpose
The author adds 'even if known by implication' to highlight that this explicit listing is for teaching and proof-citation.
This is an important method in fiqh texts: explicit mention for educational clarity.
5
Apply to daily practice
Consider a scenario where a fasting person wishes to use siwak in the afternoon or apply kohl. Based on this lesson, you can confirm these are not only permissible but sunnah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتسعة أشياء لا تُكره للصائم) وهي وإن علمت بالمفهوم مما سبق، فالتصريح به سائغ لذكر الدليل ولمقام التعليم (القُبْلَةُ والمباشرة مع الأمن) من الإنزال والوقاع، لما روي عن عائشة أنه عليه الصلاة والسلام: ( كَانَ يُقَبل وَيُبَاشِرْ وَهُوَ صائِم ) رواه البخاري ومسلم وهذا ظاهر الرواية، وعن محمد: أنه كره المباشرة الفاحشة، وهي رواية الحسن عن أبي حنيفة لأنـها قـلّ ما تخلوا عن فتنة، وفي «الجوهرة» : قيل: إن المباشرة تكره وإن أمن على الصحيح، وهو: أن يمس فرجـــه فرجها، انتهى. وفي ( الظهيرية » : وعن أبي حنيفة أنه قال: تكره المعانقة والمصافحة، وأنـه خـلاف المشهور انتهى. ( ودَهْن) بفتح الدال على أنه مصدر، وبضمها على إقامة اســم الـعـيـن مـقـام المصدر، ( الشارب) لأنه ليس فيه شيء مما ينافي الصوم، ( والكُحْلُ ) « لأنه عليه الصلاة والسلام اكتحل وهـو صائم ) ، ( والحِجَامَةُ) [ التي لا تضعفه ) عن الصوم، وينبغي له أن يؤخرها إلى وقت الغروب، (والفَصْدُ) كالحجامة، وذكر شيخ الإسلام أن شرط الكراهة ضعف يحتاج فيه إلى الفطر كما في التاتارخانية». (وَ) لا يكره لــه (السواك آخرَ النَّهارِ، بل هو سُنَّةٌ كأَولِهِ)؛ لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: ( مِنْ خَيْرِ خِلالِ الصَّائِمِ السواك ) ولعموم قوله : ( لولا أنْ أشُقَّ عَلَى أُمَّتِي لأمَرْتَهُمْ بِالسَّواكِ عِنْدَ كُلَّ صَلاةٍ ) يدخل في عموم كل صلاة الظهر والعصر والمغرب، للصائم والمفطر، ولو كان رطباً، أو مبلولاً بالماء، والمضمضة، والاستنشاق، لغير وضوء، والاغتسال، والتَّلَفُفُ بثوب مُبْتَل للتبرُّدِ على المفتى به.
And nine things are not disliked for the fasting person (and even if known by implication from what preceded, explicit mention is permissible for mentioning the proof and for educational purposes): kissing and touching with safety from emission and intercourse, based on what is narrated from Aisha that the Prophet (peace be upon him) would kiss and touch while fasting—reported by Bukhari and Muslim; this is the apparent narration. And from Muhammad that he disliked immodest touching, and it is the narration of Hasan from Abu Hanifa because it rarely is free from fitna. And in al-Jawhara: it is said that touching is disliked even if safe according to the correct view, which is that he touches her private part with his private part—end. And in al-Zahiriyya: from Abu Hanifa that he said hugging and handshaking are disliked, and it is contrary to the famous—end. And oiling the mustache (with fath of the dal as a masdar, and with damm as substitution of the noun of entity for the masdar) because there is nothing in it contrary to fasting. And kohl because the Prophet applied kohl while fasting. And cupping that does not weaken him for fasting, and he should delay it until sunset. And phlebotomy like cupping, and Shaykh al-Islam mentioned that the condition for dislike is weakness that requires breaking the fast, as in al-Tatarkhaniyya. And it is not disliked for him siwak at the end of the day, rather it is sunnah like at its beginning, due to his saying: 'Among the best characteristics of the fasting person is siwak,' and due to the generality of his saying: 'Were it not that I would burden my ummah, I would have commanded them to use siwak at every prayer'—it enters the generality of every prayer of zuhr, asr, and maghrib for the fasting and the non-fasting. And if it is wet or moistened with water. And rinsing the mouth and sniffing water for non-wudu, and bathing, and wrapping oneself in a wet cloth for cooling, according to the mufta bihi.
Source pages p. 663

Commentary
This passage lists nine actions that are not disliked (makruh) for a fasting person, providing textual proofs for each. The author begins by noting that these are implicit from the previous discussion but explicit mention is useful for education. The nine items are: 1) Kissing and touching when safe from arousal or intercourse—supported by the hadith of Aisha (ra) and this is the Zahir al-Riwayah. An intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf is noted: Imam Muhammad and a narration from Abu Hanifa dislike immodest touching even with safety. 2) Oiling the mustache—no food enters the stomach. 3) Kohl—the Prophet did it while fasting. 4) Cupping that does not weaken—if it weakens, it is disliked. 5) Phlebotomy—same as cupping. 6) Siwak at the end of the day—it is sunnah even for the fasting, based on hadith. 7) Rinsing mouth and nose for non-wudu purposes. 8) Bathing. 9) Wrapping in a wet cloth for cooling—this is the mufta bihi (Abu Yusuf's view). Abu Hanifa disliked it because it shows impatience. The passage thus clarifies that many common practices are not only permissible but sometimes recommended for the fasting person.

Key Terms
السواك (al-siwak): A tooth-cleaning stick; recommended at all times, including for the fasting person at the end of the day.
الحجامة (al-hijama): Cupping therapy; not disliked for the fasting person if it does not weaken him.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT among the nine actions listed as not disliked for the fasting person?
A) Kissing with safety
B) Applying kohl
C) Swallowing saliva intentionally
D) Bathing

Key Takeaway
The nine actions demonstrate that fasting does not require abstaining from many normal activities; the Prophet's practice provides the basis for permissibility.

Intra-Hanafi Differences on Permissibilities: Kissing, Hugging, and Wet Cloth
Intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on kissing, hugging, and wet cloth

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Imam Muhammad on kissing, hugging, and wrapping in wet cloth for the fasting person, and explain which is the mufta bihi.

Core Idea
Even within the Hanafi school, there are differences on some fasting permissibilities; the relied-upon position often follows Abu Yusuf's more lenient view.

While the nine actions are generally not disliked, the author notes internal Hanafi disagreements on kissing/touching (with safety), hugging, and wrapping in a wet cloth. Understanding these nuances is key for a mufti to issue correct fatwas.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed actions
From the passage, find the actions that have intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: kissing/touching, hugging/handshaking, and wrapping in a wet cloth.
2
State the position of each imam for each action
For kissing/touching: Zahir al-Riwayah (not disliked) vs. Imam Muhammad and a narration of Abu Hanifa (disliked if immodest). For hugging: Abu Hanifa disliked it, but the famous view is otherwise. For wet cloth: Abu Hanifa disliked it; Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad allowed it.
Note the sources: 'al-Jawhara', 'al-Zahiriyya'.
3
Determine the mufta bihi (relied-upon position)
The author indicates that for wet cloth, the mufta bihi is permissibility. For kissing/touching, the Zahir al-Riwayah (not disliked) is the predominant view.
The tarjih authority is the commenter's statement 'ala al-mufta bihi'.
4
Analyze the reasoning behind the difference
Those who dislike it argue that such actions rarely avoid fitna (arousal), while others rely on the literal permissibility from the Prophet's practice.
This reflects the broader Hanafi principle of sadd al-dhara'i' (blocking means to evil) versus the general permissibility of actions.

The Three Mustahabbat of Fasting: Suhoor, Delaying, and Hastening Iftar
Listing three recommended actions for the fasting person

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three recommended actions for a fasting person according to the Hanafi school and identify the evidence for each.

Core Idea
Three sunnah practices strengthen the fast: suhoor, delaying it, and hastening iftar.

The fasting person is encouraged to go beyond the obligatory elements and embrace recommended acts that perfect the fast. This lesson focuses on three such practices derived from the Sunnah: eating the pre-dawn meal (suhoor), delaying it until near dawn, and breaking the fast promptly after sunset. These actions carry spiritual blessing and are part of the character of the messengers.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three mustahabbat
From the passage, list the three recommended actions: suhoor, delaying suhoor, and hastening iftar.
The text explicitly lists these three after the phrase 'ويُستحب له ثلاثة أشياء'.
2
Locate the dalil for each
For suhoor, the hadith 'تَسَحَرُوا فَإِنَّ فِي السُّحُورِ بَرَكَةٌ'. For delaying suhoor and hastening iftar, the hadith about the character of the messengers. The passage also cites the blessing of suhoor even with water.
The proofs are from Bukhari, Muslim, Tabarani, and Ahmad.
3
Note the exception for cloudy days
Hastening iftar is only on clear days; on cloudy days one delays to be certain of sunset, preserving the fast from inadvertent breaking.
This shows the principle of ihtiyat (caution) in ibadah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويُستحب له ثلاثة أشياء: السحور. وتأخيره. وتعجيلُ الفِطْرِ في غير يوم غيم. ولقوله : « صلاة : اكِ أفْضَلُ مِنْ سَبْعِينَ صَلَاةٍ بِلا سِوَاكِ ) فهذه النكرة وإن كانت في الإثبات بسوا كما تعم، لوصفها بصفة عامة، فيصدق على عصر الصائم إذا استاك فيه أنها صلاة أفضل من سبعين : يصدق على عصر المفطر، كذا في «الفتح» وفي ( كفاية المجيب». روي عن عائشة أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم: كَانَ يَسْتَاكُ أَوَّلَ النَّهَارِ وَآخِرَهُ وهُوَ صَائِمٌ كذا في ( الكفاية شرح الهداية»، (و) لا يكره لـه السواك و (لو كان رَطْبَاً) أي: أخضر (أو مبلولاً بالماء ) لإطلاق ما روينا. (و) لا يكره لــه (المضمضة والاستنشاق)، وقد فعلهما (لغير وضوء. و) لا (الاغتسال. و) لا (التَّلَفْفُ بشوب مبتل)، قصد ذلك (للتبرُّدِ) ودفع الحر على المفتى به ، وهو قول أبي يوسف لأن النبي : « صب عَلَى رَأْسِهِ المَاءِ وَهُوَ صَائِمٌ مِنَ العَطَشِ أَوْ مِنَ الحَرِّ » رواه أبو داود، وكان ابن عمر يبــل الثـوب ويلفه عليه وهو صائم ، ولأن هذه الأشياء بها عون على العبادة، ودفع للضجر الطبيعي، وكرهها أبو حنيفة لما فيه من إظهار الضجر في إقامة العبادة، كما في ( البرهان». (ويُستحب له ثلاثة أشياء: السحور) لقوله : ( تَسَحَرُوا فَإِنَّ فِي السُّحُورِ بَرَكَةٌ )، قيل المراد بالبركة حصول التقوي به، أو المراد زيادة الثواب ولا منافاة، فليكن المراد من البركة جميعها، كما في «الفتح» وينبغي أن لا يكثر فيه بما لا يبقى معه إحساس بأثر الصوم؛ لإخلائه عن المراد ) كما يفعله المترفون. (و) يستحب ( تأخيره) أي: السحور، لقوله صلى الله عليه وسلم : « ثَلاثُ مِنْ أَخْلاقِ المُرْسَلِينَ: تَعْجِيلُ الإِفْطَارِ وَتَأْخِيرُ السَّحورِ وَوَضَعَ اليَمِينِ عَلَى الشَّمَالِ فِي الصَّلاةِ » رواه الطبراني، (وتعجيل الفطر) لما روينا، وهذا ( فـي غـيـر يـوم غـيـم) وفي الغيم يحتاط حفظاً للصوم عن الإفساد، فقد يفسد بظن الغروب لغيم، والتعجيل المستحب قبل استفحال النجوم، ذكره « قاضي خان ) في شرح الجامع الصغير»، وظاهر الحديث يـفـيـد حصـول البركة ولو بالماء في السحور. قال : « السَّحورُ بَرَكَةٌ فَلَا تَدَعُوهُ، وَلَوْ أَنْ يَجْرَعَ أَحَدُكُمْ جُرْعَةَ مَاءٍ فَإِنَّ اللَّهَ وَمَلائِكَتَهُ يُصَلُّونَ عَلَى المُتَسَحْرِينَ ) رواه أحمد كذا في « البحر ».
And three things are recommended for him: suhoor (pre-dawn meal), delaying it, and hastening iftar (breaking the fast) on non-cloudy days. And due to his saying: 'A prayer in which siwak is used is better than seventy prayers without siwak'—so this indefinite noun in the positive, despite being general, is qualified by a general attribute, so it applies to the afternoon prayer of a fasting person if he uses siwak in it, and to that of a non-fasting person, as in al-Fath and Kifayat al-Mujib. It is narrated from Aisha that the Prophet (peace be upon him) would use siwak at the beginning and end of the day while fasting, as in al-Kifaya Sharh al-Hidaya. And it is not disliked for him siwak even if it is wet, i.e., green, or moistened with water, due to the generality of what we narrated. And it is not disliked for him rinsing the mouth and snuffing water for non-wudu, nor bathing, nor wrapping in a wet cloth for cooling, to ward off heat, according to the mufta bihi, and it is the saying of Abu Yusuf, because the Prophet poured water on his head while fasting from thirst or heat—narrated by Abu Dawud—and Ibn Umar would wet a cloth and wrap it on while fasting. And because these things help in worship and ward off natural annoyance, but Abu Hanifa disliked them because they show annoyance in performing worship, as in al-Burhan. (And three things are recommended for him: suhoor, due to his saying: 'Take suhoor, for in suhoor there is blessing.' It is said the meaning of blessing is strengthening, or an increase in reward—no contradiction, so the meaning of blessing should be all of it, as in al-Fath. And he should not eat so much that he no longer feels the effect of fasting, to avoid missing the purpose, as the extravagant do. And delaying it (suhoor) is recommended, due to his saying: 'Three are from the character of the messengers: hastening iftar, delaying suhoor, and placing the right hand over the left in prayer'—narrated by al-Tabarani. And hastening iftar (on non-cloudy days); on cloudy days one should be cautious to preserve the fast from corruption, because it may be corrupted by assuming sunset due to clouds. The recommended haste is before the stars become numerous, as mentioned by Qadi Khan in Sharh al-Jami' al-Saghir. The apparent meaning of the hadith indicates blessing even if only with water in suhoor. He said: 'Suhoor is a blessing, so do not leave it, even if one of you takes a gulp of water, for Allah and His angels send blessings upon those who take suhoor'—narrated by Ahmad, as in al-Bahr.
Source pages p. 664

Commentary
This passage concludes the discussion of recommended actions (mustahabbat) for the fasting person. Three actions are recommended: (1) Suhoor (the pre-dawn meal), supported by the hadith 'Take suhoor, for in suhoor there is blessing.' The commentator notes that blessing can mean physical strength or spiritual reward, and one should not eat excessively. (2) Delaying suhoor, supported by the hadith that it is from the character of the messengers. (3) Hastening iftar, i.e., breaking the fast promptly after sunset, except on cloudy days where one should delay to be certain of sunset. The commentator also repeats the ruling on siwak and cloth from the previous discussion, reiterating the mufta bihi. The passage thus provides practical guidance for the fasting person to maximize spiritual benefit and avoid unnecessary hardship.

Key Terms
السحور (al-suhoor): The pre-dawn meal taken with the intention of fasting; strongly recommended.
تعجيل الفطر (ta'jil al-fitr): Hastening to break the fast immediately after confirmed sunset.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on suhoor according to the Hanafi school?
A) Fard
B) Wajib
C) Sunnah muakkadah
D) Mubah

Key Takeaway
Suhoor, delaying it, and hastening iftar are sunnah muakkadah that bring blessing and follow the prophetic practice.

Sawm — Fasting
beginner

Permissibility of Wet Miswak, Rinsing, Bathing, and Wet Cloth While Fasting
Permissibility of actions involving moisture for the fasting person with proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the mufta bihi ruling for a fasting person using a wet miswak, rinsing the mouth, bathing, or wrapping in a wet cloth, and identify the primary evidence from the Hadith and practice of the Companions.

Core Idea
Using a wet miswak, rinsing, bathing, or a wet cloth to cool down is permissible for the fasting person according to the relied-upon fatwa.

Fasting does not require complete dryness; the body may still need cooling or oral hygiene. This passage from Imdad al-Fattah clarifies that using a moist miswak, rinsing the mouth without swallowing, bathing, or wrapping in a wet cloth to beat the heat are all permitted. The legal basis is drawn from the Prophet's own practice and the general principle that actions aiding worship are encouraged, not forbidden.

Guided Path
1
Identify the acts under discussion
List the specific actions the text addresses: using a wet miswak, rinsing the mouth and nose (without wudu), bathing (ghusl), and wrapping oneself in a wet cloth for cooling.
All these actions involve moisture but do not involve swallowing water.
2
Determine the ruling (hukm) for each act
The text states each act is 'not disliked' (la yukrah) according to the mufta bihi. In Hanafi fiqh, 'not disliked' means permissible (mubah) or even recommended if it aids worship.
The mufta bihi is the position of Abu Yusuf; note the intra-madhhab disagreement.
3
Locate the primary dalil (proof)
The commentator adduces the hadith that the Prophet poured water over his head while fasting (Abu Dawud) and that Ibn Umar wet his cloth and wrapped it around himself while fasting.
These actions are explicit proofs (amal) of permissibility.
4
Understand the legal reasoning (wajh al-dalalah)
The Prophet's action of pouring water on the head indicates that cooling the body with water is permitted; analogically, wetting a cloth or rinsing the mouth shares the same underlying cause (illa) of cooling and refreshment without invalidating the fast.
The reasoning supports permissibility unless swallowing occurs.
5
Confirm the authority weight and fatwa status
The text concludes 'ala al-mufta bihi (according to the fatwa) and identifies this as the saying of Abu Yusuf. This is the operative ruling for practice.
Earlier Hanafi view (Abu Hanifa) considered these acts disliked, but later fatwa adopts the more lenient position.

Source Text

(و) لا يكره لــه (المضمضة والاستنشاق)، وقد فعلهما (لغير وضوء. و) لا (الاغتسال. و) لا (التَّلَفْفُ بشوب مبتل)، قصد ذلك (للتبرُّدِ) ودفع الحر على المفتى به ، وهو قول أبي يوسف لأن النبي : « صب عَلَى رَأْسِهِ المَاءِ وَهُوَ صَائِمٌ مِنَ العَطَشِ أَوْ مِنَ الحَرِّ » (۳) رواه أبو داود، وكان ابن عمر يبــل الثـوب ويلفه عليه وهو صائم ، ولأن هذه الأشياء بها عون على العبادة، ودفع للضجر الطبيعي، وكرهها أبو حنيفة لما فيه من إظهار الضجر في إقامة العبادة، كما في ( البرهان».
And if it is moist, or wet with water, and rinsing the mouth, and sniffing water, without ablution, and bathing, and wrapping oneself in a wet cloth to cool down, [all are permissible] according to the fatwa. And because of his saying: 'A prayer... is better than seventy prayers without a miswak.' ... It is not disliked for him to use the miswak even if it is moist, meaning green, or wet with water, due to the generality of what we narrated. And it is not disliked for him to rinse the mouth and sniff water without wudu. And not bathing. And not wrapping himself in a wet cloth, intending that for cooling and warding off heat, according to the fatwa, and it is the saying of Abu Yusuf because the Prophet poured water on his head while fasting from thirst or heat, reported by Abu Dawud, and Ibn Umar used to wet his cloth and wrap it on himself while fasting, and because these things are an aid to worship and repel natural annoyance, and Abu Hanifa disliked them because it shows annoyance in performing worship, as in al-Burhan.
Source pages p. 664

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the sharh of Nur al-Idah, clarifying the permissibility of certain actions for a fasting person. The matn states that using a moist or wet miswak, rinsing the mouth and nose without wudu, bathing, and wrapping in a wet cloth to cool down are all permissible according to the relied-upon opinion (mufta bihi). The commentator expands by providing hadith evidence: the Prophet's statement that a prayer with miswak is better than seventy without it, and the practice of the Prophet and Ibn Umar. These establish the permissibility. An intra-madhhab difference is noted: Abu Hanifa disliked these actions, considering them a sign of annoyance in worship, while Abu Yusuf permitted them because they aid worship and prevent harm. The fatwa follows Abu Yusuf. The hadith about pouring water on the head while fasting is cited as support.

Key Terms
السواك (al-siwak): Using a stick or toothbrush to clean the teeth; for the fasting person, it is permissible even if wet.
المضمضة (al-madamada): Rinsing the mouth with water without swallowing.
الاستنشاق (al-istinshaq): Sniffing water into the nose without swallowing.
الاغتسال (al-ightisal): Bathing or taking a shower.
التلفف بثوب مبتل (al-talaffuf bi-thawb mubtall): Wrapping oneself in a wet cloth to cool down.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary evidence cited in the passage for the permissibility of bathing and using a wet cloth while fasting?
A) A verse from the Quran about permissible cooling.
B) The Prophet pouring water over his head while fasting.
C) A consensus (ijma) of the Companions.
D) A qiyas on the permissibility of eating when extremely thirsty.

Key Takeaway
Using a wet miswak, rinsing, bathing, or wrapping in a wet cloth to cool down are all permissible for the fasting person according to the relied-upon Hanafi fatwa, as long as no water is swallowed intentionally.

Sawm — Fasting

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Acts of Cooling While Fasting: Abu Hanifa vs. Abu Yusuf
Intra-Hanafi dispute on permissibility of cooling actions while fasting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the difference of opinion between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf regarding the permissibility of using a wet miswak, rinsing, bathing, and wet cloth for a fasting person, and identify the mufta bihi position along with its rationale.

Core Idea
Abu Yusuf permitted cooling acts as an aid to worship; Abu Hanifa disliked them out of concern for showing annoyance—the fatwa follows Abu Yusuf.

Within the Hanafi school, Imam Abu Hanifa and his student Abu Yusuf held differing views on whether a fasting person may use wet miswak, rinse, bathe, or use a wet cloth to cool down. This lesson examines the reasoning behind each position and explains why the later fatwa adopted Abu Yusuf's more lenient view. Understanding such internal ikhtilaf is crucial for a mufti to know which opinion to give in practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue in dispute
The contested issue: Are the actions of using a wet miswak, rinsing mouth/nose without wudu, bathing, and wrapping in a wet cloth permissible (or at least not disliked) for a fasting person?
Both imams agree on the basic permissibility of these actions in principle; the dispute is about whether they are disliked (makruh) or not.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and his reasoning
Abu Hanifa held that these actions are disliked (makruh tanzihi) because they give the impression that the fasting person is annoyed or impatient with the worship of fasting.
His concern is about the appearance of discontent, not about physical invalidation.
3
State Abu Yusuf's position and his reasoning
Abu Yusuf held that these actions are not disliked; rather they are permissible or even recommended because they aid worship and prevent natural discomfort.
He cites the Prophet's action of pouring water on his head and Ibn Umar's practice as evidence.
4
Analyze the evidence each side uses
Abu Yusuf relies on explicit actions of the Prophet and a Companion (dalil naqli). Abu Hanifa relies on a general principle of avoiding anything that suggests dissatisfaction with worship (istihsan based on maqasid).
Both arguments are valid within the Hanafi usul framework.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and the practical consequence
The text states that the fatwa is on Abu Yusuf's view ('ala al-mufta bihi). Thus, in practice, a Muslim may perform these actions without any dislike. The practical difference (thamarah) is that the follower of the fatwa does not incur even a tanzihi dislike.
This is a classic case of tarjih based on ease (taysir) and the principle of harm removal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And if it is moist, or wet with water, and rinsing the mouth, and sniffing water, without ablution, and bathing, and wrapping oneself in a wet cloth to cool down, [all are permissible] according to the fatwa. And because of his saying: 'A prayer... is better than seventy prayers without a miswak.' ... Key Terms
المفتى به (al-mufta bihi): The relied-upon opinion in the school for issuing fatwa.
كراهة تنزيهية (karaha tanzihiyya): A disliked but not sinful action; avoiding it is better but not obligatory.
الضجر الطبيعي (al-dajir al-tabi'i): Natural annoyance or discomfort (e.g., from heat).
إظهار الضجر (izhar al-dajir): Showing annoyance or restlessness.

C) Because the Prophet forbade them.
D) Because they are only permissible before midday.

Key Takeaway
Intra-Hanafi differences like this one (Abu Hanifa vs. Abu Yusuf) are resolved by later tarjih; the mufta bihi for cooling actions while fasting is permissibility (Abu Yusuf), not dislike.

Sawm — Fasting

Three Recommended Acts for the Fasting Person
Stating the recommended acts for a fasting person

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three recommended acts for a fasting person in the Hanafi school and apply the conditions for hastening iftar on cloudy versus clear days.

Core Idea
Suhur, delaying it, and hastening iftar are confirmed sunnah acts that bring great blessing.

The fast is not merely abstaining from food and drink; it is also a time for certain commendable practices that increase reward and follow the prophetic example. This lesson focuses on three such acts: the pre-dawn meal (suhur), delaying that meal until late, and breaking the fast promptly after sunset. The author provides explicit hadith evidence and clarifies a crucial nuance: on cloudy days, one must exercise caution to avoid breaking the fast prematurely.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Three Acts
Read the passage and list the three acts mentioned: suhur (pre-dawn meal), delaying suhur, and hastening iftar (breaking the fast). Note that these are recommended, not obligatory.
The acts are explicitly enumerated as 'three things'.
2
Understand the Ruling Level
The Hanafi school classifies these acts as sunnah muakkadah (confirmed sunnah). This means they are highly recommended but not obligatory. Omitting them does not invalidate the fast.
The hadith mentions them as 'from the character of the messengers', indicating strong desirability.
3
Examine the Evidence (Dalil)
The author cites two hadith: one from al-Tabarani listing the three acts, and another from Ahmad emphasizing the blessing of suhur even with a sip of water. Consider how these hadith support the recommendation.
The hadith are ahad (single narrated) but accepted due to their implications and practice.
4
Apply the Exception for Cloudy Days
Hastening iftar is recommended only on clear days. On cloudy days, one should delay iftar until certain of sunset to avoid accidentally breaking the fast too early. This shows the school's concern for preserving the validity of the act.
The text says 'in ghayr yawm ghaym' (on a day that is not cloudy).
5
Recognize the Recommended Timing for Iftar
The recommended hastening is before the stars become abundant, as mentioned by Qadi Khan. This provides a practical guideline for when to break the fast.
This is a time-based qualification within the general recommendation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويُستحب له ثلاثة أشياء: السحور. وتأخيره. وتعجيلُ الفِطْرِ في غير يوم غيم. لقوله صلى الله عليه وسلم : « ثَلاثُ مِنْ أَخْلاقِ المُرْسَلِينَ: تَعْجِيلُ الإِفْطَارِ وَتَأْخِيرُ السَّحورِ وَوَضَعَ اليَمِينِ عَلَى الشَّمَالِ فِي الصَّلاةِ » رواه الطبراني. وتعجيل الفطر لما روينا، وهذا في غير يوم غيم. وفي الغيم يحتاط حفظاً للصوم عن الإفساد، فقد يفسد بظن الغروب لغيم. والتعجيل المستحب قبل استفحال النجوم، ذكره قاضي خان في شرح الجامع الصغير. وظاهر الحديث يفييد حصول البركة ولو بالماء في السحور. قال : « السَّحورُ بَرَكَةٌ فَلَا تَدَعُوهُ، وَلَوْ أَنْ يَجْرَعَ أَحَدُكُمْ جُرْعَةَ مَاءٍ فَإِنَّ اللَّهَ وَمَلائِكَتَهُ يُصَلُّونَ عَلَى المُتَسَحْرِينَ » رواه أحمد كذا في البحر.
And three things are recommended for him: suhur (pre-dawn meal), and delaying it, and hastening iftar on a day that is not cloudy. Because of his saying, peace be upon him: 'Three things are from the character of the messengers: hastening iftar, delaying suhur, and placing the right hand over the left in prayer' narrated by al-Tabarani. And hastening iftar is based on what we narrated, and this is on a day that is not cloudy. On a cloudy day, one exercises caution to preserve the fast from corruption, for it may be broken by assuming sunset due to clouds. The recommended hastening is before the stars become abundant, as mentioned by Qadi Khan in Sharh al-Jami' al-Saghir. The apparent meaning of the hadith is that blessing is attained even with water in suhur. He said: 'Suhur is a blessing, so do not abandon it, even if one of you takes a sip of water, for Allah and His angels send blessings upon those who take suhur' narrated by Ahmad, as in al-Bahr.
Source pages p. 664

Commentary
This passage is a ruling specifying the three recommended acts for a fasting person: taking suhur, delaying suhur (i.e., eating it as late as possible before dawn), and hastening iftar (breaking the fast promptly after sunset). The commentator provides hadith evidence: a hadith from al-Tabarani listing these as traits of messengers, and another from Ahmad emphasizing the blessing of suhur even with a sip of water. The ruling specifies that hastening iftar is recommended only on non-cloudy days; on cloudy days one should be cautious to avoid breaking the fast prematurely due to unclear sunset. The recommended time for iftar is before the stars become fully visible, as per Qadi Khan. Suhur is recommended even with minimal food or drink.

Key Terms
السحور (al-suhur): The pre-dawn meal taken before the fast begins; recommended even with a sip of water.
تعجيل الفطر (ta'jil al-fitr): Hastening the breaking of the fast immediately after sunset, recommended on clear days.
تأخير السحور (ta'khir al-suhur): Delaying the pre-dawn meal to just before the time of imsak (start of fast).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
On which type of day is it recommended to hasten iftar?
A) On any day, regardless of weather.
B) Only on cloudy days.
C) On days that are not cloudy.
D) Only on days when one is certain of sunset.

Key Takeaway
The three recommended acts – suhur, delaying suhur, and hastening iftar on clear days – are sunnah muakkadah; omitting them does not break the fast but loses great reward.

The Eight Excuses and the Ruling on Illness
Listing excuses and detailed ruling on illness with proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the eight excuses that permit breaking the fast, explain the Hanafi condition for illness, and apply the principle that a reasonable assumption of harm permits breaking the fast even if the harm does not materialize, and state the mufta bihi view on kaffarah.

Core Idea
Breaking the fast is permitted if you reasonably fear harm — even if the harm does not occur, no kaffarah is due.

This passage lists eight excuses (awarid) that allow a fasting person to break the fast. Illness is discussed in detail: you must fear an increase in sickness or slow recovery. The text uses the analogy of a warrior (ghazi) to show that even a strong likelihood of harm suffices, and this extends to fevers. A significant intra-Hanafi dispute arises when the feared condition does not materialize: the most correct view is that no expiation (kaffarah) is required, contrary to some earlier opinions.

Guided Path
1
List the eight excuses
Read the passage to identify the eight excuses: illness, travel, coercion, pregnancy, nursing, hunger, thirst, and old age. They are called al-awarid.
These are non-permanent impediments.
2
Understand the definition of illness
Illness is defined as a meaning that necessitates change of nature towards corruption, occurring internally. It includes eye pain, wounds, headaches, etc. The key element is fear of increase or slow recovery.
The fear must be reasonable, not speculative.
3
Analyze the warrior analogy
The text states that a warrior (ghazi) who knows he will fight in Ramadan and fears weakness may break his fast before battle, even if he is not traveling. This is because the strong likelihood of harm is considered a valid excuse.
This ruling is from al-Fath and al-Tajnis.
4
Trace the qiyas to fever cases
By analogy (qiyas), a person with a fever who breaks his fast early on the assumption that fever will come is excused if the assumption was reasonable. The principle is that the powerful probability (ghalaba) of harm is treated as actuality.
This extends to menstruation assumptions and village Eid drum confusion.
5
Evaluate the dispute on kaffarah
If the feared condition does not materialize, some early Hanafi scholars held that kaffarah is due. However, the text declares 'al-asahh la kaffarah' (the most correct is no kaffarah), supported by Qadi Khan and later authorities like al-Durar.
The mufta bihi view thus requires only qada' (make-up fast).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في العوارض جمع عارض، وهي للصائم ثمانية: المرض والسفر والإكراه والحبل، والرضاع والجوع والعطش وكبر السن)، وبها يباح الفطر. فيجوز (لِمَنْ خاف) وهو مريض (زيادة المرض) بـكـم أو كيف لو صام، والمرض معنى يوجب تغير الطبيعة إلى الفساد، يحدث أولاً في الباطن ثم يظهر أثره، وسواء كان لوجع عين أو جراحة أو صداع، فأي مرض كان إذا خافه، (أو) خاف (بطء البُرْء) منه بالصوم، جاز له الفطر؛ لأن زيادته وامتداده قد يفضي إلى الهلاك، فيجب الاحتراز عنه. وقالوا: الغازي إذا كان يعلم يقيناً أنه يقاتل العدو في شهر رمضان، ويخاف الضعف إن لم يفطر، فقبل الحرب يفطر، مسافراً كان أو مقيماً، كذا في «الفتح» وعلمه بالقتال يؤخذ مما قال في « التجنيس » : الغازي إذا كان بإزاء العدو ويعلم يقيناً... إلخ، وعلى القياس هذا قالوا فيمن له نوبة الحمـة، فأفطر في أول اليوم قبل أن تظهر الحمى على ظن أنها تعتريه فتضعفه لا بأس به؛ لأنه بحكـم الغلبة كالكائن، كما في الغازي، فإن لم تعتره لزمته الكفارة، وكذا المرأة إذا ظنت مجيء الحيض ثم لم تحض تلزمها الكفارة؛ لأنه إفطار في يوم لم تتمكن فيه شبهة إباحة الإفطار انتهى. والأصح عـدم الكفارة فيهما، كذا في حاشية ( الدرر ) وفي (مجمع الروايات » : قال في ( الجامع الصغير» لقاضي خان: والأصح لا كفارة عليهما، وكذا أهل الرساتيق (۳) إذا سمعوا أصوات الطبل يوم الثلاثين فظنوه يوم عيد، فأفطروا ثم تبين أن الطبل لغيره لا كفارة عليهم انتهى. (و) يجوز الفطر (لحامل ومرضع خافت: نقصان العقل أو الهلاك. أو المرض) سواء كان ( على نفسها، أو ولدها، نَسَباً كان أو رَضَاعَاً) (٤) ، ولها شرب الدواء إذا أخبر الطبيب، أنه يمنع استطلاق بطن الرضيع، وتفطر لهذا الـعـــذر
Chapter on the Excuses. The plural of 'aarid, and they are eight for the fasting person: illness, travel, coercion, pregnancy, nursing, hunger, thirst, and old age. With these, breaking the fast is permitted. So it is permissible for one who fears, while being ill, increase of the illness, in quantity or quality, if he fasts—and illness is a meaning that necessitates change of nature towards corruption, occurring first internally then showing its effect, whether it be from eye pain, a wound, or headache—any illness if he fears it, or fears slow recovery from it due to fasting, then it is permissible to break the fast because its increase and prolongation may lead to destruction, so one must guard against it. And they said: The warrior if he knows for certain that he will fight the enemy in Ramadan and fears weakness if he does not break his fast, then before battle he breaks his fast, whether a traveler or a resident, as in al-Fath. And his knowledge of fighting is taken from what is said in al-Tajnis: the warrior when he is facing the enemy and knows for certain... etc. And by analogy they said about one who has a fever, if he breaks his fast at the beginning of the day before the fever appears, on the assumption that it will overcome him and weaken him, there is no harm; because it is in the ruling of the prevailing, like the warrior. If it does not overcome him, then kaffarah becomes obligatory upon him. Similarly, a woman if she assumes that menstruation will come and then it does not, kaffarah becomes obligatory upon her, because it is breaking the fast on a day in which no allowance for breaking the fast was established. End. The most correct is no kaffarah upon them, as in the Hashiyah of al-Durar and in Majma' al-Riwayat. He said in al-Jami' al-Saghir by Qadi Khan: The most correct is no kaffarah upon them. Similarly, the people of villages if they hear drum sounds on the thirtieth day and assume it is Eid, so they break their fast, then it becomes clear that the drum was for something else, no kaffarah upon them. End. And it is permissible to break the fast for a pregnant woman and a nursing woman if they fear deficiency of intellect, or destruction, or illness, whether upon themselves or their child, whether by lineage or by suckling. And she can drink medicine if a doctor informs that it will prevent diarrhea in the infant, and she breaks her fast due to this excuse.
Source pages p. 665

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the excuses (awarid) that permit a fasting person to break the fast. It lists eight excuses: illness, travel, coercion, pregnancy, nursing, hunger, thirst, and old age. The detailed discussion focuses on illness and pregnancy/nursing. For illness, the key condition is fear of increased sickness or slow recovery; the definition of illness is a change in the body's nature leading to corruption. The text then discusses the case of a warrior (ghazi) who fears weakness if he fasts—he may break his fast before battle, even if not a traveler, because of the strong likelihood of harm. By analogy, a person with a fever may break his fast early on a reasonable assumption. However, if the feared condition does not materialize, there is a difference of opinion: some say kaffarah is due, but the most correct opinion (al-asahh) is no kaffarah. This is supported by Qadi Khan and others. For a pregnant or nursing woman, the permissibility to break the fast is based on fear of harm to her mind, her life, her health, or that of her child (whether biological or through suckling). She may also take medication to prevent harm to the infant and break her fast for this reason.

Key Terms
العوارض (al-awarid): Excuses or circumstances that permit a fasting person to break the fast; eight are listed.
الغلبة (al-ghalaba): Overwhelming likelihood or strong probability; a legal principle that treats a highly probable event as if it has occurred.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is one of the eight excuses that permit breaking the fast?
A) Fear of poverty
B) Intense hunger
C) Dislike of fasting
D) Forgetting the intention

Key Takeaway
A reasonable assumption of harm permits breaking the fast, and if the harm does not materialize, only qada' is required – no kaffarah.
