Ruling on Pregnancy and Nursing as Excuses for Breaking the Fast
Detailed ruling on pregnancy and nursing as specific excuses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a pregnant or nursing woman may break the fast, explain the scope of harm (self or child, biological or suckling), and apply the ruling on taking medication to prevent harm to the infant.

Core Idea
Pregnant and nursing women may break the fast if they fear harm to their own mental or physical health or to their child's health.

The second part of the passage addresses pregnancy (haml) and nursing (rida'). The permissibility is conditional upon fear of harm: deficiency of intellect (aql), destruction (halak), or illness (marad). The harm can be to herself or her child, whether the child is biological or through suckling (rida'). Additionally, a nursing mother may take prescribed medication to prevent diarrhea in the infant and break her fast for this excuse.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two specific excuses
The passage specifically mentions 'hamil' (pregnant woman) and 'murdi'' (nursing woman). These are two of the eight excuses.
2
List the feared harms
The fears that permit breaking the fast are: deficiency of intellect (nusqan al-aql), destruction (halak), or illness (marad). These apply to herself or her child.
The child is defined broadly: biological relation or suckling.
3
Understand the scope of the child
The harm to the child is not limited to one's own biological child; it includes a child through suckling (radaa'), such as a wet nurse.
This reflects the Hanafi view that the obligation of nursing can be acquired.
4
Examine the ruling on medication
If a doctor informs the mother that a medicine will prevent diarrhea (istitla') in the infant, she may take it and break her fast for this excuse.
The medication must be prescribed by a trustworthy doctor (al-tabib).

8 min
When Fear of Illness Permits Breaking Fast
Clarifying when fear of illness excuses fasting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which fear of illness permits breaking fast and apply the Hanafi ruling that no kaffarah is required for unfulfilled expectations (mufta bihi).

Core Idea
Fear of increased illness or slow recovery permits breaking fast, and no kaffarah is due if the expected harm does not occur.

The chapter on excusing factors ('awarid) for fasting lists eight conditions that permit breaking the fast. Illness is the first, and this passage elaborates when fear of worsening or slow recovery justifies iftar. The ruling extends to analogous cases such as a warrior expecting battle, one anticipating a fever, a woman expecting menstruation, and villagers misled by drums. The key nuance: the relied-upon Hanafi position absolves kaffarah when the expected excuse does not materialize, based on treating strong probability as legal reality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the excusing factor and its legal effect
Recognize that illness is one of eight 'awarid. The passage states that fear of increase (quantitative or qualitative) or slow recovery permits iftar, because of potential harm (halak).
The permission is mubah, not obligatory.
2
Analyze the condition for permission: ghalabat al-zann
The fear must be based on strong probability (ghalabat al-zann), not mere doubt. This is derived from the warrior case: 'knows with certainty' or from analogy to fever expectation.
Weak fear does not excuse.
3
Examine the analogous cases
The passage extends the ruling to: warrior facing enemy, one expecting a fever, woman suspecting menstruation, villagers hearing drums. In each, if the expected harm does not occur, the initial break is treated as valid.
The analogy is based on dominance of the excuse.
4
Resolve the kaffarah dispute
The text reports a difference: some earlier sources required kaffarah if the excuse does not materialize. The relied-upon view (al-mu'tamad) is no kaffarah, as in Al-Durar, Majma' al-Riwayat.
This is the operative position for fatwa.
5
Apply the principle to new scenarios
Using the principle that a valid assumption is treated as reality (tanazil al-zann manzilat al-yaqin) in necessities, we can apply to modern cases like chronic illness, medical advice, etc.
Consult reliable medical opinion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في العوارض
جمع عارض، وهي للصائم ثمانية: المرض والسفر والإكراه والحبل، والرضاع والجوع والعطش وكبر السن)، وبها يباح الفطر. فيجوز (لِمَنْ خاف) وهو مريض (زيادة المرض) بـكـم أو كيف لو صام، والمرض معنى يوجب تغير الطبيعة إلى الفساد، يحدث أولاً في الباطن ثم يظهر أثره، وسواء كان لوجع عين أو جراحة أو صداع، فأي مرض كان إذا خافه، (أو) خاف (بطء البُرْء) منه بالصوم، جاز له الفطر؛ لأن زيادته وامتداده قد يفضي إلى الهلاك، فيجب الاحتراز عنه. وقالوا: الغازي إذا كان يعلم يقيناً أنه يقاتل العدو في شهر رمضان، ويخاف الضعف إن لم يفطر، فقبل الحرب يفطر، مسافراً كان أو مقيماً، كذا في «الفتح» وعلمه بالقتال يؤخذ مما قال في « التجنيس » : الغازي إذا كان بإزاء العدو ويعلم يقيناً... إلخ، وعلى القياس هذا قالوا فيمن له نوبة الحمـة، فأفطر في أول اليوم قبل أن تظهر الحمى على ظن أنها تعتريه فتضعفه لا بأس به؛ لأنه بحكـم الغلبة كالكائن، كما في الغازي، فإن لم تعتره لزمته الكفارة، وكذا المرأة إذا ظنت مجيء الحيض ثم لم تحض تلزمها الكفارة؛ لأنه إفطار في يوم لم تتمكن فيه شبهة إباحة الإفطار انتهى. والأصح عـدم الكفارة فيهما، كذا في حاشية ( الدرر ) وفي (مجمع الروايات » : قال في ( الجامع الصغير» لقاضي خان: والأصح لا كفارة عليهما، وكذا أهل الرساتيق إذا سمعوا أصوات الطبل يوم الثلاثين فظنوه يوم عيد، فأفطروا ثم تبين أن الطبل لغيره لا كفارة عليهم انتهى.
Chapter on excusing factors. Plural of 'arid, they are for the fasting person eight: illness, travel, compulsion, pregnancy, breastfeeding, hunger, thirst, and old age. With these, breaking fast is permitted. So it is permissible (for one who fears) while ill (increase of illness) in amount or kind if he fasts. Illness is a meaning that causes change of nature to corruption, occurring first internally then its effect appears, whether from eye pain, wound, or headache. Any illness if he fears it, (or) fears (slowness of recovery) from it by fasting, he may break fast because its increase and extension may lead to destruction, so avoidance is obligatory. They said: The warrior (ghazi) if he knows with certainty that he will fight the enemy in Ramadan, and fears weakness if he does not break fast, then before war he breaks fast, whether traveler or resident. Likewise in 'Al-Fath'. His knowledge of fighting is taken from what was said in 'Al-Tajnīs': The warrior if he is facing the enemy and knows with certainty etc. By analogy, they said regarding one who has a bout of fever, who breaks fast early in the day before the fever appears, on the assumption that it will attack him and weaken him: there is no harm, because due to the dominance it is like existing, as in the case of the warrior. If it does not attack him, then kaffarah is obligatory upon him. Similarly, if a woman suspects menstruation and then does not menstruate, kaffarah is obligatory upon her because it is breaking fast on a day when no permission to break fast was possible. End. The correct view is no kaffarah in both cases, as in the margin of 'Al-Durar' and in 'Majma' al-Riwayat': In 'Al-Jami' al-Saghir' by Qadi Khan: The correct view is no kaffarah upon them. Similar: villagers who hear drum sounds on the thirtieth and think it is Eid, so they break fast, then it becomes clear the drum was for something else: no kaffarah upon them. End.
Source pages p. 665

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on excusing factors ('awarid) for fasting. It lists eight excuses that permit breaking fast: illness, travel, compulsion, pregnancy, breastfeeding, hunger, thirst, and old age. The author then elaborates on the first excuse: fear of increased illness or delayed recovery. The key principle is that valid fear must be based on reasonable expectation of harm. The discussion includes special cases: a warrior who knows he will fight in Ramadan may break fast before war to maintain strength, even if not traveling. By analogy, one expecting a fever may break fast early, though the ruling on kaffarah if the fever does not occur is debated: the relied-upon position (al-mu'tamad) is that no kaffarah is required either for the fever case or for a woman expecting menstruation who does not menstruate. The same applies to villagers who mistakenly break fast due to false Eid drums. The Hanafi school prioritizes avoiding harm, but without clear proof of necessity, the original obligation remains.

Key Terms
العوارض (al-'awarid): Events or conditions that excuse one from fasting or permit breaking it; specifically eight: illness, travel, etc.
زيادة المرض (ziyādat al-maraḍ): Increase in illness in intensity (qualitative) or duration (quantitative) that results from fasting.
غلبة الظن (ghalabat al-zann): Strong probability or dominant assumption, legally treated as certainty in matters of necessity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Kaffarah is required.
B) No kaffarah is required.
C) Kaffarah is required only if the person was certain.
D) Kaffarah is required only if the person broke after midday.

Key Takeaway
Fear of increased illness or slow recovery permits breaking fast, and no kaffarah is required if the expected harm does not occur, based on the principle that a valid assumption is treated as reality in matters of necessity.

Breaking Fast for Pregnant and Breastfeeding Women
Explaining permission to break fast for pregnant and breastfeeding women

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on breaking fast for pregnant and breastfeeding women, identify the conditions for valid fear, and cite the primary evidence.

Core Idea
Pregnant and breastfeeding women may break fast if they have strong probability of harm to themselves or their child.

Fasting during Ramadan is a pillar of Islam, but Allah provides concessions for those in hardship. This lesson examines the specific permission for pregnant and breastfeeding women, clarifying the conditions under which they may break fast and the evidence supporting this ruling. The Hanafi school bases this on a clear hadith and the principle of removing hardship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The text states that breaking fast is permissible (ja'iz) for pregnant and breastfeeding women under certain conditions. Note the hukm level: mubah (permissible), not wajib or haram.
The ruling is conditional; without fear, fasting remains obligatory.
2
Identify the conditions for permission
The women must fear (khafat) harm: deficiency of intellect, destruction, or illness. This fear can be for herself or her child, biological or via nursing.
The fear must be based on overwhelming probability (ghalabat al-zann), not mere doubt.
3
Identify the evidence
The primary proof is the hadith: 'Indeed Allah has placed away from the traveler the fast and half of the prayer, and from the pregnant and the nursing the fast.' This directly includes both women.
The hadith is ahad (single narration), but its meaning is clear and relied upon.
4
Analyze the rebuttal of an alternative view
Some argued that 'murdi' (nursing) refers only to a wet nurse. The text refutes this: the hadith does not specify, and nursing is religiously obligatory for the mother, especially if the father cannot afford a wet nurse.
Thus the mother is included, and the permission extends to her.
5
Determine the standard for valid fear
The fear must be based on ghalabat al-zann (overwhelming probability), established by prior personal experience or the report of a skilled, just Muslim physician.
This gives weight to medical advice in determining the necessity to break fast.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و) يجوز الفطر (لحامل ومرضع خافت: نقصان العقل أو الهلاك. أو المرض) سواء كان ( على نفسها، أو ولدها، نَسَباً كان أو رَضَاعَاً) ، ولها شرب الدواء إذا أخبر الطبيب، أنه يمنع استطلاق بطن الرضيع، وتفطر لهذا الـعـــذر، والخوف المعتبرُ ما كان مُسْتَنِدَا لِغَلَبَةِ الظَّنِّ: بتجربة، أو إخبار طبيب مُسلمٍ حاذِقٍ عَدل. وذلك؛ لقوله عليه أفضل الصلاة والسلام : ( إنَّ اللَّهَ وَضَعَ عَنِ المُسَافِرِ الصَّوْمَ وَشَطْرَ الصَّلاةِ وَعَنِ الحُبْلَى وَالمُرْضِعِ الصَّومَ ) ، ولأنَّهُمَا يلحقهما الحَرَجُ بِالصَّوْمِ، فشرع الإفطار في حقهما كالمسافر والمريض، وما قيل: إن المراد بالمرضع الظئر فمردود بهذا الحديث، وبأن الإرضاع واجب على الأم ديانة، لا سيما إذا كان الأب معسراً.
(And) it is permissible to break fast (for a pregnant woman and a breastfeeding woman who fear: deficiency of intellect, or destruction, or illness) whether (upon herself or her child, whether lineage or nursing). And she may drink medicine if a doctor informs that it prevents diarrhea of the infant, and she breaks fast for this excuse. The fear that is considered is what is based on overwhelming probability: by experience or by the report of a Muslim, skilled, just physician. That is due to his saying, peace be upon him: 'Indeed Allah has placed away from the traveler the fast and half of the prayer, and from the pregnant and the nursing the fast.' And because hardship affects them by fasting, so breaking fast is legislated for them like the traveler and the sick. And what is said that the intended meaning of 'nursing' is the wet nurse is refuted by this hadith, and because nursing is obligatory upon the mother religiously, especially if the father is insolvent.
Source pages p. 665

Commentary
This section addresses the ruling for pregnant and breastfeeding women. They are permitted to break fast if they fear any harm, including mental impairment (deficiency of intellect), death, or illness to themselves or their child. The child may be biological or foster (by nursing). The permissibility is based on the hadith, which explicitly mentions the pregnant and nursing women. Some argued that 'nursing' (murdi') refers only to a wet nurse, but the author refutes this: the hadith does not specify, and nursing is religiously obligatory for the mother, especially when the father cannot afford a wet nurse. The condition for valid fear is strong probability (ghalabat al-zann), established by prior experience or the testimony of a Muslim physician who is skilled and just (not openly sinful). The Hanafi school thus gives significant weight to medical advice.

Key Terms
المرضع (al-murḍi'): A woman who is breastfeeding a child, whether the child is her own biological child or a foster child through wet-nursing.
غلبة الظن (ghalabat al-ẓann): Overwhelming probability that harm will occur, based on experience or reliable medical opinion.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Overwhelming probability based on experience or a reliable Muslim doctor's report.
C) Only a doctor's report, without need for personal experience.

Key Takeaway
Pregnant and breastfeeding women may break fast if they have strong evidence of harm to themselves or their child; a mere feeling is insufficient.

Severe Thirst/Hunger and Travel: When to Break the Fast
Explaining permissibility of breaking fast due to severe thirst/hunger and travel preferences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on breaking fast due to severe thirst/hunger and travel, and apply the preference rule when traveling with companions who are breaking fast.

Core Idea
Breaking fast is permissible for severe thirst/hunger and travel, but fasting is preferred unless harm or social harmony dictates otherwise.

This lesson examines additional excuses beyond illness that permit breaking the fast in Ramadan: severe thirst or hunger that threatens life or intellect, and travel. It also clarifies the nuanced preferences regarding fasting while traveling, especially when the traveler is part of a group that is breaking fast or shares expenses. Finally, it addresses whether a person who dies while still under such an excuse must make a will regarding missed fasts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two additional excuses
The passage mentions severe thirst and severe hunger as justifications for breaking the fast. The key condition is that the person fears destruction (halak) or loss of intellect. Determine whether the cause is self-inflicted or not, as that affects the obligation of kaffarah.
Self-inflicted causes (e.g., hard labor) may require kaffarah according to some Hanafi scholars.
2
Analyze the travel ruling: basic permissibility
The Quranic verse (2:184) allows the traveler to break his fast. The Hanafi ruling is that breaking is mubah (permissible), but fasting is more virtuous (ahabb) if it causes no harm. Establish that this is the default position.
The proof is the verse: 'So whoever among you is ill or on a journey [during them] – then an equal number of days [are to be made up].'
3
Apply the social preference adjustment
If the traveler's companions are breaking fast or they share expenses, it is better (afdal) for him to break fast to maintain group harmony (muwafaqah al-jama'ah). This is an exception to the general preference for fasting.
Social unity and avoiding hardship for the group override the individual's desire for extra reward.
4
Note the final ruling on making a will
A person who dies while his excuse (e.g., illness, travel) continues is not obligated to make a will (wasiyya) regarding missed fasts or their compensation, because the obligation was not yet due. This is consistent with the principle that obligations are not inherited unless they were already due.
This applies to both qada and kaffarah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولمنْ حَصَلَ لـ عطش شديد، أو جوع يخافُ منه الهلاك. وللمسافِرِ الفِطْرُ، وصومُهُ أحبُّ إن لم يَضُرَهُ، ولم تكن عامَةُ رُفْقَتِهِ مفطرين، ولا مشتركين في النفقَةِ. فإن كانوا مشتركين، أو مفطرين، فالأفضلُ فِطْرُهُ، مُوَافَقَةٌ للجماعة، ولا يجب الإيصاءُ على مَنْ ماتَ قبلَ زَوالِ عُذرِهِ بِمَرَضِ وَسَفَرٍ ونَحوِهِ كَمَا تَقَدَّمَ .
And for one who experiences severe thirst, or hunger from which he fears destruction. And for the traveler, breaking fast is permissible, but his fasting is more beloved if it does not harm him, and if the majority of his companions are not breaking fast, and they are not sharing expenses. If they are sharing expenses or are breaking fast, then breaking fast is preferable for him, in agreement with the group. And it is not obligatory to make a will (regarding kaffarah) for one who dies before his excuse (of illness, travel, etc.) ends, as previously mentioned.
Source pages p. 666

Commentary
This final section covers three more excuses: severe thirst, severe hunger, and travel. For thirst and hunger, if one fears death or loss of intellect, breaking fast is permitted. However, if the thirst/hunger is self-inflicted (e.g., by exhausting labor), kaffarah may be required (the text notes a difference of opinion). For travel, the basic ruling: a traveler who began his journey before dawn may break fast, but fasting is generally better if it causes no harm. However, considerations of companionship matter: if most of his caravan are breaking fast or the group shares expenses, it is better for him to break fast to maintain unity and avoid hardship. Finally, a person who dies while still under a valid excuse (such as ongoing illness or travel) does not have to make a will regarding any missed fasts (qada or kaffarah) because the obligation was not yet due.

Key Terms
الرفقة (al-rafqah): The traveling companions, especially those sharing the journey and possibly expenses.
الإيصاء (al-iṣā'): Making a will or testament; here specifically regarding making up missed fasts or paying kaffarah after death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) He must break fast because expenses are shared.

Key Takeaway
Breaking the fast is permitted for severe thirst/hunger and travel, but social considerations with companions can shift the preference from fasting to breaking.

Definition of Valid Fear (Khawf Mu'tabar) for Breaking Fast
Defines valid fear and its criteria for breaking fast

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of valid fear (khawf mu'tabar) and apply the two methods of establishing it (experience and doctor's report).

Core Idea
Valid fear permits breaking fast only when based on strong probability, not mere doubt.

In fasting, not every fear of harm justifies breaking the fast. The Hanafi school requires a legally valid fear (khawf mu'tabar) grounded in strong probability (ghalabat al-zann). This lesson unpacks how such fear is established—either through personal experience or the report of a qualified Muslim physician—and addresses key differences in the physician's qualifications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal act and its ruling
The act is breaking the fast due to fear of harm, which is mubah (permitted) under certain conditions.
The ruling is permission, not obligation.
2
Understand the criterion: strong probability (ghalabat al-zann)
The fear must be based on strong probability, not mere possibility or certainty.
Ghalabat al-zann is treated as certainty in legal rulings.
3
Examine the two evidence sources
Valid fear is established by previous personal experience (tajribah) or the report of a Muslim skilled physician (tabib muslim hadhiq).
The hadith on pregnant/breastfeeding women supports the doctor's report method.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi difference on the doctor's uprightness
Some require the doctor to be 'adl (upright); others only require he is not outwardly sinful. The mufta bihi is the latter according to al-Kamal.
This difference does not affect the validity of the doctor's report in most cases.
5
Apply to special cases
Consider pregnant/breastfeeding women, severe thirst/hunger, and the laborer scenario where self-exertion negates the permission.
The text warns against self-inflicted hardship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والخوف المعتبرُ ما كان مُسْتَنِدَا لِغَلَبَةِ الظَّنِّ: بتجربة، أو إخبار طبيب مُسلمٍ حاذِقٍ عَدل. ولمنْ حَصَلَ لـ عطش شديد، أو جوع يخاف منه الهلاك. كما في « التاتارخانية» وذلك؛ لقوله عليه أفضل الصلاة والسلام : ( إنَّ اللَّهَ وَضَعَ عَنِ المُسَافِرِ الصَّوْمَ وَشَطْرَ الصَّلاةِ وَعَنِ الحُبْلَى وَالمُرْضِعِ الصَّومَ )، ولأنَّهُمَا يلحقهما الحَرَجُ بِالصَّوْمِ، فشرع الإفطار في حقهما كالمسافر والمريض، وما قيل: إن المراد بالمرضع الظئر فمردود بهذا الحديث، وبأن الإرضاع واجب على الأم ديانة، لا سيما إذا كان الأب معسراً، والخوف المعتبر) لإباحة الفطر طريق معرفته هو ( ما كان مُسْتَنِدَا لِغَلَبَةِ الظَّنِّ)؛ لتنزيله منزلة اليقين ( بتجربة) سابقة، (أو إخبار طبيب مُسلم حاذق عدل، كذا في ( البرهان»، وقال « الكمال » : مسلم حاذق غـيـر ظـاهـر الفسق. وقيل: عدالته شرط ) (ولمنْ حَصَلَ له عطش شديد، أو جوع) مفرط ( يخاف منه الهلاك)، أو نقصان العقل أو ذهاب بعض الحواس، وكان ذلك لا بإتعاب نفسه، إذ لو كان به تلزمه الكفارة. وقيل: لا. سئل «علي بن أحمد عن المحترف: إذا كان يعلم أنه لو اشتغل بحرفته يلحقه مرض يبيح الفطر، وهو محتاج إلى تحصيل النفقة ، هل يباح له الأكل قبل أن يمرض؟ فمنع من ذلك أشد المنع، وكذا حكاه عن استاذه ( الوبري ) ، وإذا لم يكفه عمل نصف النهار ، يستريح في النصف الباقي، وهـو محجوج بأقصر أيام الشتاء، كذا في ( التاتارخانية»،
And the fear considered (valid) is that which is based on strong probability: by previous experience, or the report of a Muslim skilled, just physician. And for one who experiences severe thirst or hunger from which he fears destruction... as in al-Tatarakhaniyyah; that is due to his saying, may the best of prayers and peace be upon him: 'Indeed Allah has removed from the traveler the fast and half of the prayer, and from the pregnant and breastfeeding woman the fast', and because hardship affects them by fasting, so breaking fast was prescribed in their case like the traveler and the sick. And what is said that by 'breastfeeding' is meant the wet nurse is rejected by this hadith, and because breastfeeding is obligatory on the mother religiously, especially if the father is insolvent. And the valid fear for permitting breaking fast, its method of knowledge is that which is based on strong probability, because it is treated as certainty: by previous experience, or the report of a Muslim skilled just physician. Thus in al-Burhan, and al-Kamal said: a skilled Muslim not apparently sinful. And it was said: his uprightness is a condition. And for one who experiences severe thirst or extreme hunger from which he fears destruction, or loss of intellect or loss of some senses, and that is not caused by exhausting himself, for if it were, then expiation is obligatory upon him. And it was said: no. Ali ibn Ahmad was asked about the laborer: if he knows that if he engages in his craft a disease will befall him that permits breaking fast, and he needs to earn maintenance, is it permitted for him to eat before he falls ill? He strongly forbade that, and likewise he narrated from his teacher al-Wabari. And if half a day's work is not sufficient for him, he rests in the remaining half, and he is refuted by the shortest days of winter. Thus in al-Tatarakhaniyyah.
Source pages p. 666

Commentary
This passage defines what constitutes valid fear (khawf mu'tabar) that permits breaking the fast in the Hanafi school. The author begins by stating that valid fear is based on strong probability (ghalabat al-zann), not certainty. This strong probability can be established in two ways: first, through personal previous experience—for example, if someone knows from past experience that fasting under certain conditions causes illness, that suffices. Second, through the report of a Muslim physician who is both skilled (haadhiq) and upright (adl). The text then discusses the case of pregnant and breastfeeding women, providing a hadith that Allah exempts them from fasting due to hardship. It rebuts the opinion that the hadith refers only to wet nurses by citing the hadith and noting that breastfeeding is obligatory on the mother. Returning to the definition, the commentator explains that the knowledge of valid fear is based on strong probability because it is treated as certainty in law. There is a difference of opinion on the doctor's conditions: al-Kamal requires him to be Muslim, skilled, and not apparently sinful; others require uprightness (adalah). The passage then covers extreme thirst or hunger that one fears will lead to destruction, loss of intellect, or loss of senses. If these conditions are caused by one's own exertion (e.g., intentional labor), then breaking fast is not permitted and expiation becomes obligatory. The text mentions a difference of opinion (qila: la). Finally, it addresses the case of a laborer (muhtari) who knows that working will cause illness that permits breaking fast. The scholar Ali ibn Ahmad strongly prohibited him from eating before illness actually occurs, even if he needs the income, since he may adjust his work schedule. The passage ends with a note about short winter days.

Key Terms
الخوف المعتبر (al-khawf al-mu'tabar): Fear that is legally valid to permit breaking fast, based on strong probability of harm.
غلبة الظن (ghalabat al-zann): Strong probability or dominant conjecture, treated as certainty in legal rulings.
طبيب حاذق عدل (tabib hadhiq 'adl): A Muslim physician who is skilled in his field and upright in character (adl), or at least not apparently sinful.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How is valid fear (khawf mu'tabar) determined according to this passage?
A) Through personal certainty (yaqin) of harm
B) Through strong probability (ghalabat al-zann) via experience or a trustworthy Muslim doctor
C) Through any feeling of discomfort while fasting
D) Through the testimony of at least two witnesses

Key Takeaway
Valid fear (khawf mu'tabar) permits breaking fast only when based on strong probability (ghalabat al-zann) from experience or a qualified Muslim physician’s report.

Traveler's Fasting: When to Fast and When to Break
Ruling on traveler's fasting: when it is preferable and when breaking is better

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi conditions for a traveler's fasting preference and identify when breaking is better.

Core Idea
A traveler may break fast, but fasting is better unless it causes harm or social unity with companions requires breaking.

Fasting while traveling is a nuanced issue in Hanafi fiqh. While the Quran grants the traveler the option to break, the school further refines this with conditions of timing, personal harm, and the status of one's traveling companions. This passage from Nur al-Idah with its commentary clarifies when fasting is preferred and when breaking is better, grounding rulings in scripture and practical social ethics.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject and the baseline ruling
The passage addresses the traveler who initiates travel before dawn. The baseline ruling: he has the option to break fast (al-fitr) during travel, as per Quran 2:184. But this option is conditional on when travel began.
Travel after becoming a faster (after dawn) does not permit breaking.
2
Understand the first condition for preference
Fasting is 'more beloved' (ahabbu) if it does not cause harm to the traveler. This is based on Quran 2:184: 'And that you fast is better for you.' Harm can be physical or excessive hardship.
This sets a default preference for fasting when able.
3
Recognize the social condition that overrides preference
If the majority of the traveling companions are not fasting (muftirin) or if they share expenses (mushtarikun fi al-nafaqah), then it is better for the traveler to break fast. The reason is muwafaqah li al-jama'ah (agreement with the group).
This avoids burdening others and maintains harmony.
4
Synthesize and apply to scenarios
Combine the conditions: (1) travel initiated before dawn, (2) no harm from fasting, (3) companions' status and expense-sharing. The ruling is: fasting is preferred unless harm or social considerations tip the scale to breaking.
The mufta bihi position: break fast when companions are breaking or sharing expenses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( وللمسافر) الذي أنشأ السفر قبل طلوع الفجر، إذ لا يباح له الفطر بإنشائه السفر بعدما أصبح صائماً، بخلاف ما لو حلَّ به مرض بعده فله ( الفطر)؛ لقولـــه تعـــالى: أنا ما مَعْدُودَاتٍ فَمَن كَانَ مِنكُم مَّرِيضًا أَوْ عَلَى سَفَرٍ فَعِدَّةٌ مِّنْ أَيَّامٍ أُخَرَ) [البقرة: ١٨٤] (وصومه) أي: المسافر (أحبُّ إِن لم يَضُرَّهُ) لقوله تعالى: ﴿ وَأَن تَصُومُوا خَيْرٌ لَكُمْ) [البقرة: ١٨٤] (و) هذا إذا لم تكن عامة رُفْقَتِهِ مفطرين، ولا مشتركين في النّفقة. فإنْ كانوا مشتركين، أو مفطرين، فالأفضلُ فطره) أي: المسافر (مُوَافَقَةٌ للجماعة)، كما في (الجوهرة) عن (الفتاوى ) .
(And for the traveler) who initiates travel before dawn, for breaking fast is not permitted for him by initiating travel after he has become a faster, unlike if illness befalls him after that, then he has (the option to break fast); due to His statement, the Most High: '...fasts of a number of days. So whoever among you is ill or on a journey, then a number of other days.' (And his fast) i.e., the traveler's (is more beloved if it does not harm him) due to His statement: 'And that you fast is better for you.' (And) this is when the majority of his companions are not breaking fast, nor are they sharing in expenses. For if they are sharing, or are breaking fast, then breaking is better (for the traveler) in agreement with the group, as in al-Jawharah from al-Fatawa.
Source pages p. 666

Commentary
This passage gives the detailed Hanafi ruling on fasting for a traveler. It begins by clarifying that the permission to break fast applies only if the traveler initiated the journey before dawn. If he began his journey after becoming a faster (i.e., after dawn), he may not break fast due to his travel. However, if he becomes ill after dawn, he may break fast because illness is a separate excuse. The foundational proof is Quran 2:184 which grants a concession to the traveler and the sick. The passage then addresses the preference: fasting is more beloved (ahabbu) for the traveler if it does not cause him harm, based on Quran 2:184 'that you fast is better for you'. However, there is an important nuance: if the majority of his traveling companions are not fasting (muftirin) or if they share expenses (mushtarikun fi al-nafaqah), then it is better for the traveler to break fast to maintain unity and avoid burdening the group. This ruling is cited from al-Jawharah quoting al-Fatawa. The underlying principle is that while the traveler has the choice, communal harmony and avoiding hardship take precedence.

Key Terms
صومه أحب (sawmuhu ahabbu): It is more beloved (i.e., recommended) for the traveler to fast if it does not harm him.
رفقة (rufqah): Traveling companions; their fasting status and expense-sharing affect the ruling.
مفطرين (muftirin): Those who are not fasting (i.e., breaking their fast).
مشتركين في النفقة (mushtarikun fi al-nafaqah): Those who share expenses (e.g., pooled money for food).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When is it better for a traveler to break fast according to this passage?
A) When the traveler feels any weakness
B) When the majority of companions are fasting
C) When companions are breaking fast or sharing expenses
D) When the journey is less than three days

Key Takeaway
A traveler in the Hanafi school may break fast, but the preference to fast or break depends on absence of personal harm and the social context of companions.

Wasiyyah for Missed Fasts and the Ruling on Continuity in Qada
Ruling on making a will for missed fasts and continuity in making them up

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions that make wasiyyah obligatory for missed fasts and explain why continuity is not required but recommended in qada.

Core Idea
Making up missed fasts is not required to be consecutive; continuity is only recommended.

This passage from the Hanafi fiqh text discusses the obligation of making a will (wasiyyah) for missed Ramadan fasts when a person dies after recovery. It also clarifies a common confusion: while many assume qada must be consecutive like Ramadan, the Hanafi school holds that continuity (tataabu') is not required—only recommended. The author backs this with Quranic proof and then provides a crucial categorization of which fasts require consecutive observance and which do not.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on wasiyyah
Determine when wasiyyah is obligatory (after ability to make up fasts) and when it is not required (if death occurs before excuse ends).
2
Determine the ruling on continuity in qada
From the explicit text, note that continuity is not a condition (shart) but is recommended (mustahab).
The proof is from Quran 2:184.
3
Examine the Quranic proof
Analyze the verse 'fiddatun min ayyamin ukhara' – it is general and does not stipulate order, so continuity is not required.
This is a Hanafi usul principle: 'al-amm yufidu al-zahir'.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
Study the disagreement on the vow case: when a person vows to fast a month but recovers for only one day, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad require wasiyyah for all, while a different view (Muhammad) says only for healthy days.
The mufta bihi is the majority view.
5
Categorize consecutive vs. non-consecutive fasts
Use the note at the end to list which fasts require tataabu' (four by text, three by reports) and which are free (qada, voluntary, etc.).
This classification helps avoid confusion in application.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(ولا يجب الإيصاء) بكفارة ما أفطره (على مَنْ ماتَ قبلَ زَوالِ عُذرِهِ بمرض وسفر ونحوه، كما تقدم) من الأعذار المبيحة للفطر، وقَضَوْا مَا قَدَرُوا على قَضَائِهِ بِقَدْرِ الإقامة، والصحة، ولا يُشترط التتابعُ في القضاءِ لفوات شرطه بفقد عدة من أيام أخر، (وَ) إن أدركوا العدو (قَضَوْا ما قَدَرُوا على قَضَائِهِ)، وإن لم يقضوا للزمهم الإيصاء (بقَدْرِ (الإقامة من السفر (والصحة)، ثم برئ من المرض وزال الـعــذر اتفاقاً على الصحيح، والخلاف فيمن نذر أن يصوم شهراً إذا برئ يوماً، يلزمه الإيصاء بالإطعام لجميــع الشهر عندهما، وعند محمد قضى ما صح فيه، كما في «الفتح» ( ولا يُشترط التتابع في القضاء) لقوله تعالى: ﴿فَعِدَّةٌ مِّنْ أَيَّامٍ أُخَرَ﴾ [البقرة: ١٨٤] من غير شرط الترتيب لكن المستحب أن يقضيه متتابعاً؛ مسارعة إلى إسقاط الواجب، ولهذا يستحب له أن لا يؤخره بعد القدرة كذا في « التبيين». تنبيه: أربعة متتابعة بالنص (۱): شهر رمضان أداءً، وكفارة الظهار، والقتل واليمين، والتي يتخير فيها قضاء رمضان وصوم فدية الحلق للمحرم والمتعة والقران وجزاء الصيد، وثلاثة لم تذكر في القرآن)، وثبتت في الأخبار : صوم كفارة الإفطار عمداً في رمضان، وهو متتابع والتطوع متخير فيه، والنذر هو على أقسام إما أن ينذر أياماً متتابعة معينة، أو غير معينة بخصوصها، ومنـه مـا لــزم بنـذر الاعتكاف وهو متتابع وإن لم ينص عليه، إلا أن يصرح بعدم التتابع في النذر.
(And it is not obligatory to make a will) for the expiation of what he broke (for one who dies before his excuse (of illness, travel, etc.) ends, as previously mentioned) of the excuses that permit breaking fast. And they make up what they are able to make up, according to the measure of residence and health, and continuity is not required in making up (qada) because its condition is lost due to the lack of a number of other days. (And) if they encounter the enemy (they make up what they are able to make up), and if they do not make up, the obligation of making a will (wasiyyah) is required of them (according to the measure of residence from travel and health). Then he recovers from illness and the excuse is removed, unanimously, on the correct view. And the disagreement is regarding one who vows to fast a month; if he recovers for a day, the obligation of making a will to feed (the poor) for the entire month is upon him according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad?), while according to Muhammad he makes up what he was healthy in, as in al-Fath. (And continuity is not required in qada) due to His statement: 'then a number of other days' without a condition of order, but it is recommended to make it up consecutively, hastening to discharge the obligation. For this reason, it is recommended not to delay it after capability, thus in al-Tabyin. A note: Four types are consecutive by text: performing Ramadan, expiation of zihar, killing, and oath; and those in which one is free to choose include making up Ramadan, fasting of fidyah for shaving the head for the muhrim, tamattu', qiran, and penalty for hunting; and three not mentioned in the Quran are established in reports: the fast of expiation for intentionally breaking Ramadan – it is consecutive – and voluntary fast is free to choose; and the vow has divisions: either one vows specific consecutive days, or unspecified days, and among them what is binding by vow of i'tikaf, and it is consecutive even if not stipulated, unless he explicitly states non-consecutive in the vow.
Source pages p. 667

Commentary
This passage covers several related rulings. First, it states that a person who dies while still having an excuse (illness, travel) that prevented fasting is not obligated to make a will (wasiyyah) for kaffarah of missed fasts. However, if a person recovers and is able to make up fasts but dies before doing so, then a will is required. The next ruling: those who were unable to fast (e.g., due to travel or illness) should make up as many days as they can according to the portion of the year they were in health/residence. They are not required to be consecutive (tataabu') in making up fasts because the condition of continuity is lost due to the interruption of days. The proof is Quran 2:184 which simply says 'several other days' without ordering. Then a special case: if someone vows to fast a month and then recovers for just one day, there is a difference: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (al-Shaybani?) hold that he must make a will for feeding for all month, while al-Shaybani (or another) says he makes up what he was healthy for. The text concludes that continuity is not required in qada, but it is recommended (mustahab) to make them up consecutively to hasten discharging the obligation. The passage ends with an important note: four types of fast are required to be consecutive by explicit text (Ramadan performance, expiation for zihar, killing, and oath), while four others are free (mukhayyar) regarding continuity: qada of Ramadan, fidyah for shaving head, tamattu', qiran, and penalty for hunting. Additionally, three types from reports: kaffarah for intentionally breaking Ramadan is consecutive, voluntary fast is free, and vows are divided. This distinction is crucial for understanding when and where continuity is mandatory.

Key Terms
الإيصاء (al-isa'): Obligation to make a will (wasiyyah) for kaffarah of missed fasts if one dies after having ability but before completing qada.
التتابع (al-tataabu'): Doing something consecutively without interruption.
قضاء (qada): Making up missed obligatory fasts after the month of Ramadan.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this lesson, which of the following is explicitly stated to require consecutive observance?
A) Making up missed Ramadan fasts (qada)
B) Expiation for zihar
C) Voluntary fasting
D) Fidyah for shaving head

Key Takeaway
Qada of missed Ramadan fasts does not require consecutive observance; continuity is only mustahab, but certain other fasts (like expiations) are wajib to be consecutive.

Classification of Fasts: Quranic and Sunnah Types
Catalogues fourteen types of fasts: eight from Quran, six from Sunnah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and categorize the fourteen types of fasts according to the Hanafi school, distinguishing between consecutive and optional Quranic fasts and the six Sunnah fasts.

Core Idea
Fourteen types of fasts: four consecutive Quranic, four optional Quranic, and six from Sunnah.

Understanding the full taxonomy of fasts is essential for proper fiqh application. This passage from the Hanafi tradition catalogues all types, clarifying when continuity is required and when it is optional. Mastery of this classification ensures you can correctly assign the ruling to any scenario.

Guided Path
1
Identify the overall classification scheme
Note that there are 14 types of fasts total: 8 from the Quran and 6 from the Sunnah. The Quranic ones are further divided into 4 that require continuity (mutatabi'ah) and 4 where continuity is optional (bi al-khiyar).
This dual classification is unique to the Hanafi school.
2
List the four consecutive Quranic fasts
These are: 1) fasting Ramadan (Q 2:185), 2) expiation for zihar (Q 58:4), 3) expiation for accidental killing (Q 4:92), and 4) expiation for breaking an oath (Q 5:89). The requirement of continuity is supported by Ibn Mas'ud's recitation.
The verse for oath expiation originally does not mention continuity, but the famous recitation adds it.
3
List the four optional Quranic fasts
The faster may choose to do these consecutively or separately: 1) making up missed Ramadan days (qada, Q 2:184), 2) fast of tamattu' (Q 2:196), 3) fidyah for shaving the head during ihram (Q 2:196), and 4) penalty for hunting in ihram or the sanctuary (Q 5:95).
The choice is left to the individual, making these more flexible.
4
List the six Sunnah fasts
According to al-Samarqandi, these are: 1) expiation for intentionally breaking Ramadan, 2) vowed fast (nazr), 3) voluntary fast (tatawwu'), 4) fast made obligatory by an oath (e.g., 'By Allah, I will fast a month'), 5) fast of i'tikaf, and 6) making up a voluntary fast after breaking it.
These are established by Sunnah, not directly from the Quran.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۱) اعلم أن جنس الصيامات كلها أربعة عشر نوعاً ثمانية منها في القرآن أربعة متتابعة وأربعة بالخيار وستة ثبتت بالسنة، أما الأربعة المتتابعة فهي أداء رمضان، لقوله تعالى: فَمَنْ شَهِدَ مِنْكُمُ الشَّهْرَ فَلْيَصُمْهُ [البقرة: ١٨٥] وكفارة الظهار، في قوله تعالى: فَصِيَامُ شَهْرَيْنِ مُتَتَابِعَيْنِ مِنْ قَبْلِ أَنْ يَتَمَاسًا [ المجادلة: 4 ]، والقتل أي: الخطأ في قوله تعالى: فَصِيَامُ شَهْرَيْنِ مُتَتَابِعَيْنِ تَوْبَةً مِنَ اللهِ [ النساء: ٩٢ ]، واليمين في قوله تعالى: فَصِيَامُ ثَلَاثَةِ أَيَّامٍ ذَلِكَ كَفَّارَةُ أَيْمَانِكُمْ [المائدة: ٨٩ ]، وإنما اشترط فيها التتابع، لأن ابـن مسـعـود قــرأ: فصيام ثلاثة أيام متتابعة ) وهي قراءة مشهورة يجوز بها الزيادة على الكتاب. وأما الأربعة التي بالخيار فهي: قضاء رمضان لقوله تعالى: فَمَنْ كَانَ مِنْكُمْ مَرِيضاً أوْ عَلَى سَفَرٍ فَعِدَّةٌ مِنْ أيام أخر [البقرة: ١٨٤]، وصوم المتعة لقوله تعالى: فَمَنْ تَمَتَّعَ بِالْعُمْرَةِ إِلَى الْحَجِّ فَمَا اسْتَيْسَرَ مِنَ الْهَدْيِ فَمَنْ لَمْ يَجِدْ فَصِيَامُ ثَلَاثَةِ أَيَّامٍ فِي الْحَجِّ وَسَبْعَةٍ إِذَا رَجَعْتُمْ [البقرة: ١٩٦] وفدية الحلق للمحرم لقوله تعالى: فَمَنْ كَانَ مِنْكُمْ مَرِيضاً أَوْ بِهِ أذى مِنْ رَأْسِهِ فَفِدْيَةٌ مِنْ صِيَامٍ أَوْ صَدَقَةٍ أَوْ نُسُكٍ [ البقرة: ١٩٦ ]، وجزاء الصيد المقتول حــال الإحرام أو في الحرم لقوله تعالى: كَفَّارَةٌ طَعَامُ مَسَاكِينَ أَوْ عَدْلُ ذَلِكَ صِيَاماً لِيَذُوقَ وَبَالَ أمْرِهِ [ المـائـدة: ٩٥ ]. بدائــع الصنائع (۲۱۰/۲)، وتحفة الفقهاء (٣٤١/٢). (۲) بل ستة كما ذكرها السمرقندي في تحفة الفقهاء (٣٤١/٢) : وهي: صوم كفارة الفطر في شهر رمضان عمداً، وصوم النذر، وصوم التطوع، والصوم الواجب باليمين بقول الرجل : « والله لأصومن شهراً»، وصوم الاعتكاف، وصوم قضاء التطوع بالإفطار.
(1) Know that the genus of all fasts is fourteen types: eight of them are in the Quran: four are consecutive and four are optional, and six are established by the Sunnah. As for the four consecutive: performing Ramadan, due to His statement: 'So whoever among you witnesses the month, let him fast it.' And expiation of zihar, in His statement: 'So fasting two consecutive months before they touch.' And killing, i.e., accidental, in His statement: 'So fasting two consecutive months as repentance from Allah.' And the oath, in His statement: 'So fasting three days; that is the expiation of your oaths.' And continuity is stipulated in them because Ibn Mas'ud recited: 'fasting three consecutive days' and it is a famous recitation by which an addition to the Book is permissible. As for the four which are optional: the making up of Ramadan, due to His statement: 'So whoever among you is ill or on a journey, then a number of other days.' And the fast of tamattu', due to His statement: 'So whoever enjoys the Umrah to the Hajj, then whatever is easy of the sacrificial animal. And whoever does not find, then fasting three days during Hajj and seven when you return.' And the fidyah of shaving for the muhrim, due to His statement: 'So whoever among you is ill or has an ailment of the head, then a ransom of fasting or charity or ritual offering.' And the penalty for hunting killed while in ihram or in the sanctuary, due to His statement: 'A compensation: feeding needy people or the equivalent of that in fasting, that he may taste the consequence of his matter.' (Bada'i' al-Sana'i' 2/210, Tuhfat al-Fuqaha 2/341). (2) Rather, six, as al-Samarqandi mentioned in Tuhfat al-Fuqaha (2/341): the fast of expiation for intentionally breaking Ramadan, the fast of vow, voluntary fast, the fast obligatory by oath such as a man saying 'By Allah, I will fast a month', the fast of i'tikaf, and the fast of making up voluntary fast after breaking it.
Source pages p. 667

Commentary
This footnote provides a comprehensive taxonomy of all types of fasts according to the Hanafi school, citing Bada'i' al-Sana'i' and Tuhfat al-Fuqaha. The author states that there are fourteen types: eight derived from the Quran and six from the Sunnah. The four consecutive (mutatabi') Quranic fasts are: 1) performance of Ramadan (Q 2:185), 2) expiation for zihar (Q 58:4), 3) expiation for accidental killing (Q 4:92), and 4) expiation for oath-breaking (Q 5:89). The reason continuity is required in these is that Ibn Mas'ud recited the verse regarding oath with 'mutatabi'ah' (consecutive), and such recitation is considered a valid addition even if not in the standard text. The four optional (bi al-khiyar) Quranic fasts are: 1) qada of Ramadan (Q 2:184), 2) fast of tamattu' (Q 2:196), 3) fidyah for shaving the head during ihram (Q 2:196), and 4) penalty for hunting (Q 5:95). Then the author quotes al-Samarqandi to correct that there are actually six from Sunnah, not three as previously hinted. These six are: 1) expiation for intentionally breaking Ramadan, 2) vowed fast (nazr), 3) voluntary fast (tatawwu'), 4) fast made obligatory by oath (example: 'By Allah, I will fast a month'), 5) fast of i'tikaf, and 6) making up a voluntary fast after breaking it. This classification is important for understanding when continuity is mandatory and when it is optional.

Key Terms
متتابعة (mutatabi'ah): Consecutive fasts without interruption; required in four Quranic types.
بالخيار (bi al-khiyar): Optional regarding continuity; the faster may choose to do them consecutively or separately.
صوم النذر (sawm al-nadhr): Fast vowed as an act of worship; obligatory once vowed.
صوم التطوع (sawm al-tatawwu'): Voluntary fast that is recommended but not obligatory.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Four
C) Six
D) Two

Key Takeaway
Lأنواع الصيام أربعة عشر: ثمانية من القرآن (أربعة متتابعة وأربعة بالخيار) وستة من السنة.

Classification of Fasts by Continuity (Tatabu')
Listing fasts that must be consecutive vs optional by Quran and Sunnah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish which fasts must be consecutive and which are optional, and justify the Hanafi position on the continuity of oath expiation using the qira'ah of Ibn Mas'ud.

Core Idea
Four fasts are mandated consecutive by Quranic text; for others, continuity is optional unless specified.

Fasting in Islam varies in its requirement of continuity. This passage from Imdad al-Fattah systematically classifies all types of fasts into those that must be consecutive (mutatabi') and those where the observer has a choice. Understanding this classification is essential for correct practice, as breaking a mandatory consecutive fast invalidates it and requires restart. The Hanafi school relies on a famous qira'ah (recitation) of Ibn Mas'ud to prove continuity in oath expiation, which distinguishes it from other schools.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four Quranic consecutive fasts
From the footnote, list the four fasts that are explicitly stated in the Quran with the word 'consecutive' (or interpreted as such by Ibn Mas'ud's qira'ah).
These are: Ramadan, expiation of zihar, expiation of manslaughter, and expiation of oath.
2
Identify the four Quranic optional fasts
List the four fasts mentioned in the Quran where the text does not require continuity, giving the observer a choice.
These are: making up missed Ramadan, tamattu' fast, fidyah for shaving (ihram), and penalty for hunting (ihram).
3
Understand the six Sunnah fasts and their continuity rules
Identify the six additional fasts established by hadith (as per al-Samarqandi) and note which require continuity (e.g., expiation for deliberate Ramadan break) and which are optional (e.g., voluntary fasts).
Among these, the expiation for deliberate Ramadan break is consecutive; others depend on the nature of the vow or oath.
4
Analyze the Hanafi proof for continuity in oath expiation
Examine how the Hanafi school uses Ibn Mas'ud's recitation of 'three consecutive days' (Quran 5:89) as a proof for mandatory continuity. Consider the principles of accepting a famous qira'ah as tafsir.
This is a point of ikhtilaf with other schools; the Hanafi response is that the qira'ah adds to the written text and is widely transmitted.
5
Apply the classification to a scenario
Given a real-life situation (e.g., a person must fast for an oath), determine whether consecutive fasting is required and what the consequence of breaking the sequence would be.
Consequence: if mandatory consecutive fast is broken, the fast is invalid and must be restarted.

تنبيه: أربعة متتابعة بالنص (۱): شهر رمضان أداءً، وكفارة الظهار، والقتل واليمين، والتي يتخير فيها قضاء رمضان وصوم فدية الحلق للمحرم والمتعة والقران وجزاء الصيد، وثلاثة لم تذكر في القرآن)، وثبتت في الأخبار : صوم كفارة الإفطار عمداً في رمضان، وهو متتابع والتطوع متخير فيه، والنذر هو على أقسام إما أن ينذر أياماً متتابعة معينة، أو غير معينة بخصوصها، ومنـه مـا لــزم بنـذر الاعتكاف وهو متتابع وإن لم ينص عليه، إلا أن يصرح بعدم التتابع في النذر. (۲) بل ستة كما ذكرها السمرقندي في تحفة الفقهاء (٣٤١/٢) : وهي: صوم كفارة الفطر في شهر رمضان عمداً، وصوم النذر، وصوم التطوع، والصوم الواجب باليمين بقول الرجل : « والله لأصومن شهراً»، وصوم الاعتكاف، وصوم قضاء التطوع بالإفطار.
Note: Four (fasts) are consecutive by text: performance of Ramadan, expiation of zihar, killing, and oath; and those in which there is choice: making up Ramadan, fasting of fidyah for shaving (the head) for the muhrim, tamattu', qiran, and penalty for hunting; and three not mentioned in the Quran but established in reports: fasting of expiation for deliberately breaking a fast in Ramadan (consecutive), voluntary fasting (optional), and vow (nathr) has divisions: either he vows specific consecutive days, or non-specific, and among it is what becomes obligatory by vow of i'tikaf which is consecutive even if not stated, unless he explicitly states non-consecutiveness in the vow. (1) Know that the genus of all fasts is fourteen types: eight in the Quran—four consecutive and four optional—and six established by Sunnah. As for the four consecutive: performance of Ramadan—His saying: 'So whoever among you witnesses the month, let him fast it' [2:185]; expiation of zihar—'fasting two consecutive months' [58:4]; killing (i.e., manslaughter)—'fasting two consecutive months' [4:92]; and oath—'fasting three days' [5:89]; and continuity was stipulated because Ibn Mas'ud recited 'fasting three consecutive days'—a well-known recitation that allows addition to the written text. As for the four optional: making up Ramadan—'a number of other days' [2:184]; tamattu' fast—'fasting three days during Hajj and seven when you return' [2:196]; fidyah for shaving for the muhrim—'a fidyah of fasting or charity or sacrifice' [2:196]; and penalty for hunting killed while in ihram or in the Haram—'a compensation: feeding the poor or the equivalent thereof in fasting' [5:95]. (2) Rather six as mentioned by al-Samarqandi in Tuhfat al-Fuqaha': fasting of expiation for deliberately breaking a fast in Ramadan, fasting of vow, voluntary fasting, fasting obligatory by oath (saying 'By Allah I will fast a month'), fasting of i'tikaf, and fasting of making up a broken voluntary fast.
Source pages p. 667

Commentary
This passage is a detailed classification (definition-ruling) of the types of fasts according to whether they must be performed consecutively or not. The text begins with a note (tanbih) listing four fasts that are mandatory to be consecutive by explicit Quranic text: the performance of Ramadan, expiation for zihar (a form of divorce), expiation for accidental killing, and expiation for a broken oath. Then it lists four fasts where one has a choice regarding continuity: making up missed Ramadan fasts, the fasting of fidyah for shaving the head while in ihram, the tamattu' fast, and the penalty for hunting while in ihram. Then it mentions three additional fasts not in the Quran but established in hadith: the expiatory fast for deliberately breaking a Ramadan fast (consecutive), voluntary fasts (non-consecutive optional), and vowed fasts (nathr) which depend on the wording. The footnote (1) expands that there are fourteen types total: eight in the Quran (four consecutive, four optional) and six from Sunnah (the above three plus others: nathr, tatawwu', oath, i'tikaf, and making up broken tatawwu'). The purpose is to clarify which fasts require continuity to be valid and which do not, as this affects the ruling of breaking a fast in between.

Key Terms
متتابع (mutatābiʿ): Consecutive, without interruption, required for certain fasts like expiations.
كفارة (kaffārah): Expiation, a fasting obligation to atone for a sin or violation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Expiation for zihar
B) Ramadan performance
C) Making up missed Ramadan fasts
D) Expiation for oath

Key Takeaway
Only four types of fasts are mandated consecutive by Quran: Ramadan, zihar, manslaughter, and oath expiation; all others are optional in continuity unless specified.

Ruling on Making Up Missed Fasts: Continuity Not Required
Ruling on qada with proof from Quran

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on continuity in qada and apply the Quranic proof to derive that continuity is not obligatory.

Core Idea
Qada of Ramadan is wajib but continuity is not required; the obligation is to fast a number of days without condition of sequence.

In this lesson, we examine the ruling on making up missed Ramadan fasts (qada). The passage from Imdad al-Fattah clarifies that while qada is obligatory, performing the make-up fasts consecutively is not a requirement. The proof is the Quranic verse 'a number of other days' without specifying sequence. We will also explore the recommended practice and exceptions, such as when a new Ramadan arrives before completing qada.

Guided Path
1
Identify the obligation of qada
Determine that qada is wajib based on the verse. The text says 'They make up what they are able to make up' indicating obligation.
2
Analyze the condition of ability
The ability to make up fasts depends on duration of stay (for travelers) and health. The text mentions 'according to the duration of stay and health'.
3
Extract the ruling on continuity (tatabu)
The text explicitly states 'continuous is not required in making up' and cites the verse without condition of sequence. Understand that the absence of a condition means continuity is not obligatory.
Note the cross-madhhab difference: Shafi'i requires continuity.
4
Consider the recommended practice
Although not obligatory, it is mustahab to make up fasts consecutively to hasten discharge of the obligation.
5
Apply the precedence rule
If a new Ramadan arrives before completing qada, the current Ramadan (ada) takes precedence over qada, and no fidya is required for the delay.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقَضَوْا مَا قَدَرُوا على قَضَائِهِ بِقَدْرِ الإقامة، والصحة، ولا يُشترط التتابعُ في القضاءِ لفوات شرطه بفقد عدة من أيام أخر، (وَ) إن أدركوا العدو (قَضَوْا ما قَدَرُوا على قَضَائِهِ)، وإن لم يقضوا للزمهم الإيصاء (بقَدْرِ (الإقامة من السفر (والصحة)، ثم برئ من المرض وزال الـعــذر اتفاقاً على الصحيح، والخلاف فيمن نذر أن يصوم شهراً إذا برئ يوماً، يلزمه الإيصاء بالإطعام لجميــع الشهر عندهما، وعند محمد قضى ما صح فيه، كما في «الفتح» ( ولا يُشترط التتابع في القضاء) لقوله تعالى: ﴿فَعِدَّةٌ مِّنْ أَيَّامٍ أُخَرَ [البقرة: ١٨٤] من غير شرط الترتيب لكن المستحب أن يقضيه متتابعاً؛ مسارعة إلى إسقاط الواجب، ولهذا يستحب له أن لا يؤخره بعد القدرة كذا في « التبيين». فإن جَاءَ رمضانُ آخَرُ قُدِّمَ على القَضَاءِ، ولا فِدْيَةَ بالتأخير إليه.
They make up what they are able to make up according to the duration of stay and health, and continuity is not required in making up (fasts) because its condition is lost due to the lack of a number of other days. And if they encounter the enemy, they make up what they are able to make up. And if they did not make up, they are required to make a will (for fidya) according to the duration of stay from travel and health. Then he recovers from illness and the excuse ceases unanimously upon the correct view. And the dispute is regarding one who vowed to fast a month: if he recovers one day, he is required to make a will for feeding for the entire month according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), and according to Muhammad (al-Shaybani) he makes up what he was healthy for, as in al-Fath. And continuity is not required in making up (fasts) due to His statement, the Exalted: ﴾A number of other days﴿ [al-Baqarah 2:184] without the condition of sequence, but it is recommended to make them up consecutively, hastening to discharge the obligation. Hence it is recommended not to delay after ability. Thus in al-Tabyin. Then if another Ramadan comes, it is given precedence over making up (the missed fasts), and there is no fidya for delaying until it.
Source pages p. 667

Commentary
This passage from Imdad al-Fattah addresses the ruling on making up missed Ramadan fasts (qada). It is a ruling with proof from the Quran. The text states that a person must make up whatever fasts they are able to, based on the duration of their stay (if traveling) and their health. The key point is that continuity (tawatur) is not required in qada, because the condition for holding the fast consecutively is lost when one has missed multiple days. The proof is the Quranic verse 'a number of other days' without specifying sequence. However, it is recommended (mustahab) to make them up consecutively to hasten the obligation. The ruling also covers the case of one who made a vow (nathr) to fast a month: according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, if he recovers one day, he must make a will for fidya for the entire month; according to Muhammad al-Shaybani, he only makes up the days he was healthy. Then the text explains that if the next Ramadan comes before the qada is completed, the current Ramadan (performance/ada) takes precedence, and there is no fidya (compensation) for delaying the qada until the next Ramadan.

Key Terms
قضاء (qaḍāʾ): Making up missed obligatory fasts after the month of Ramadan.
تتابع (tatābuʿ): Performing a series of fasts consecutively without interruption.
فدية (fidyah): Compensatory feeding of a poor person for each missed fast.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Yes, it is obligatory.
B) No, continuity is not required but recommended.
C) Only if the person vows to do so.
D) It depends on the number of days missed.

Key Takeaway
Qada of Ramadan fasts is wajib, but continuity is not a condition; it is recommended to fast consecutively, and if a new Ramadan arrives, ada takes precedence without fidya.

Fidyah for the Decrepit Elderly: Conditions and Exceptions
Ruling on elderly persons breaking fast and paying fidya

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for a decrepit elderly person to pay fidyah and distinguish between original fasts and expiatory fasts in this context.

Core Idea
A permanently incapacitated elderly person may break Ramadan fast and must pay fidyah, but fidyah does not substitute for expiatory fasts.

This passage from Imdad al-Fattah explains the Hanafi ruling on those who are too old to fast. While they are excused from the physical obligation, they are not excused from compensation. However, the school makes a critical distinction between fasting as an original obligation and fasting as a substitute for monetary expiation. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly applying fidyah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary ruling and its subject
Who is allowed to break fast and what must they do? The passage states that a decrepit old man and woman may break fast and must pay fidyah per day.
The Arabic term is 'shaykh fānin' and 'ajūz fāniyah'.
2
Determine the conditions for fidyah obligation
Fidyah becomes wajib only if the incapacity is permanent and continues until death (for non-travelers). The amount is half a sa' of wheat per day.
The condition of continuity distinguishes it from temporary illnesses.
3
Analyze the scope of fidyah
Fidyah applies only to original fasts (e.g., Ramadan, perpetual vow). It does not apply to expiatory fasts (kaffarah) because those are substitutes for monetary compensation.
This is a key distinction: fidyah substitutes for the physical act, not for another substitute.
4
Compare with expiatory fasts
For kaffarah (oath, killing, etc.), the primary obligation is monetary; fasting is only allowed when unable to pay. Therefore, a decrepit person cannot replace kaffarah fasts with fidyah.
The text explicitly prohibits fidyah for kaffarah: 'لا تجوز له الفدية'.
5
Note the qualification regarding travelers
Al-Zayla'i holds that a decrepit traveler who dies before returning does not owe fidyah, based on the principle of leniency. The majority (mufta bihi) still obligates fidyah for all permanently incapacitated.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf with practical consequences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(و) لا تجوز له الفدية إلا عن صوم هو أصل بنفسه، لا بدل عن غيره، حتى لو وجبَتْ عليه كفارة يمين، أو قتل) أو ظهاراً أو إفطاراً، فلم يجد ما يُكَفِّرُ به منْ عِشق وإطعام وكسوة، (وهو شـيـخ فـان، أو لم يَصُمْ) حال قدرته على الصوم ( حتى صَارَ فَانِياً، لا تجوز له الفدية؛ لأنَّ الصوم هنا بَدَلٌ عَنْ غيره) وهو التكفير بالمال، وكذا لا يجوز المصير إلى الصوم إلا عند العجز عما يكفر بـه مـن المـال، فإن أوصى بالتكفير جاز من ثلثه، ويجوز في الفدية الإباحة في الطعام أكلتان مشبعتان، كما يجوز التمليك بخلاف صدقة الفطر لا بد فيها من التمليك كالزكاة، كما في «الفتح» وفي « الدرر والـغـرر » : اعلم أن ما شرع بلفظ الإطعام أو الطعام " يجوز فيه التمليك والإباحة، ومـا شـرع بلفظ الإيتاء )
And breaking the fast is permissible for a decrepit old man and a decrepit old woman. And fidyah is obligatory upon them, for each day half a sa' of wheat, like one who vowed to fast forever and became weak from it due to being occupied with livelihood: he breaks and pays fidyah. If he cannot afford fidyah due to his poverty, he seeks forgiveness from Allah and asks Him for acquittal. And if an expiation for an oath or killing became obligatory upon him, and he could not find means to expiate with manumission, and he is a decrepit old man, or he did not fast until he became decrepit, fidyah is not permissible for him because the fasting here is a substitute for something else. Then if another Ramadan comes and he did not make up the missed fasts, performance is given precedence over making up, as previously mentioned, and there is no fidyah for delaying until it. And breaking the fast is permissible for a decrepit old man and old woman. In al-Haqa'iq from al-Ziyadat al-Burhaniyya: the interpretation of a decrepit old man is that he is unable to fast at present, and his incapacity increases each day until death becomes certain due to old age. End quote. In al-Nihaya: he is called 'fani' because he is close to perishing or because his strength has perished. And fidyah is obligatory upon them (the decrepit old man and old woman), but fidyah is not permissible for others with excuses except for one who is unable to fulfill a vow of perpetual fasting, as will be mentioned. And it is for each day half a sa' of wheat or its value, on condition of continuous incapacity until death. If the decrepit person is traveling and dies before settling, it is appropriate that fidyah is not obligatory upon him like other companions because he differs from others in leniency, not severity. Al-Zayla'i said that. And like one who vowed to fast forever and became weak from it due to being occupied with livelihood: he breaks and pays fidyah because he is certain he cannot make them up. If he cannot afford fidyah due to his poverty, he seeks forgiveness from Allah and asks for acquittal. And fidyah is only permissible for a fast that is an original obligation, not for a substitute, so if an expiation for oath, killing, zihar, or breaking fast became obligatory upon him and he could not find means to expiate with manumission, feeding, or clothing, and he is a decrepit old man, or did not fast until he became decrepit, fidyah is not permissible for him because fasting here is a substitute for something else. And it is not permissible to resort to fasting except when unable to expiate with wealth. If he bequeaths expiation, it is permissible from one-third of his estate. And in fidyah, it is permissible to give the food as a meal (two satisfying meals) as well as ownership, unlike sadaqat al-fitr which requires ownership like zakat, as in al-Fath. And in al-Durar wa al-Ghurar: know that whatever is prescribed with the wording 'it'am' (feeding) or 'ta'am' (food), it is permissible to give as ownership or as a meal; and whatever is prescribed with the wording 'ita'' (giving) [requires ownership].
Source pages p. 668

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling for elderly people who are unable to fast. It is a ruling with proof from the general principle derived from the Quran and Sunnah. The text states that a shaykh fani (a very old man) and an ajuz faniya (a very old woman) are permitted to break their fast during Ramadan and must pay fidyah (compensation) of half a sa' of wheat per day. This is similar to someone who vowed to fast every day (nathr al-abad) but becomes unable due to livelihood needs. However, if the person cannot afford the fidyah due to poverty, they should seek forgiveness from Allah. The key condition is that the incapacity lasts until death if they are permanent residents; if they are travelers and die before settling, fidyah is not obligatory. Importantly, fidyah is only allowed for fasts that are original obligations (like Ramadan or perpetual vow). It is not allowed for expiatory fasts (kaffarah) such as for oath, killing, zihar, or intentionally breaking a fast, because in those cases, fasting is a substitute for a monetary expiation (manumission, feeding, clothing). The proper sequence for expiation is: first try manumission, then if unable, feeding/clothing, and only when unable to do those does fasting become an option. For the elderly who cannot do any, fidyah is not permitted because the fast itself is a substitute. The text also distinguishes between forms of payment: fidyah can be given as a meal (ibaha) or as ownership (tamlik), whereas zakat and sadaqat al-fitr must be ownership. The ruling emphasizes the distinction between original obligations and substitutes.

Key Terms
شيخ فان (shaykh fānin): A very old man whose strength has perished and who is unable to fast; expected to die soon.
فدية (fidyah): Compensation, usually half a sa' of wheat per missed fast, for those permanently unable to fast.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, for which of the following is fidyah NOT permissible?
A) Decrepit old man missing Ramadan fasts.
B) Person who vowed perpetual fasting and now cannot.
C) Person who must expiate for killing but cannot afford anything.
D) Person who is chronically ill and cannot fast.

Key Takeaway
Fidyah is an obligatory compensation for permanently incapacitated individuals, but it only applies to original fasts, not to expiatory fasts that are substitutes for monetary atonement.

Ruling on Breaking a Voluntary Fast and the Excuse of Hospitality
Expands ruling with proof and intra-school difference

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on breaking a voluntary fast, distinguish between the positions of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, and apply the excuse of hospitality correctly.

Core Idea
A voluntary fast may be broken for a valid excuse before noon; without excuse it is makruh, not haram.

In this lesson, we examine the nuanced Hanafi ruling on breaking a voluntary fast. The passage presents a debate within the school: while the relied-upon position holds that breaking without excuse is makruh, a narration from Abu Yusuf permits it absolutely. We will trace the proofs and understand when hospitality qualifies as an excuse.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling category
The act is breaking a voluntary fast (futur al-mutatawwi'). Identify the default ruling according to the zahir al-riwayah and note the intra-Hanafi difference.
The passage begins by stating the permissibility in one narration, then clarifies the relied-upon position.
2
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf (in Al-Muntaqa) holds it is permissible to break without an excuse; Abu Hanifa and the majority hold it is makruh. Identify which narration is considered more sound.
Al-Kamal ibn al-Humam considers Abu Yusuf's narration 'awjah' (more appropriate).
3
Examine the dalil from hadith
Two hadith are cited: the Prophet (ﷺ) broke his fast after starting it, and the hadith about invitation. Determine how each is used to support the respective positions.
The hadith of breaking is used by Abu Yusuf; the invitation hadith is used to show that breaking for invitation is virtuous but not obligatory.
4
Understand the condition of hospitality
Hospitality (al-diyafah) is a valid excuse only before noon (qabl al-zawal). After noon, it is not an excuse except if not breaking causes parental disobedience.
The rationale: fasting at the beginning of the day is less burdensome, so intention is not required initially.
5
Apply the ruling to an example
Given a scenario (e.g., a voluntary faster is invited to lunch before noon), state the ruling: he may break, must make up the day later. If after noon, he should not break unless it involves parental disobedience.
Also note the exception for the five forbidden days (Eid and Tashriq) where no qada is required.
