(ويجوز للمتطوّع) بالصوم ( الفِطْرُ بلا عُدْرٍ، في رواية) عــن أبـي يوسف، وهي رواية « المنتقى» قال ( الكمال : واعتقادي أن رواية ( المنتقى» أوجه، ثم بين الوجه رحمه الله تعالى؛ لما روى مسلم عن عائشة أنها قالت: دَخَلَ النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم ذَاتَ يَومِ فَقَالَ: «هَلْ عِنْدَكُمْ شيء؟ فقلنا: لا، فقال: إنِّي إذَنْ صَائِمٌ ثُمَّ أَتَى يَوْمَاً آخَر فَقُلْنَا: يَا رَسُولَ الله أَهْدِي إِلَيْنَا حَيس، فَقَالَ: أرنيه، فَلَقَدْ أصْبَحْتُ صَائِمَاً ، فَأَكَلَ ) . وزاد النسائي: « وَلَكِنْ أصُومُ يَوْمَا مَكَانَهُ » )، وصحح هذه الزيادة أبو محمد عبد الحق، وذكر الكرخي وأبو بكر : أنه ليس له أن يفطر إلا من عذر، انتهى. وهو ظاهر الرواية كما في «الفتح» لما روي أنه عليه الصلاة والسلام قال: « إِذا دُعِي أَحَدُكُمْ إِلَى طَعَامِ فَلْيُجِبْ فَإِنْ كَانَ مُفْطِرَاً فَلْيَأْكُلْ، وإِنْ كَانَ صَائِمَاً فَلْيُصَلِّ » (۳)، أي: فَلْيَدْعُ. قال القرطبي: ثبت عنــه عـلـيـه الصلاة والسلام. ولو كان الفطر جائزاً كان الأفضل الفطر، لإجابة الدعوة التي هي السنة انتهى. كذا في « التبيين». وصححه في ( المحيط ) : اعلم إذاً أن فساد الصوم والصلاة بعد الشروع فيهما مكروه، وليس بحرام لأن الدليل ليس قطعي الدلالة)، كذا في « البحر » وإذا عرض عــذر أُبيح للمتطوع الفطر اتفاقاً. (والضيافة عذر، على الأظهر ) ، كذا في ( البرهان ، وه النهاية للصيف والمُضيف)، كذا في « البحر » عن «شرح الوقاية»، فيما قبل الزوال لا بعده .انتهى. إلا أن يكون في عدم فطــره بعده عقوق لأحد الأبوين لا غيرهما، حتى لو حلف عليه رجل بالطلاق الثلاث ليفطرن لا يفطر بعده، ووجه الفرق أن الصوم في أول اليوم لم يتأكد عادة؛ لما عرف أنه لا يشق على البدن، ولهذا لا تشترط النية في أول اليوم، ولا كذلك بعد الزوال، كذا في «التجنيس » . وفيه: لو أن صائماً حلف [ عليه ] رجل بطلاق امرأته أن يفطر، فإن كان متطوعاً يفطر لحق أخيه، وإن كان عن قضاء رمضان وله البِشَارَةُ بهذه الفائدة الجليلة، وإذا أفطر على أي حال عليه القضاء، إلا إذا شَرَعَ مُتَطَوعاً في خمسةِ أيامٍ: يَوْمَي العيدَيْنِ، وأيام التشريق، فلا يَلْزَمُهُ قَضاؤُها بإفسادِها، في ظاهر الرواية، والله أعلم.
And it is permissible for the voluntary faster to break his fast without an excuse, in one narration, and hospitality is an excuse, on the most apparent [opinion], for the guest and the host. And performance requires possession. (It is permissible for the voluntary faster) to break his fast without an excuse, in a narration from Abu Yusuf, and it is the narration of Al-Muntaqa. Al-Kamal said: My belief is that the narration of Al-Muntaqa is more appropriate. Then he explained the reason, may Allah have mercy on him; because Muslim narrated from Aisha that she said: The Prophet entered one day and said: 'Do you have anything?' We said: No. He said: 'Then I am fasting.' Then he came another day and we said: O Messenger of Allah, a hais (a type of food) was gifted to us. He said: 'Show it to me; indeed I began [the day] fasting, so he ate.' And Al-Nasa'i added: 'But I will fast a day in its place.' Abu Muhammad Abd al-Haqq authenticated this addition. Al-Karkhi and Abu Bakr mentioned that he may not break his fast except for an excuse, end. And it is the apparent narration as in Al-Fath, because it is narrated that he, peace be upon him, said: 'When one of you is invited to food, let him respond; if he is not fasting, let him eat; if he is fasting, let him pray' i.e., let him supplicate. Al-Qurtubi said: It is established from him, peace be upon him. And if breaking the fast were permissible, it would be better to break it to respond to the invitation which is Sunnah, end. Thus in Al-Tabyin. And he declared it correct in Al-Muhit: Know then that spoiling the fast and the prayer after starting them is makruh, not haram, because the evidence is not definitively indicative. Thus in Al-Bahr. And if an excuse arises, it is unanimously permitted for the voluntary faster to break his fast. (And hospitality is an excuse, on the most apparent [opinion]), thus in Al-Burhan, and it is [the ruling] for the guest and the host, thus in Al-Bahr from Sharh al-Wiqaya, before noon, not after it, end. Except if not breaking after it would involve disobedience to one of the parents, not others, even if a man swore on him with triple divorce that he would break, he does not break after it. The reason for the difference is that fasting at the beginning of the day is usually not firmly established, because it is known that it is not burdensome on the body, and therefore intention is not required at the beginning of the day. Unlike after noon. Thus in Al-Tajnيس. And in it: If a fasting person is sworn upon [by a man] with the divorce of his wife to break his fast, if he is a voluntary faster he breaks for the sake of his brother; if it is for making up Ramadan... and he has the glad tiding of this great benefit. And if he breaks his fast in any case, he must make it up, except if he began as a voluntary faster on five days: the two Eid days and the days of Tashriq; then it is not necessary for him to make them up if he invalidates them, in the apparent narration. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 669

Commentary
This passage is a commentary expanding on the ruling regarding breaking a voluntary fast. The base text states: 'It is permissible for the voluntary faster to break his fast without an excuse, in one narration, and hospitality is an excuse, on the most apparent [opinion], for the guest and the host. And performance requires possession.' The commentator then explains that this permissibility is in a narration from Abu Yusuf, specifically the narration of Al-Muntaqa. Al-Kamal ibn al-Humam (the author of Fath al-Qadir) considers this narration more appropriate and supports it with hadith. Two hadiths are cited: first, the hadith of Aisha where the Prophet (peace be upon him) began the day fasting but later ate when offered food; second, the hadith 'When one of you is invited to food...' which indicates that if a fasting person is invited, he should pray (supplicate) but if breaking were permissible, eating would be better to honor the invitation. Al-Qurtubi is quoted confirming that if breaking were permissible, it would be better to accept the invitation. However, the commentator clarifies that despite the permissibility in one narration, the relied-upon position (zahir al-riwayah) is that breaking without excuse is makruh but not haram, because the evidence is not definitively indicative. Then the passage discusses hospitality as an excuse, noting that it is only valid before noon (before zawal), not after, unless not breaking would involve disobedience to parents. The distinction is explained: fasting at the beginning of the day is not firmly established and not burdensome, so intention is not required at that time; after noon it becomes more burdensome and thus breaking is not allowed for hospitality. Finally, the passage mentions the case of a man who swears by divorce to make a fasting person break his fast: if the faster is voluntary, he may break to save his brother from divorce; if it is a make-up fast, he does not break. The glad tiding of reward is mentioned from a hadith. The passage ends by stating that breaking a voluntary fast obligates qada' (makeup), except on five forbidden days (Eid and Tashriq) according to the apparent narration from Abu Hanifa.

Key Terms
متطوع (mutatawwi'): One who performs a voluntary (nafl) act of worship, here specifically voluntary fasting.
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwayah): The well-known and authoritative transmitted rulings from the six core books of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani, which are relied upon in the school.
الضيافة (al-diyafah): Hospitality; the obligation or commendable act of hosting a guest, which is considered a valid excuse for a voluntary faster to break his fast before noon.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Makruh (disliked but not haram)

Key Takeaway
Breaking a voluntary fast without excuse is makruh tanzihi and requires qada; hospitality is a valid excuse only before noon, with an exception for parental obedience after noon.

Qada' for Broken Voluntary Fast and Exception of Forbidden Days
States obligation of qada' for voluntary fast except on five forbidden days

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on qada' for a broken voluntary fast on the five forbidden days and explain the intra-madhhab disagreement between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
A voluntary fast broken on Eid or Tashriq days: no makeup required – Abu Hanifa's view is the relied-upon position.

This passage presents a key exception to the general rule that a broken voluntary fast must be made up. It addresses the five days when fasting is prohibited. While the Sahibayn argue that any commencement of worship is binding, Abu Hanifa distinguishes based on the inherent prohibition of the fast itself. Understanding this dispute sharpens your grasp on how the Hanafi school navigates conflicting principles.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule and the exception
Parse the Arabic: 'And if he breaks his fast in any case, he must make it up, except if he began as a voluntary faster on five days.' The general rule is qada' wajib; the exception is for five specific days.
Note the clause 'إلا إذا شرع متطوعاً' indicates the exception.
2
List the five forbidden days
The two Eids (Eid al-Fitr and Eid al-Adha) and the three days of Tashriq (11th, 12th, 13th of Dhul Hijjah).
Fasting on these days is haram.
3
State the two positions in the intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu Hanifa: no qada' required because the fast itself is prohibited. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad: qada' required because commencement of worship is binding like a vow. The text indicates the relied-upon view is Abu Hanifa's ('fi zahir al-riwayah').
Understand the different usul principles applied.
4
Apply the rule to a new scenario
Given a person who starts a voluntary fast on Eid al-Adha and breaks it, the mufta bihi ruling is: no qada' needed.
Check the day: if it's one of the five, no qada'; otherwise, qada' is wajib.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا أفطر على أي حال عليه القضاء، إلا إذا شَرَعَ مُتَطَوعاً في خمسةِ أيامٍ: يَوْمَي العيدَيْنِ، وأيام التشريق، فلا يَلْزَمُهُ قَضاؤُها بإفسادِها، في ظاهر الرواية، والله أعلم.
And if he breaks his fast in any case, he must make it up, except if he began as a voluntary faster on five days: the two Eid days and the days of Tashriq; then it is not necessary for him to make them up if he invalidates them, in the apparent narration. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 670

Commentary
This passage is a legal ruling with an intra-Hanafi dispute. It establishes that a person who begins a voluntary fast and then breaks it must make it up (qada'), regardless of the reason. This is obligatory to preserve the parts of worship already performed from being wasted. However, there is an exception for five days in which the fast is inherently prohibited: the two Eid days (Eid al-Fitr and Eid al-Adha) and the three days of Tashriq (11th, 12th, 13th of Dhul Hijjah). On these days, fasting is forbidden (haram). Since the act of fasting itself is prohibited, merely starting it is a violation, and thus the shari'ah commands to break it (as per the hadith). According to the 'apparent narration' (zahir al-riwayah) from Imam Abu Hanifa, no qada' is required if one invalidates a voluntary fast begun on these days. The reason: one is ordered to break the fast because starting it was prohibited, so it is as if the fast was never validly begun. However, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (al-Shaybani) hold that qada' is required, because the commencement (shuru') is binding like a vow (nadhr). The commentator explains: Abu Hanifa's view is that since the prohibition was violated by starting, there is no obligation to complete, unlike a vow where no prohibition is violated by the vow itself. The reliance is on the 'apparent narration' (i.e., Abu Hanifa's view) as indicated by the text.

Key Terms
القضاء (al-qada'): The obligation to perform a makeup fast for a fast that was invalidated or missed.
أيام التشريق (ayyam al-tashriq): The three days following Eid al-Adha (11th, 12th, 13th of Dhul Hijjah) when fasting is prohibited.
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwayah): The well-known and authoritative transmitted rulings from the six core books of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani, which are relied upon in the school.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Qada' is required
B) Qada' is not required
C) Qada' is recommended
D) It is forbidden to make it up

Key Takeaway
According to the relied-upon Hanafi view, a voluntary fast broken on the two Eids and days of Tashriq does not require qada'.

Sawm — Fasting

Qada for Breaking a Voluntary Fast on Prohibited Days
States the ruling on qada for breaking a voluntary fast and the exception for five days

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on qada for a broken voluntary fast on the five prohibited days and contrast it with the view of the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
A voluntary fast begun on a prohibited day creates no obligation of qada because the commencement itself is disobedience.

In this passage, we learn the general rule that breaking a voluntary fast requires qada (making it up). However, there is a key exception for five specific days when fasting itself is prohibited: the two Eids and the three days of Tashriq. On these days, if one begins a voluntary fast and then breaks it, no qada is required according to the relied-upon narration from Imam Abu Hanifa. This exception sparks an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad argue that qada is still obligatory, analogizing to a vow. Let's unpack the reasoning and understand why Abu Hanifa's view is preferred.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Rule
The passage first states that if a voluntary fast is broken in any case, qada is required. This is the default ruling, agreed upon by all Hanafi imams.
The general rule applies to all voluntary fasts started on permissible days.
2
Locate the Exception
The exception immediately follows: 'except if he began a voluntary fast on five days: the two Eid days and the days of Tashriq.' For these days, no qada is binding according to Zahir al-Riwayah.
The five days are: Eid al-Fitr, Eid al-Adha, and the 11th, 12th, and 13th of Dhul Hijjah.
3
Note the Intra-Hanafi Dispute
The passage then clarifies that this is the position of Abu Hanifa, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) hold that qada is required even on these days.
This is an example of ikhtilaf within the school; the relied-upon view is Abu Hanifa's.
4
Analyze the Proofs
Abu Hanifa's proof: fasting on these days is commanded to be broken; the very act of starting is an act of disobedience (turning away from Allah's hospitality), so no obligation of completion or qada arises. The Sahibayn analogize to a vow (nadhr) or prayer in prohibited times, where the commencement creates an obligation.
The hadith cited about breaking for a brother is an incentive, not a proof for the exception.
5
Determine the Mufta Bihi
The text explicitly states 'in Zahir al-Riwayah' and attributes the exception to Abu Hanifa. This indicates that the relied-upon position for fatwa is Abu Hanifa's view: no qada required.
The authority weight is Zahir al-Riwayah, which is the primary source of Hanafi law.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا أفطر على أي حال عليه القضاء، إلا إذا شَرَعَ مُتَطَوّعاً في خمسةِ أيامٍ: يَوْمَي العيدَيْنِ، وأيام التشريق، فلا يَلْزَمُهُ قَضاؤُها بإفسادِها، في ظاهر الرواية. رجل أصبح صائماً متطوعاً فدخل على أخ من إخوانه فسأله أن يفطر لا بأس أن يفطر لقول النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم: مَنْ أَفْطَرَ لَحِقَ أَخِيهِ يُكْتَبُ لَهُ ثَوابَ صَوْمِ أَلْفِ يَوْمٍ. وإذا أفطر المتطوع على أي حال كان عليه القضاء، لا خلاف بين أصحابنا في وجوبه، صيانة لما مضى عن البطلان. إلا إذا شَرَعَ مُتَطَوعاً بالصوم في خمسة أيام: يَوْمَي العيدين، وأيام التشريق، فلا يَلْزَمُهُ قَضاؤُها بإفسادِها، في ظاهر الرواية عن أبي حنيفة رحمه الله تعالى؛ لأن صومها مأمور بنقضه ولم يجب عليه إتمامه؛ لأنه بنفس الشروع ارتكب المنهي عنه؛ للإعراض عن ضيافة الله فأمر بقطعه. وعن أبي يوسف ومحمد عليه القضاء.
And if he breaks the fast in any case, qada is required upon him, except if he began a voluntary fast on five days: the two Eid days and the days of Tashriq, then qada for spoiling them is not binding upon him, according to Zahir al-Riwayah. A man who became a voluntary faster and entered upon a brother who asked him to break the fast – there is no harm in breaking it due to the Prophet's saying: 'Whoever breaks for his brother, the reward of a thousand days of fasting is written for him.' And if the voluntary faster breaks in any case, qada is required upon him, there is no disagreement among our companions about its obligation, to preserve what has passed from invalidity. Except if he began a voluntary fast on five days: the two Eid days and the days of Tashriq, then qada for spoiling them is not binding upon him, in Zahir al-Riwayah from Abu Hanifa, because fasting on these days is commanded to be broken and he is not required to complete it; for by the very commencement he commits the prohibited act due to turning away from Allah's hospitality, so he is commanded to cut it. And according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, qada is required upon him.
Source pages p. 670

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling on breaking a voluntary fast and the obligation of qada (making up the fast). It begins by stating the general rule: if a person breaks any voluntary fast, qada is required. However, an exception is made for five specific days: the two Eids (Eid al-Fitr and Eid al-Adha) and the three days of Tashriq (11th, 12th, 13th of Dhul Hijjah). In these days, if one starts a voluntary fast and then breaks it, no qada is necessary according to the relied-upon narration (Zahir al-Riwayah) from Imam Abu Hanifa. The reason given is that fasting on these days is itself prohibited; the person is commanded to break it because he has neglected the hospitality of Allah. Therefore, the commencement of the fast is an act of disobedience, and no obligation of completion or qada arises. The passage also cites a hadith encouraging one to break his fast for the sake of a brother, promising great reward. However, this hadith is not directly used as a proof for the exception but as an incentive for breaking a voluntary fast generally. The passage then notes an intra-Hanafi dispute: Imam Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad hold that qada is required even in these five days, analogizing the commencement to a vow (nadhr) or to starting prayer in prohibited times. Abu Hanifa distinguishes because the act of starting is itself a violation, unlike a vow where the commitment is pure obedience. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is Abu Hanifa's view as it is the Zahir al-Riwayah.

Key Terms
تطوع (tatattu‘): Voluntary supererogatory act of worship, here specifically a voluntary fast.
قضاء (qada’): Making up a missed or broken obligatory act; here, making up a voluntary fast that was broken.
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwayah): The transmitted and relied-upon narrations from Abu Hanifa and his students, forming the primary source of Hanafi law.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the relied-upon Hanafi ruling regarding qada for breaking a voluntary fast begun on the day of Eid al-Fitr?
A) Qada is required, as per all Imams.
B) No qada is required, as per Imam Abu Hanifa in Zahir al-Riwayah.
C) Qada is required only if the fast is broken before midday.
D) The fast is invalid, but qada is still obligatory.

Key Takeaway
On the five prohibited days, no qada is required for a broken voluntary fast because the fast itself is invalid from the start.

Conditional Vow: Performance Before Condition Invalid
Ruling on conditional vow fulfilment timing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a conditional vow and explain why performing the vowed act before the condition occurs does not fulfill the vow.

Core Idea
A vow suspended on a condition binds only after the condition occurs; early performance does not fulfill it.

This passage from Nur al-Idah clarifies a key distinction in vows: when a vow is made conditional on a future event, the obligation does not arise until that event happens. Many assume that performing the act early shows devotion, but the Hanafi school holds that it does not count toward the vow. Understanding this prevents incomplete obligations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its category
The act is a conditional vow (nathr mu'allaq bi-shart), a type of nadhr where the obligation is suspended on a future condition.
The vow becomes obligatory (wajib) only when the condition is fulfilled.
2
Determine the hukm level and its trigger
The hukm level is wajib, but it is contingent upon the existence of the condition. Before the condition, there is no obligation.
The Hanafi school distinguishes between the obligation to perform and the act itself; early performance is a voluntary act, not fulfillment of the vow.
3
State the condition for validity of fulfilment
The condition (shart) must have occurred before performing the vowed act. If the act is done before the condition, it does not count towards the vow.
The text says 'لا يجزئه عنه ما فعله قبل وجود شرطه' - it does not suffice him.
4
Explain the legal rationale
Because the vow is suspended on the condition; before the condition, the obligation does not exist. Performance is only valid after the cause (sabab) to which the vow was attached exists.
Rationale: المعلق بالشرط عدم قبل وجوده - what is suspended on a condition is nonexistent before its existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن علق النذر بشرط لا يجزئه عنه ما فعله قبل وجود شرطه؛ لأن المعلق بالشرط عدم قبل وجوده، وإنما يجوز الأداء بعد وجود السبب الذي علق بالنذر به.
And if he attaches the vow to a condition, what he does before the existence of its condition does not suffice him; because what is suspended on a condition is nonexistent before its existence, and performance is only permissible after the existence of the cause to which the vow was attached.
Source pages p. 672

Commentary
This passage states the ruling on a conditional vow (nathr mu'allaq bi-shart). In the Hanafi school, if a person makes a vow conditional upon a future event—for example, 'If Zaid returns, I must give such-and-such in charity'—then performing the vowed act before that condition occurs does not fulfill the vow. The reason is that the vow is suspended (mu'allaq) on the condition; before the condition exists, the obligation does not exist. Valid performance can only occur after the cause (sabab) to which the vow was attached has come into existence. This ruling is based on the nature of conditional obligations: the obligation arises only when the condition is realized. The text is authoritative (Zahir al-Riwayah) as it reflects the standard Hanafi position.

Key Terms
النذر (al-nadhr): A vow: making an act of worship obligatory upon oneself by utterance.
معلق بالشرط (mu'allaq bi-al-shart): Suspended on a condition: the obligation of the vow is contingent upon a future event.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A person vows: 'If my business partner returns from travel, I will fast three days.' He fasts the day after making the vow, before his partner returns. According to the Hanafi school, does this fulfill his vow?
A) Yes, because he performed the fast with intention for the vow.
B) No, because the condition (partner's return) has not yet occurred.
C) Yes, because he can intend the fast for the vow and it counts as good deed.
D) No, because the vow was invalid from the start.

Key Takeaway
A conditional vow is only fulfilled after its condition materializes; early performance does not discharge the obligation.

Types of I'tikaf and the Condition of Fasting for Vowed I'tikaf
Explains the three types of i'tikaf and the condition of fasting for obligatory i'tikaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the three types of i'tikaf (sunnah kifayah, mustahabb, and vowed) and state the Hanafi ruling on fasting as a condition for the vowed i'tikaf, citing the evidence.

Core Idea
Fasting is required only for vowed i'tikaf; supererogatory i'tikaf does not require fasting.

In this lesson, we examine the classification of i'tikaf into three types according to the Hanafi school. The author explains that i'tikaf in the last ten days of Ramadan is a communal sunnah, while any other time is simply recommended. He then addresses a key dispute: whether fasting is a condition for i'tikaf. The Hanafi position is that fasting is only required for a vowed (obligatory) i'tikaf, based on the hadith 'No i'tikaf except with fasting.'

Guided Path
1
Classify i'tikaf types
Identify the three types: sunnah kifayah (last ten days of Ramadan), mustahabb (any other time), and vowed (obligatory due to a vow). Note the ruling for each.
The sunnah kifayah is established by the Prophet's consistent practice.
2
Identify the disputed condition
Recognize that the central issue is whether fasting is a condition for i'tikaf validity. The text explicitly states it is a condition only for the vowed type.
3
Examine the evidence
The dalil is the hadith: 'No i'tikaf except with fasting.' Consider its transmission (qati al-thubut) and how it is interpreted.
The Hanafi interpretation restricts this condition to vowed i'tikaf.
4
Contrast with supererogatory i'tikaf
Understand that according to Zahir al-Riwayah, fasting is not a condition for supererogatory i'tikaf. The Prophet's own practice (he did not always fast during i'tikaf) supports this.
This distinction is unique to the Hanafi school.
5
Note the ikhtilaf on Laylat al-Qadr
Although secondary, the passage discusses the disagreement about the exact night. This highlights the virtue of i'tikaf in the last ten days.
Abu Hanifa holds it is not fixed; the two companions say it is fixed.

Sunnah kifayah, strongly recommended in the last ten days of Ramadan; due to what is in the Sahihain and others from the hadith of 'A'isha that the Prophet (peace be upon him) used to perform i'tikaf in the last ten days of Ramadan until Allah took him, then his wives performed i'tikaf after him. And when he performed i'tikaf in the middle ten days, Jibril came to him and said: 'That which you seek is ahead of you,' meaning Laylat al-Qadr, so he performed i'tikaf in the last ten days. As a result of this, most hold that Laylat al-Qadr is in the last ten days of Ramadan. Some said it is on the 21st, some on the 27th, and it is reported in Sahih: 'Seek it in the last ten days and seek it on every odd night.' From Abu Hanifa: it is in Ramadan, but it is not known which night it is; it may precede or delay. According to the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), it is also in Ramadan but it is fixed and does not precede or delay. According to the well-known from the Imam (Abu Hanifa), it rotates through the year, sometimes in Ramadan and sometimes outside it. There are other sayings: it is the first night of Ramadan, al-Hasan said the 17th, some said the 19th, from Zayd ibn Thabit: the 24th, from 'Ikrimah: the 25th. Abu Hanifa responded to the restricted evidences that it is in the last ten days by saying that the reference is to the Ramadan in which he (peace be upon him) sought it. Among its signs: it is bright, calm, neither hot nor cold; the sun rises on its morning without rays, as if it is a basin. It was hidden so that people strive in seeking it, thereby gaining the reward of the one who strives in worship, just as Allah hid the Hour so that they would be on guard against its sudden occurrence. And Allah knows best. Thus said al-Kamal (may Allah have mercy on him). The third type of i'tikaf: mustahabb in other than that, i.e., at any time one wishes, other than the last ten days of Ramadan, and if it is not vowed. Fasting is a condition for the validity of the vowed i'tikaf, and there is no vow except with the tongue, because it is among its related matters, unlike intention whose place is the heart. For the vowed is not valid without fasting, due to what we have presented of his saying: 'No i'tikaf except with fasting.' Unlike the supererogatory, for in the apparent narration (zahir al-riwayah) it is not a condition therein, due to his saying: 'The person in i'tikaf is not...'

Commentary
This passage continues the commentary on the types of i'tikaf. It details the second type: sunnah kifayah (communal sunnah) in the last ten days of Ramadan, established by the Prophet's consistent practice. The passage then engages in an extensive discussion about Laylat al-Qadr (the Night of Decree), presenting various opinions from the Companions and the Hanafi imams. Abu Hanifa holds that it is in Ramadan but not fixed; the two companions say it is fixed. Al-Kamal ibn al-Humam reconciles views. The third type is mustahabb (recommended) at any other time. The key ruling is that fasting is a condition (shart) for the validity of vowed (obligatory) i'tikaf, based on the hadith 'No i'tikaf except with fasting.' For supererogatory i'tikaf, fasting is not a condition according to the Zahir al-Riwayah. The passage also notes that a vow is made by the tongue, not by intention alone.

Key Terms
سنة كفاية (sunnah kifayah): A communal sunnah: if some perform it, the obligation is fulfilled for all; if none do, all are sinful.
المنذور (al-manthur): The vowed i'tikaf, which becomes obligatory due to a vow
ليلة القدر (laylat al-qadr): The Night of Decree, hidden in the last ten days of Ramadan, with signs mentioned in the text
صوم (sawm): Fasting, the act of abstaining from food, drink, and sexual relations from dawn to sunset

Key Terms from Source Passage

In the Hanafi school, fasting is a condition only for vowed (obligatory) i'tikaf, not for sunnah or mustahabb i'tikaf.

Vowing a Month of I'tikaf: The Effect of Intention
Ruling on intention for i'tikaf vow

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish when an intention to limit a vow to days only is valid and when it requires explicit exclusion.

Core Idea
A month is a fixed measure; you cannot split it by mere intention.

When someone vows a month of i'tikaf, can they internally intend only the daytime and be excused from the nights? The answer depends on the linguistic nature of the word 'month'. In this lesson, we will see why a month is treated as an indivisible unit in Hanafi fiqh, and how explicit exception (istithna) can modify the vow.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage distinguishes between vowing i'tikaf for less than a month and vowing for a full month. For the first, intending days only is valid; for the second, it is not without explicit exception.
2
Understand the key term 'شهر'
The text explains that 'month' (shahr) is an 'ism muqaddar' (a name for a specific measure) that inherently includes both days and nights. It is not a general term like 'ten' that can apply to parts.
3
Analyze the role of explicit exception (استثناء)
If the person explicitly says 'a month except the nights', this is valid because exception (istithna) is speech about the remainder. The vow then becomes thirty days. If he says 'except the days', the remainder is nights alone, which is invalid because i'tikaf requires fasting (only during the day).
4
Apply the linguistic reasoning
Understand the analogy: just as 'ten' cannot refer to five except by metaphor, 'month' cannot refer to only days or only nights without explicit exclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وصح ليه النهر خاصة، دونَ الليالي. وإن نذر اعتكاف شهر، ودوى الشهر خاصة، أو الليالي خاصة، لا تَعْمَلُ بَيْتُهُ إِلَّا أَنْ يُصَرْحَ بالاستثناء. (وصح نيَّةُ النُّهُر) جمع نهار (خاصة) بالاعتكاف، إذا نوى تخصيصه بالأيام دونَ الليالي)، إذا نذر اعتكاف دون الشهر لأنه نوى حقيقة كلامه فتعمل نیته كقوله نذرت اعتكاف عشرين يوماً، ونوى بياض النهار خاصة منها صحت نيته. (وإنْ نَذَرَ اعتکاف شهر معين أو غير معين (ونوى الشهر خاصة، أو الليالي خاصة، لا تَعْمَلُ نَيْتُهُ إلا أن يُصَرِّح بالاستثناء) اتفاقاً؛ لأن الشهر اسم لمقدر يشمل الأيام والليالي، وليس باسم عــام كالعشرة على مجموع الآحاد، فلا ينطلق على ما دون ذلك العدد أصلاً، كما لا تنطلق العشرة على الخمسة مثلاً حقيقة ولا مجازاً، أما لو قال شهراً بالنهر دون الليالي لزمه كما قال وهو ظاهر، أو استثنى فقال: شهراً إلا الليالي لأن الاستثناء تكلم بالباقي بعد الثنيا فكأنه قال: ثلاثين نهاراً، ولو استثنى الأيام لا يجب عليه شيء؛ لأن الباقي الليالي المجردة ولا يصح فيها لمنافاتها شرطه وهو الصوم، هـذا مـن فتح القدير بعناية المولى النصير
It is valid his intention for the days (nahar) only, without the nights. And if he vows i'tikaf of a month, and intends the month only or the nights only, his intention does not take effect unless he explicitly excludes. (It is valid his intention for the days specially) i.e. the plural of day, for i'tikaf, if he intends to specify it to the days without the nights, when he vows i'tikaf for less than a month because he intends the reality of his speech, so his intention is effective, like if he says 'I have vowed i'tikaf for twenty days' and intends only the daylight hours of them, his intention is valid. (And if he vows i'tikaf of a specific or unspecified month and intends the month only or the nights only, his intention does not take effect unless he explicitly excludes) by agreement, because 'month' is a name for a specific quantity that includes days and nights, and it is not a general term like 'ten' which applies to the composite of units, so it does not apply to anything less than that number at all, just as 'ten' does not apply to five, for example, literally or metaphorically. However, if he said 'a month with its days without its nights' it is binding on him as he said, and that is apparent; or if he made an exception and said 'a month except the nights', then the exception is speech about the remainder after exclusion, so it is as if he said 'thirty days'. And if he excepts the days, nothing is obligatory on him, because the remainder is bare nights and i'tikaf is not valid for them because they contradict its condition, which is fasting.
Source pages p. 679

Commentary
This passage distinguishes between vowing i'tikaf for a number of days less than a month and vowing for a full month. For vows of less than a month, if a person intends only the daytime (nahar) and expresses that, his intention is valid because 'day' can be understood as daylight in common usage. But when vowing a month, the term 'month' is 'ism al-muqaddar' (a name for a defined measure) that necessarily includes both days and nights. Therefore, if he intends only the days or only the nights of a month, his intention is not effective unless he explicitly excludes the other part (e.g., 'a month except the nights'). If he excludes nights, it becomes a vow of thirty days, which is valid. If he excludes days, the remainder is nights alone, and i'tikaf is not valid for nights alone because i'tikaf requires fasting (which is only during the day). This reasoning is taken from Fath al-Qadeer.

Key Terms
شهر (shahr): A month; a specific measure of time comprising days and nights.
النهر (al-nahār): Daytime; the period from sunrise to sunset.
استثناء (istithnā’): Exclusion; an exception phrase like 'except' that removes part of the vow.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Thirty days of i'tikaf
C) A month including nights
D) Only the nights

Key Takeaway
A month vow is indivisible by mere intention; only explicit exception can alter the obligation.

The Hadith on Fasting for the Deceased: A Primary Proof in Hanafi Fiqh
Hadith incipit serving as evidence for qada' of fasts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the hadith 'Whoever dies and has a month of fasting...' and explain its role as the primary textual evidence in Hanafi fiqh for the permissibility of fasting on behalf of a deceased person.

Core Idea
Fasting for the deceased is proven by the Prophet's hadith; it is an act of devotion transferable to the departed.

In Hanafi fiqh, the obligation of missed fasts does not vanish with death. The hadith narrated by Ibn Abbas (may Allah be pleased with them) provides the direct textual basis for performing qada' on behalf of the deceased. This lesson examines the hadith, its legal import, and how the Hanafi school employs it.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the hadith text
Learn the exact Arabic words: 'مَنْ مَاتَ وَعَلَيْهِ صَوْمُ شَهْرٍ'. This is the incipit of the full hadith.
The full hadith is longer; the incipit is the key phrase triggering the ruling.
2
Identify the legal issue
The hadith addresses a person who dies while an obligatory fast (Ramadan or vowed) is still owed. The ruling is about fulfilling that obligation posthumously.
The fast is a debt owed to Allah.
3
Understand the Hanafi application
The Prophet (PBUH) instructed a relative to fast on behalf of the deceased. This indicates that the deceased's obligation can be fulfilled by another, specifically a guardian or relative.
Hanafi scholars base their ruling on the imperative in the hadith.
4
Recognize the school's reliance
This hadith is the foundational proof in the Hanafi school for the permissibility and recommendation of fasting for the deceased. It is cited in major works like al-Hidayah.
Other schools may use additional proofs, but this hadith is primary for Hanafis.
5
Apply the ruling
Using this hadith, a Hanafi mufti would rule that a relative may fast on behalf of a deceased person who missed fasts due to a valid excuse, and it is recommended (sunnah) to do so.
The hadith covers both fard (Ramadan) and wajib (vowed) fasts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مَنْ مَاتَ وَعَلَيْهِ صَوْمُ شَهْرٍ
Whoever dies and upon him is a month of fasting...
Source pages p. 733

Commentary
This passage is a hadith incipit from the index of Imdad al-Fattah. It quotes the Prophetic hadith: 'Whoever dies and has a month of fasting owed...' In Hanafi fiqh, this hadith is the primary proof that an obligatory fast can be fulfilled on behalf of the deceased by a relative, based on the principle of qada' for missed acts of worship. The full ruling and details are elaborated in the commentary on the relevant chapter.

Key Terms
صوم (sawm): Fasting, specifically the obligatory fast of Ramadan or a vowed fast.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary evidence in Hanafi fiqh for fasting on behalf of a deceased person?
A) Consensus of the scholars
B) The hadith 'Whoever dies and has a month of fasting'
C) Analogy to prayer on behalf of the deceased
D) The principle of istihsan

Key Takeaway
The hadith 'مَنْ مَاتَ وَعَلَيْهِ صَوْمُ شَهْرٍ' is the foundational proof for the permissibility of fasting for the deceased in Hanafi fiqh.
